## Session 5

[Page 1]

**Resonance is the intensity of the relationship between the customer and the brand.**

### 4 Components of Resonance

#### 1. Behavioral Loyalty
- Repeat purchases
- Share of category volume (market share by volume)
- Byron Sharp – customers are not sticky

> Go to Facebook – ask opinion
> To fight this – keep increasing penetration
> Engage-surprise-delight on every encounter
> Even high market share brands can use occasions.

#### 2. Attitudinal Attachment (Addiction)
There must be clinging for the brand.

- [Professor's emphasis – blue text]: Example of one brand where customers experience clinging.
  - US – Malboro: 50% didn't switch despite harmful effects of smoking
  - London – queue strand office – copy of Holme's serial
  - [Professor's emphasis – red text]: El Ries and Jack Trout – segmenting is done to the market, positioning is to the mind.
  - Jagadeesh Set – power of 3 (volume)

#### 3. Sense of Community
- COMRADE – Council of old monk rum addicted drinkers and eccentrics.
- Just Herbs – 100% built through crowd sourcing.
  - Form a group – iterative way – form a picture in website – let customers manipulate it
  - This is what Dainik Bhaskar did when they launched
  - This is the way you launch something – Called **[Key concept – highlighted in cyan]: Co creation**
  - (Book by C K Prahlad and Ramaswamy)
- Xiaomi – amazing user community, services model. (45 minute video)
  - Users co create, they fight competitive refurbishing – users buy product, use cheap, refurbish
  - Phone sold so fast – Flipkart thought it was a glitch on their site – in 10 years, they became a fortune 500 company.

#### 4. Active Engagement
It is marked by deep engagement, attachment, and loyalty.
- When do you say customer is engaged with the brand?
  - Attachment is the state in which a customer buys a brand not because he needs it, because he loves it.
  - Measures of engagement – He spends time, energy, money for the brand.

### Other Visible Signs of Resonance

1. **Intensity** – Sachin fans – god

[Page 2]

2. **Activity** – Jennifer Anniston – Instagram – old friends photo – Instagram server broke down.
   - Salience – What do you know about the brand?
   - Performance and imagery – what the brand does for you
   - Judgement and feelings – what you think of the brand
   - Resonance – How you get attached to the brand – this is when you actually have a customer, and not necessarily repeat purchase.

---

### [Key concept – highlighted in yellow]: When there is brand identity, why define brand equity?
What impact it has is brand equity.

**Comparison Table: Brand Identity vs. Brand Equity**

| Brand Identity | Brand Equity |
|---|---|
| Feel | Measure |
| Qualitative | Quantitative |
| One paragraph | One metric |
| With company (sender) | With customer (receiver) |
| Cause | Consequence |

---

### Brand Avoidance
Reject a specific brand, even though it is available, accessible and financially affordable.

**Types:**
1. **Experiential** – Due to bad experience. E.g.: Snap Deal
2. **Identity** – Due to conflict of self-identity and brand's perceived identity. De-individuation – become popular for the masses
3. **Moral** – Conflict between the values of the consumer and those associated with the brand.
4. **Deficit-value** – When they feel that they have not got adequate value in return for the money they pay.
5. **Advertising** – Due to distasteful ad components like content, sound, endorser or frequency.

---

### Branding Services

**Search good** – search for information

**How are search goods different compared to the other 2?**
- Can evaluate the value before purchase
- Can be able to separate product from service
- [Professor's emphasis – blue text]: Search goods are more products, other two are more services
- Product to service ratio: Search goods 80:20 (car), Durable 100:0 (chocolate), FMCG – shampoo
- Experience good 50:50 and 30:70

[Page 3]

- Credence 20:80 and 0:100 (spirituality)

**[Diagram Description – Product-Service Continuum Slide]:**
A horizontal arrow diagram showing a spectrum from "PREDOMINANTLY PRODUCT" on the left to "PREDOMINANTLY SERVICE" on the right. Below this arrow is a second arrow pointing right. The continuum is divided into three zones:
- Left zone: **SEARCH GOODS** – with downward arrows pointing to examples: FMCG 100:0 (Soap, Shampoo), DURABLE 80:20 (Car, Friz)
- Middle zone: **EXPERIENCE GOODS** – with downward arrows pointing to: B2C SERVICE 50:50 (Restaurant, Bank), B2B SERVICE 30:70 (Subcontracting, Software)
- Right zone: **CREDENCE GOODS** – with downward arrows pointing to: KNOWLEDGE GOODS 20:80 (Consultancy, IIMB), SPIRITUALITY 0:100 (Vaishno Devi, Jaggi Vasudev)

**Product and service are a continuum, not different.**

## Session 6

[Page 3 continued]

### [Professor's emphasis – red/pink text]: The Framework

> Why should I use this framework of 3 types of goods, 7Ps and 5 elements of branding?
> What is the deliverable of this framework (there is also a summary framework in moodle)?
> Use the first column, you can brand a toilet soap (soft) or a refrigerator (durable)
> Use the second column, you can brand a bank (b2c service) or sub-contracting (b2b service)
> Use the third column, you can brand a tax consultant or credit rating agency
>
> The sequence in product-service continuum seems to be FMCG, durable, b2c service, b2b service, professional services, spirituality
> [Professor's emphasis – red text]: The adjectives for b2b service seems to be mid way between b2c service and credence

**Why is inseparability low in search goods as compared to experience goods?**

[Page 4]

### Product – Inseparability

[Professor's emphasis – red/pink text]:
> Why is inseparability low in search goods as compared to experience goods? (P6)
>
> A typical example of a search good is a washing machine.
> We do not care who manufactures a washing machine…
> …as long as it carries the brand name of our choice (be it Whirlpool or Bosch)
>
> A typical example of an experience good is a chef.
> When the chef changes, the taste of the dish changes.
> That is the concept of inseparability in services.
>
> In products we can separate the product from the person who makes it (washing m/c).
> Which is why a lot of durables and FMCG manufacturing is outsourced.
> Biscuits, soaps, toothpastes, washing machines etc are sub contracted.
> Marks and Spencer's shirts are made in India.
> So in FMCG and durables the focus is on building the brand first.
> In services often the service provider is the brand, they can't be easily separated.

---

### Perceived Risk

**Dimensions of perceived risk?**

[Professor's emphasis – red text]:
> Perceived risk is a subjective assessment of what can go right or wrong.
> What are the different dimensions of perceived risk?

There are 6 dimensions to perceived risk:
- **Functional risk** – this laptop is not functioning
- **Physical risk** – this laptop is damaged in delivery
- **Financial risk** – this laptop ate up too much of my money
- **Social risk** – this laptop's speed is embarrassing
- **Psychological risk** – what if this laptop stops suddenly
- **Time risk** – what if the laptop doesn't come to me on time (p188, Kotler and Keller)

[Professor's emphasis – red text]:
> Now, why is perceived risk greater in a credence > experience > search?
> Because the intangibles are higher.
> The risk of using a bad doctor is more damaging than going to the wrong barber.

---

### Price and Place

**How to know whether something is credence or not?**
- Ask how much does an average employee earn there – revenue of the company per person
- Bulk bracket consulting – MBB, IBM, Infosys 12:4:1
- Search good – a lot more certainty from our side – checkout 3 buy 1
- Experience – you know how good it is while consuming the brand
- Credence – income tax law – difficult to judge someone else's work – corp tax (30-22) for domestic companies, but Reliance paid 3% – hired work – bunch of CAs

[Page 5]

**Showrooming** – look at all the models in a shop, order it online – going to shop only to get information.

Google scholar – Vaio media

### [Professor's emphasis – red text]: Price, Place

#### Price Estimation
[Professor's emphasis – red/pink text]:
> Why is price estimation difficult in a credence good compared to experience good?
> Neuro surgeon vs retail banker.
> How aneurism in brain is handled?
> How a good branch manager in a bank makes life easy for you.
> For which do you pay more and why?
> Your ability to judge a credence good is very low.
> Hence more difficult to estimate price of credence good than experience good.

#### Place
[Professor's emphasis – red text]:
> How far away can it be located?
> How far will you travel for a washing machine (search good)?
> 5 km to a mall, better still can you make it available online, showrooming?

---

### Place, Promotion

**Credence vs Experience goods:**
- High knowledge quotient – your ability to judge them is very limited
- Professional services
- High perceived risk

Eg: Me and mime – all waiters are dough and dumb, bar exchange – price changes with respect to stock market

[Page 6]

### [Professor's emphasis – red text]: Place, Promo

[Professor's emphasis – pink text]:
> How far will you travel for a good restaurant (experience good)?
> A little more, maybe 10 km
>
> How far will you travel for a good tax consultant (credence good)?
> How far will you travel for Haj, Vaishno Devi (super credence good)?
> Any distance, it is very important.

#### Promotion
[Professor's emphasis – pink text]:
> What is the best ad for a restaurant?
> Word-of-mouth from existing customers.
> Interestingly we are too busy flashing more ads trying to get new customers.
> Ads are important but not half as important as serving the existing customers well.
> There is only one way of growing in services in the long haul, what is it?
> Building a sustained reputation for service with **[emphasis]** existing customers.

---

[Page 7]

### [Professor's emphasis – red text]: Promotion, Physical Evidence

#### Physical Evidence
[Professor's emphasis – red text]:
> Define physical evidence in one line?
> Sights, sounds and smells of the service.
>
> In a washing machine physical evidence is inevitable.
> If the machine doesn't work it won't be accepted.
> Physical evidence as product is almost intrinsic to a search good.
>
> But in an experience a gestalt of tangibles and intangibles matters.
> Therefore in a multiplex, theatre being Dolby equipped is important.
> But as important is the fact that the food counter guys are polite.
> The movie hall staff must be courteous.
> [Professor's question – red text]: Did you go to the multiplex for movie or courtesy?

[Professor's emphasis – red text]: Physical Evidence (continued)

> In a credence good physical evidence is even more difficult.
> What is physical about a psychiatrist's or a lawyer's advice?
> [Professor's emphasis – green text]: How much did Reliance pay as corporate tax in 2020-21? [1]
> [Professor's emphasis – red text]: 3% vs 22% applicable – thanks to the tax planning skills of its C.A.s
> They are not bunking taxes, they are using exemptions in tax laws.

---

### Brand as Process

**Why is process so important in services?**
- More customer centric
- Customer engagement is more
- Credence / experience – you're a part of the service
- For replicability and scaling – standardisation

[Professor's emphasis – red text]: Brand as Process

> Why is process so important in services?
> For replicability and scaling.
> The process of interaction between a customer and a bank is usually clear.
> The same is not true between a patient and a doctor or a client and a lawyer.
> Much more complex, why?
> [Professor's questions – red text]: How many legal intricacies does a client understand?
> How many things like "angina pectoris" and "pyrexia" do you understand?
> Thus a tax consultant may have to customize more compared to a retail bank.
> [Professor's example – pink text]: Palkhivala's junior team
>
> Also customer's involvement is much higher in a credence good.
> Meaning, there is more **"anxiety"** surrounding a doctor's or a lawyer's service.
> An amusement park or a movie theatre has involvement, but **"fun"**.

---

[Page 8]

### Organisation

[Professor's emphasis – red text]: Brand as Organization

#### Organizational Culture
- Experience good like retail bank – operations driven, incremental innovation
- Credence good like consultancy – knowledge driven, purposeful and deep
- [Professor's emphasis – pink text]: IIMB constantly adding cases written by faculty to the general pool of knowledge

#### Skill Set Required
- Retail Bank – routine skills, meticulous, process driven
- Filling the forms mandatorily and diligently – [Professor's emphasis – pink text]: Tech Mahindra AI system for banks
- Consultancy – advanced skills, continuous upgradation

#### Type of Person Needed
- Bank – specialist with certain general capabilities
- Consultancy – specialist with deep knowledge in his/her domain
- [Professor's emphasis – pink text]: Actuaries who design insurance policies have very tough qualifying exams

---

### Person

[Professor's emphasis – red text]: Brand as Person

#### Role
- Experience good like retail bank – solves simple problems ([Professor's example – pink text]: ATM not functioning)
- Credence good like consultancy – solves advanced problems ([Professor's example – pink text]: factory location choice)

#### Personification
- Experience good – one who engages and delights ([Professor's example – pink text]: a well made movie)
- Credence good – one who enlightens and clarifies ([Professor's example – pink text]: a good teacher)

- Bank – [Professor's emphasis – pink text]: Good natured uncle you believe
- Consultancy – [Professor's emphasis – pink text]: Expert who doesn't waste his time or yours

---

### Symbol

[Professor's emphasis – red text]: Brand as Symbol

#### Logo
- Experience good – a lively symbol ([Professor's example – pink text]: a happy family)
- Credence good – a reflective symbol ([Professor's example – pink text]: queen on a chess board)

#### Adjectives
- Experience good – connecting, pleasant, accommodating
  - [Professor's example – pink text]: Somebody like Shah Rukh Khan or Amitabh Bachchan
- Credence good – deep, wise
  - [Professor's example – pink text]: Somebody like Chanakya or Einstein

#### Typical Slogan
- Experience good – "your friend, well wisher"
  - [Professor's example – pink text]: Like a friendly neighbour
- Credence good – "your advisor, guide"
  - [Professor's example – pink text]: Like C K Prahlad, the strategy guru

---

[Page 9]

In credence, there is a power relationship.

**Properties / Characteristics of Credence Goods:**
- Expert, best, no other substitute
- Hard to judge
- Very hard to put a price on
- Very high on intangibles (80%)
- Very high knowledge quotient
- Difficult to judge even after consumption
- Book – Karma Yogi – about the metro man
- Movie – modern times – great comedy

### [Professor's emphasis – red text]: Credence vs Others

[Professor's emphasis – red text]:
> What are defining characteristics of credence good?
> How do you clearly set apart a credence good from an experience good?

i) Credence goods are very high on intangibles (80%?)

ii) Credence good difficult to judge even after consumption – Pavrotti, B. Joshi, case method (Case method – questions)

iii) Credence goods cost a lot, because it is knowledge work, price estimation difficult

iv) Credence goods deliver an order of magnitude difference in service
   - [Professor's contrast – pink text]: Delighted (experience) vs dumbstruck (credence) – Delhi Metro in 10,500 crore

iv) Credence goods inspire a lot of fakes
   - [Professor's examples – green text]: Vivekanda vs fake sadhus, fake paintings of Picasso, Charlie "Outchaplined!"

v) Credence goods are difficult to delegate (attorney blocking)

vi) Ads are not of much use if you are a credence good (IIT, IIM)

[Professor's emphasis – red text]: Credence vs Others (continued)

vii) Credence goods are in cutting edge work ([Professor's examples – pink text]: Parikh – Satyam saved, Bill Gross)

viii) Credence goods are science (doctor), experience goods are art ([Professor's example – pink text]: Picasso's paintings)
   - Both of them are difficult to judge
   - What if it is between the two?
   - [Professor's emphasis – green text]: Ask if it is more art or more science

**What follows:**

[Page 10]

### [Professor's emphasis – red text]: What Follows

[Professor's emphasis – pink text]:
> The one word that characterizes a credence good?
> **Belief**
>
> What is the key to a credence good?
> What is the one common skill the following need?
> Doctor, teacher, classical music, lawyer, tax consultant, credit rating, spiritual gurus
> **Knowledge**
> [Professor's emphasis – pink text]: Belief in a credence good stems from the assurance of its knowledge quotient
>
> You are trying to become a credence good.
> Do you have "knowledge" to stake claim as an I-banker, consultant?

### Brand Engagement

**Digital Marketing** – students ppt
**Branding Digitally**
**JSW – B2B Brands – extreme engineering – Nat Geo**
**Drivers of Engagement**
**Singularization**

[Professor's emphasis – red text]: Brand Engagement

#### Drivers of Engagement

**i) Brand Characteristics**

a) Brands with non-rational benefits engage better
   - [Professor's emphasis – pink text]: JK's Smart Tyre has digital tracking of real time information on vehicle location, air-leak detection, engine diagnosis [1]
   - This offers "peace of mind"

b) Brands publicly consumed engage better (generally)
   - [Professor's emphasis – pink text]: Kabaddi or FIFA has to be watched live, Singularization! [1a]

**ii) Message Characteristics**
   - [Professor's emphasis – red text]: Is the message relevant?
   - [Professor's emphasis – pink text]: Cashify.in helps you buy, sell, repair, recycle your mobiles [2]

Engagement depends on how many non-digital interesting things you have.

Banks – Mumbai – dabbawalaas

Pakistan – kidnapping industry – lorries kidnap – always moving

[Page 11]

[Professor's emphasis – red text]: Brand Engagement

**iii) Medium Characteristics**
   - [Professor's emphasis – red text]: How unique is your channel?
   - [Professor's example – pink text]: FM Radio in cities, Ads in metro, CCTV in small town stations

**iv) Consumer Characteristics**
   a) Loyalty – a middle aged buyer of the Hindu newspaper
   b) Self expressiveness – pride, fun [4]
      - https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=_BVfneC6dXY [upto 1:15]
      - "Coca-Cola – The Happy Flag"
   c) Co-creation – "Dettol co-created with Moms" consults 800 mothers [5]

### Levels of Brand Engagement

[Professor's emphasis – red text]: Levels of Engagement

**High Brand Engagement:**
- [Professor's emphasis – pink text]: Xiaomi's first fans, volunteer testers of its user interface MIUI
- Updates its OS every week based on fan updates [9]
- [Professor's emphasis – pink text]: Jayadevan's book on Xiaomi on how fan clubs are built [6], [7]
- Xiaomi is taking the place of Huawei
- [Professor's emphasis – pink text]: Fans fly thousands of miles to be in its product launch [8]

[Professor's emphasis – green text]: Check the latest problem fans solved for Xiaomi:
> "When you ring an office/bank in India, you will hear an annoying 'interactive voice recognition' (IVR) system. Xiaomi's unpaid fans are painstakingly mapping all the options ('for billing, press 3') etc and uploading the results onto the fan site. Xiaomi then adds this feature in its next update. Next time you can use MIUI's visual cues to navigate IVR in seconds rather than waste many minutes" [9]

Medium – good, not great – co-creation

Low – in high frequency buys – likely, not necessary. Why?
- Yes, haven't put much thought into it
- No, Dettol: frequently buying it, but it co-creates with mothers
- Yes, FMCG where competitors are adding new features – habitual buying

**Other products/services with low brand loyalty?**
- Habitual buying
- Situations in which you can't go from shop to shop

[Page 12]

- Eg: Bottled water – fulfilling immediate need
- Multiplexes – you're engaged more with the movies, than with the multiplex – content is more important

**Indicators of Low Brand Loyalty:**
- How many shops will you go for?
- Advocacy – how many people will you tell?
- How many future purchases will you make?

[Professor's emphasis – red text]: Levels of Engagement

**Moderate Brand Engagement:**
- [Professor's emphasis – pink text]: Giving useful feedback to the brand if there is an issue
- ITC being asked to tweak its sauce to make it spicy

**Low Brand Engagement:**
- [Professor's emphasis – pink text]: Author says low engagement happens in high frequency buys
- Are we sure of this? When does it happen?
- In convenience buying
- For instance, you run out of salt or milk at home
- The neighborhood grocery doesn't have your brand
- You buy whatever is there
- [Professor's question – pink text]: Think of other products/services with low brand loyalty? Multiplexes?, bottled water?, incandescent lamps?

**Bounce Rate:**
- Clicks and leaves, comes in and goes out
- How long is important in engagement

[Professor's emphasis – red text]: Levels of Engagement (continued)

a) Dhara's Facebook has 1,50,000 followers
   - Only 0.1% report liking their posts, engagement should be better

b) Whirlpool has lower bounce rates than competitors globally
   - Bounce Rate is the percentage of single-page sessions (i.e. sessions in which the person left your site from the entrance page without interacting with the page)

c) Mysore Sandal has only 684 likes on its Facebook pages
   - By contrast, Santoor has 196000 likes

[Page 13]

**Negative Engagement:**
Videos of money like news

When there is an adverse reaction? What would be your first move?

[Professor's emphasis – red text]: Levels of Engagement

**Negative Engagement:**
i) Poor reviews on mouthshut.com, Amazon
ii) Interestingly several strong brands suffer it selectively. But they also have a bunch of loyal fans
iii) Cafe Coffee Day's tryst with negative social media coverage
iv) Finance companies get negative coverage on YouTube for the manner in which they recover money that is due
v) Across media platforms (Instagram, Twitter etc) PVR gets more positive engagement than negative

[Professor's emphasis – pink text]: Imp – When there is adverse reaction, especially when the brand knows it has committed a mistake:
- Own up an apology
- Otherwise, PR crisis overnight

The problem with digital is it doesn't stop with a handful of people, it's the spread.

**Types of Engagement:**
- More engaged influencing the less engaged
- Bunch of opinion leaders influencing others

[Professor's emphasis – highlighted yellow]: Website – mart(pradeep kashyap)
[Professor's emphasis – highlighted yellow]: For articles on rural marketing

Digital world is seamless; so it will comeback to you.

---

### [Professor's emphasis – red text]: 3 Types of Engagement

**i) Trickle Down Effect?**
- [Professor's emphasis – pink text]: It is not the rich influencing the poor
- It is the "more engaged" influencing "the less engaged"
- [Professor's emphasis – pink text]: Rural youth (especially immigrants) engage with digital media
- They are important influencers of durable purchases [P. Kashyap]

**ii) Reverse Influence?**
- A few negatively engaged users reversing the majority view
- [Professor's examples – pink text]: Nano negative w-o-m, fire in the car, just a few cases [21]
- ITC got a court order to restrain a video that said….
- …Aashirvad atta had plastic in it [22]

**iii) Hypertargeting? Prerequisite?**
- I should know your activity minute by minute
- [Professor's examples – pink text]: Sugarbox – app for hyperlocal entertainment (in railway station)
- Airtel can geo-target customers every minute [23]

[Page 14]

It depends on what you stand for.

**Eg. of Low level engagement, high loyalty?**

### Platforms / Channels of Engagement

[Professor's emphasis – red text]: Platforms of Engagement

**Three types of channels:**
i) Paid channel – like a YouTube ad sponsored by a brand
ii) Owned channel – company's own site
   - https://www.jayanti.international/; https://www.sukra.com/
iii) Earned channel – e.g. brand likes on Facebook
   - [Professor's example – pink text]: Bandhan Bank has 360982 followers and 356928 likes. Brand seems to be positively engaged.
iv) Shared media – forwards, word-of-mouth
   - [Professor's note – pink text]: This seems like a variant of earned channel

[Professor's emphasis – red text]: Platforms of Engagement (continued)

i) Company website
ii) E-mail marketing
iii) Display ads

**Social Media Paid Channels:**
i) Facebook
ii) Twitter
iii) Instagram
iv) Pinterest
v) Video
Besides:
- [Professor's emphasis – pink text]: Mobile marketing, influencer marketing, content marketing…
- Some of these are platforms, some are types of engagement

---

### Brand Equity Measurement

Brand equity measurement system – research procedure – up to date data about it and its competitors.
- Indirect approach – identifying and tracking customers brand knowledge – feelings / perceptions
- Direct approach – actual impact on customer's brand knowledge – marketing program

**Return on Marketing Investment (ROMI)**

[Page 15]

- Measuring long-term value of marketing in terms of short term and long term impact on consumers
- ROMI measures tied to short term change in sales

---

### Brand Audit

Examining a brand to find out its sources of brand equity – recommend plan of action – improve marketing performance.

Internal – company focused exercise.

Understanding brand both from the perspective of firm and consumer.

Whenever shifts in strategic directions are likely.

**Steps:**
- Agreement on objective, scopes and approach
- Data Collection
- Report preparation and presentation

Also, external, customer focused – assess health of brand.

**Maximising Internal Branding:**
- Positive and more productive work environment
- Help foster an organisation's identity
- Clear positioning within employees
- What do you stand for?
- Employees become passionate brand advocates

**Great places to work:**
1. https://www.greatplacetowork.in/great/profile/india-best/Ujjivan-Small-Finance-Bank
2. https://www.greatplacetowork.in/great/rated/100-best/Sayaji-Hotels-Ltd [multiple sub-links provided for Sayaji Hotels]

[Professor's emphasis – highlighted yellow]: **Barbeque Nation** (service) – [Multiple links provided for Barbeque Nation as example of great place to work]

## Secondary Associations

### [Professor's emphasis – red text]: How to Leverage Secondary Associations?

i) **Companies** (through branding strategies)
   - Gorilla glass (Corning) extended its application to mobiles

ii) **Countries** (through country-of-origin)
   - Wine famous because it hails from Champagne in France
   - Literature also calls it authenticity or ingredient story

iii) **Channels** (through type of channel used)
   - Brand stocked in Prada vs Westside

iv) **Other Brands** (through co-branding)
   - Lego co-branding with Adidas
   - LEGO x Adidas Originals #ATOZX #ADIDASZX
   - Shoe made in the signature colors of Legos

[Page 18]

### [Professor's emphasis – red text]: How to Leverage Secondary Associations? (continued)

v) **Characters** (through licensing)
   - Chota Bheem's association with Asian Paints, Savlon etc [1], [2]

vi) **Spokespersons** (through endorsement)
   - Hema Malini endorsing Kent

vii) **Events** (through channel strategy)
   - Vivo was the main sponsor for IPL in 2021

viii) **Third Party Sources** (awards)
   - ISO-9000, CMM Level 5

---

### [Professor's emphasis – red text]: Leveraging the Company

This aspect will be dealt with separately in brand architecture.
Here we focus on what can be done using sibling brands.

a) Create a new brand
   - [Professor's example – pink text]: Tata consumer has launched the brand Sampann to expand its food range to spices, dal, besan and so on.

b) Adopt or modify an existing brand
   - [Professor's example – pink text]: Parle-G, G became "genius" from "glucose"

c) Combine with an existing brand
   - [Professor's example – pink text]: Maruti moving into Nexa to improve image

---

### [Professor's emphasis – red text]: Leveraging Distribution

The book's account of leveraging distribution is narrow, why?
- It considers only how the retailer impacts the brand
- But in India organized retail is not very big
- Therefore, a brand is more impacted by its own channels

Consider the following:
i) Banks using correspondent model
ii) Epigamia uses modern trade channel more than others [4]
iii) ID Foods ships directly to consumers to deliver fresh food in time
iv) Companies using dabbawallas to deliver pizzas in Mumbai
v) HUL's Shakti SHGs now cover distribution in half of rural India

**Co-Branding** – only when both the brand names appear

[Page 19]

### [Professor's emphasis – red text]: What is Co-Branding?

i) Co-branding allows brand assets to be leveraged with other brand names to derive a brand value that is greater than both the individual parts (Rao & Ruekert, 1994)

ii) Co-branding occurs when 2 or more brands come together for a joint offering in some form (Keller et al., 2011)

iii) Co-branding alliances are described as multiple brands forming a separate and unique product, with their success being reliant on producing positive consumer evaluations (Dickinson & Heath, 2008)

[Professor's emphasis – red text]: What are the other words/phrases associated with co-branding?
Strategic alliances, joint marketing, joint branding, joint promotion & composite brand extension.

---

### Co-Branding – Interesting Concepts

i) A necessary condition for co-branding's success is that the two brands should have brand equity individually.

ii) More importantly there must be a logical fit between the two brands
   - [Professor's example – pink text]: Lego partnered with Shell for sometime, why? [5]
   - To benefit from race car association, speed….
   - But broke it in 2014, why? [6]
   - [Professor's explanation – pink text]: Oil companies seen as polluters by general public, Greenpeace protests

iii) Is Amitabh Bachchan endorsing ICICI Bank co-branding?
   - [Professor's answer – pink text]: No, it is celebrity endorsement.
   - When is it co-branding?
   - [Professor's answer – pink text]: Both names should appear simultaneously
   - Adidas Neo by Selena Gomez

### Co-Branding – Interesting Concepts (continued)

iv) How do you know if an association between two brands is an e.g. of co-branding or not?
   - [Professor's answer – pink text]: Both the brands should be seen in the ad

v) Collaboration between a company's own brands is also co-branding, according to Keller
   - Usually co-branding is between brands of 2 different companies

[Page 20]

### [Professor's emphasis – red text]: Why Co-Brand?

**Multiple Reasons:**

i) Pooling competencies – PVR and Pepsi (Kapferer, 2012, p 144-6)
   - PVR needs F&B revenue, Pepsi needs distribution

ii) To enter a segment that needs skills opposed to skills in present one
   - Ola is a very mundane brand, Myntra is fashion
   - An exciting makeover for Ola through Ola Myntra express
   - Young girls get customers' sartorial tastes, likes, dislikes on phone
   - They then custom make an image makeover dress for them
   - Deliver it to customers personally in an Ola-Myntra express (car)

iii) To move up or down the price line
   - Puma with Ferrari – to access class audience
   - Citi with Delhi Metro – to access mass audience

### [Professor's emphasis – red text]: Why Co-Brand? (continued)

iv) To monetize a short time licensing window
   - K. Ranaut range for Global Desi, Magnum ice cream for S. Kapoor

v) To create a buzz around the brand
   - Lifebuoy co-branding with Hulk
   - If LB changes color from white to green, Hulk kills 99.9% of germs
   - [Link provided]

vi) Improves customer confidence when 2 strong brands are together
   - Toyota and Maruti Suzuki cross-badge each other's brands…
   - …as part of a global deal
   - MS gets Toyota's Corolla and technology
   - Toyota gets access to MS's Baleno, Ciaz (premium range)

---

### [Professor's emphasis – red text]: 4 Levels of Co-Branding

**What are the 4 levels of co-branding (CB)?**
1. Reach/Awareness CB
2. Values Endorsement CB
3. Ingredient CB
4. Complementary Competence CB

[Professor's emphasis – red text]: What increases as we move up the 4 levels?
**Seriousness of an engagement**

---

[Page 21]

### [Professor's emphasis – red text]: Reach Co-Brand

[Professor's question – red text]: When does reach co-branding happen?
When one of the brands is a distribution gateway.

[Professor's question – red text]: Examples of reach/awareness co-branding?
When the two brands appear side by side, don't do much to each other:
i) Shopper's Stop and HDFC
ii) HSBC Westside
iii) Visa with Citi and JetAir

**[Image Description – Reach Co-Brand Example]:**
A photograph of a gold-coloured credit card showing:
- "JET AIRWAYS" branding at the top left
- "CitiBusiness" in the middle-left
- Card number: 4564 3799 8765 400X
- Expiry dates: 00/00 and 01/01
- Cardholder name: ANIL PRASAD JP/105385865
- "citibank" logo on the right side
- "VISA" logo prominently displayed at the bottom right
This is an example of a Jet Airways + Citibank + Visa three-way reach/awareness co-brand on a credit card.

  - "Imran Khan And Mandira Bedi At Ariel India And Whirlpool Event"
  - [YouTube link provided] (upto 30 sec)

---

### Ingredient Co-Branding

**Why ingredient branding?**

i) The ingredient must be highly differentiated and create sustainable value for customers (Kotler and Pfoertsch, 2015)

ii) The ingredient is central to the performance of final product

iii) OEM encourages co-branding, because ingredient invests in the brand specific to OEM offerings

iv) The final product itself is highly differentiated, therefore makes business sense for the ingredient to co-brand

v) The ingredients might themselves sell separately as brands

vi) When you want to directly address the customer's customer – b2b2c

[Page 22]

### Some Ingredient Co-Brands:
- Nvidia
- Java
- Nutrasweet
- Kevlar – bicycle tyres, bullet proof vests, shoes, racing sails
- Tetrapak
- Blaupunkt
- Bose
- Swarovski (with designers)
- Cummins
- Zeiss
- Tyre brands – MRF, Ceat, Michelin, Bridgestone
- GE
- Bosch

Lycra, a material ingredient brand.
Co-branded with jeans – Calvin Klein, Henry and Belle, Marc Allison.
Ad – "Denim with LYCRA® fabric Taxi TV ad"

Any outstanding examples of ingredient co-branding?
Du Pont has 1700 brands [3].
What is Poly tetra flouro ethylene better known as?
**Teflon**
Teflon co-branded with several other applications (paint, non-stick cook)
"Royale Teflon Ad"

**Intel Inside** – mother of all ingredient brands

Any other interesting example?

"A dragon is coming – to your phone" (Qualcomm Snapdragon commercial)

Dolby with theatres, more co-promotion with theatres, with Mary Kom!
"Exclusive Screening of Mary Kom In Dolby Atmos"

Tetrapak?, Intel?

### Complementary Competence Co-Branding (CCC)

**When can a relationship be called CCC?**
- Exclusivity and Longevity seem to be must haves for CCC
- When there is high integration of activities between co-brands, meaning?
- Joint design and production of a new product, competence sharing

**How will you get illustrations for CCC?**
- Look for JVs, e.g.
- Classic example in India – **Maruti Suzuki**

Others in India:
- Tata-Starbucks
- HDFC-Ergo

[Professor's emphasis – highlighted cyan]: **Licensing?**

### Extension Evaluation Grid

[Professor's emphasis – red text]: How to group items in Kapferer's grid under some headings?

[Professor's emphasis – green text]: External aspects, meaning?
1. Is it a growing market?
6. How entrenched are competitors?
7. How fast can they copy?
12. Will distribution accept it?

[Professor's emphasis – green text]: Brand related aspects:
5. Will it impact positively the brand equity?
8. Does the product have a clear differentiation?
3. Are the brand assets transferable?
13. Is it consistent with brand identity? (removed Co reference)

[Page 24]

### Extension Evaluation Grid (continued)

[Professor's emphasis – red text]: Organizational aspects, meaning?
16. Does it capitalise on the company's expertise in:
   a) production?
   b) advertising?
   c) logistics?
   d) sales force?
   e) retail location?
   f) pricing/promotion?

17. Does this meet the company's profitability objectives?

18. Can the company sustain competition (Does it have the financial resources needed to compete)?

[Professor's emphasis – red text]: Extension Evaluation Grid – Fit (External-Internal Dialogue), meaning?
2. Are its success factors close to our strengths?
4. Are the brand assets still assets in this market?

[Professor's emphasis – green text]: Customers:
14. Does it capitalize on the brand or company's present customers?
9. Is it a motivating difference? (for customers)

[Professor's emphasis – green text]: Redundant points?
10. Can the company produce it?
11. Can it produce at a normal cost?
- [Professor's note – pink text]: Most examples are FMCG, subcontracting is the norm
- Most sensitive defense equipment is subcontracted (NASA, Hitachi, Toshiba)

[Page 25]

### Extension Evaluation Grid – Summary

[Professor's emphasis – red text]: What is strength of the grid?
Fairly detailed

[Professor's emphasis – green text]: Any pointers missed?
Managing 4Ps of the extension (limitation of this pointer?)

Can we find a better heading for external aspects?
Growing market? Competitor strength? Easy to copy?
Porter – Market attractiveness (plus value chain)

[Professor's emphasis – green text]: Focus on 6 broad issues in brand extension:
i) Market attractiveness
ii) Brand value consistency
iii) Organizational strengths
iv) Internal-external fit
v) Customers
vi) Managing 4Ps

[Professor's question – red text]: Any issue not mentioned here but impacts extensions?

Answer – employees – fit with the brand extension, extension should fit well with the skill set of employees in a service industry.

---

### Learning from the Grid

[Professor's emphasis – red text]: What are the learnings from the application of grid to extension success and failure?

i) The grid is a good tool to assess success or failure

ii) Difficult to assess market attractiveness (Mahindra, Dabur, Aashirvad)

iii) Even when the extension is weak on some of the parameters, with organizational persistence the brand might succeed

iv) A brand extension may fail even if one of the 5/6 parameters in the grid fail

v) Greatest probability of success if the extension ticks several boxes

---

### [Professor's emphasis – red text]: Brand Extension – Aaker and Keller

Aaker and Keller (1990) opine…..
An extension should satisfy 3 criteria [E]:
[Professor's emphasis – pink text]: Complementarity, Substitutability, and Transferability; meaning?
- [Professor's emphasis – pink text]: Complementarity – hair oil extending into a hair comb
- [Professor's emphasis – pink text]: Substitutability – not easily substitutable (a new H. Potter book)
- [Professor's emphasis – pink text]: Transferability – brand assets transferable? Success factors close?

As long as you upgrade, that's good.
If you become contemporary, it's a problem. Extension is not necessary to be successful.

[Page 26]

### [Key concept – blue text]: Brand Extension – Brand Name Has to Appear.

**[Diagram Description – Figure 12.9: Type of Brand and Ability to Extend Further]:**
A triangular / pyramid-style diagram showing brand extendability. The vertical axis is labeled "Type of brand" with five levels from bottom to top: Product, Formula, Know-how, Interest, Philosophy. The horizontal axis represents "Distance between extension and the brand prototype" with positions A, B, (C), D, E marked left to right. The diagram shows five nested triangles of increasing size, with:
- The innermost (smallest) triangle corresponding to "Product" brand type – has the narrowest base (least extendability)
- Progressively larger triangles for Formula, Know-how, Interest
- The outermost (largest) triangle for "Philosophy" brand type – has the widest base (most extendability, can extend to any distance from brand prototype)
This illustrates that the higher the brand type on the hierarchy, the further/wider the brand can extend.

**Example for these:**

Formula – Are there brands/situations where it becomes difficult to stretch?
These are brands that are very strong in their parent category.
If the brands become strongly identified with parent category, it becomes difficult to stretch.
Eg: Colgate – very strong dental equity – very difficult to stretch beyond mouth.

### Brand Extendability Table

| Type | Key Word | Extendability | Examples |
|---|---|---|---|
| Formula brand (e.g., Lifebuoy) | soap/steel | Poor | Dhara, Lifebuoy, Colgate |
| Know-how brand (e.g., Bic, Eureka Forbes) | disposable/clean | Better | Eureka Forbes, Bic, Amul (food), Asian Paints (décor) |
| Interest brand (e.g., Sony) | digital (TV, laptop, game) | Wide | Sony, Panasonic in India |
| Philosophy brand (e.g., Tata, Griffes) | anything | Elastic | Tata, Samsung, Amazon (future?) |

*Additional note:* Adjective common to water purifier and vacuum cleaner? **Clean**. Adjective common to lighter, ball pen and razor? **Disposable**.

### [Professor's emphasis – red/blue]: Principles of Brand Extension

### [Professor's emphasis – red text]: Types of Brand Extension

[Professor's question – red text]: Under how many headings can all the brand extensions in the world be classified?

**[Diagram Description – Types of Brand Extension Classification]:**
A branching tree diagram showing how marketing literature classifies brand extensions, with a parallel "Our classification" and "Illustration" column:
- "Line Extension" in marketing literature → maps to "Product Related" in our classification → Illustration: Gillette Mach3, Vector
- "Brand Extension" in marketing literature branches into two:
  - → "Image Related" → Illustration: Gillette skincare solution
  - → "Unrelated" → Illustration: Gillette telecom!

Notes:
- Product related are variants
- Image related are related products – neighbouring categories
- Unrelated – conglomerates – not been doing too well – careful, don't spread to too many businesses

Last 10 years, lending rates going down. Money is getting cheap now. One reason why conglomerates were strong earlier was access to capital was difficult in the past.

**Vertical Brand Extensions:**
- Difficult either way
- What is the word you own in the mind of the customer – if its low price / anything to do with price, stretching it will be difficult
- On the other hand, e.g. Tata – different price in different categories
- Astrum, Centrum, Steelium – B2B brands – Tata

### [Professor's emphasis – red text]: Tauber's 7 Extension Types

1. **Same product in a different form** – Surf solid, liquid
2. **Distinctive ingredient** – Liva (viscose)
3. **Companion product** – Colgate tooth brush and paste
4. **Customer franchise** – Manyavar (weddings)
5. **Expertise** – Honda engines
6. **Benefit** – Chik Hairfall prevent
7. **Designer/ethnic** – Anita Dongre, Mysore Silk

### [Professor's emphasis – red text]: Why Extend a Brand?

[Professor's emphasis – red text]: ONE IN TEN LAUNCHES CLICK
- New brand launch costs 50 million $, hence new brand launch risky
- Counter – How many brand extensions click?

i) **ENERGIZING A BRAND** – e.g.?
   - [Professor's emphasis – pink text]: Increases visibility if the brand extends into more variants
   - Eg: Odonil Citra, Jasmine, Lavender, Rose, Sandal

ii) **EXPANDING CORE PROMISE TO NEW USERS** – e.g.?
   - [Professor's emphasis – pink text]: Godrej shaving cream to shaving cake

Book – Apple: A History of Egomania – Jim Carlton

---

### [Professor's emphasis – red text]: Cons of Brand Extension

[Professor's emphasis – pink text]:
> Failure of the extension might have adverse implication for the parent. How far is this fear justified?
> Apple's Newton, Audi 4000 to Audi 5000

[Professor's question – pink text]:
> How severe is this problem of adverse feedback in India?
> Not much evidence
> i) Parle's Frooti orange vs Frooti mango
> ii) Pond's soap, shampoo, toothpaste failure vs facial care
> iii) Nirma tooth paste vs Nirma detergent

**Paradox of brand extensions**

Indian customer is a baniya – doesn't care whether the brand is Indian or not.

[Page 29]

### [Professor's emphasis – red text]: Paradox of Brand Extensions

[Professor's emphasis – pink text]:
> What is common to Horlicks, Ponds, Lux?
> Parents successful, extensions failed.
> If the brand has a huge market share in the parent category, then…extending it will be very difficult.
>
> What is the plus and minus of having a strong brand?
> (+) → market share, profits, leadership
> (-) → very strong brands cannot be extended
>
> Unless a brand is strong it cannot be extended. If it is too strong it cannot be extended. How to know, when to extend when not to?
> After the parent crosses 25% market share – progressively difficult.

---

### [Professor's emphasis – red text]: Brand Extension – Good

**Good – Quality Associations:**
[Professor's emphasis – pink text]:
> Perceived quality of the brand in its original context is a significant predictor of how the extension would be evaluated.
> Meaning?
> If the parent Park Avenue (dressing) is seen as good, then…
> …their extension into hair care, perfume also seen as good.
> http://parkavenuegroomin.com/

**Bad – Poor Quality Perceptions:**
[Professor's emphasis – pink text]:
> What does this mean?
> If the parent has poor quality perceptions, can it be stretched?
> Can Viva be stretched?
> Can Maltova be stretched?
> Why bother? First attend to the parent, make it stronger.

Have share of voice, doesn't have share of pocket – brands like this cannot be stretched.
First get the parent to perform, then think about extension.

---

### [Professor's emphasis – red text]: Brand Extensions – "Ugly"

[Professor's emphasis – pink text]:
> **Ugly** – Existing associations are weakened.
> This happened in the case of King Fisher.
> One wrong brand extension into airlines killed the group.
> Vijay Mallya had to not only sell liquor business to pay debts.
> He also had to flee the country to save himself from arrest!

At least 7 other categories.

Second article on brand extensions is not compulsory for your exams.

### Brand Relationship Spectrum

**What's the relationship with the parent?**
9 types of relationship.

**[Diagram Description – Brand Relationship Spectrum (Aaker)]:**
A hierarchical tree diagram showing the full Brand Relationship Spectrum. The main trunk is "Brand Relationship Spectrum" which splits into three main branches:

1. **House of Brands** – splits into:
   - **Not Connected** – examples: RCA (GE), Saturn (GM), Nutrisweet (GD Searle)
   - **Shadow Endorser** – examples: Tide (P&G), Lexus (Toyota), Touchstone (Disney)

2. **Endorsed Brands** – splits into:
   - **Token Endorsement** – examples: Grape Nuts from Post, Universal Pictures, A Sony Company; Docker's, LS & Co.
   - **Linked Name** – examples: DKNY, McMuffin, Nestea
   - **Strong Endorsement** – examples: Courtyard by Marriott, Obsession by Calvin Klein, Friends & Family by MCI

3. **Subbrands** – splits into:
   - **Co-Drivers** – examples: Gillette Mach3, Sony Trinitron, DuPont Stainmaster
   - **Master Brand as Driver** – examples: Buick LeSabre, HP Deskjet, Dell Dimension

4. **Branded House** – splits into:
   - **Different Identity** – examples: GE Capital, GE Appliance; Club Med Singles vs. Couples; Levi-Europe, Levi US
   - **Same Identity** – examples: BMW, Healthy Choice, Virgin

**Which type would you choose and why?**
- Shadow endorser – discreet connect with the parent – there but not there

**Which is the one type you would surely avoid and why?**

**How do I know whether it is shadow / token?**
- Label / packaging
- E.g.: Sunfeast

[Page 31]

[Professor's emphasis – pink text]:
> There are 9 types of relationship.
> Which type would you choose and why?
> Which is the one type you would surely avoid and why?
> [Professor's question – red text]: How to know if a design is a house of brands, shadow/token endorser?
> 4 bases for knowing this:
> a) Packaging
> b) See the communication – ads, sp, dm, pr, ps. First two platforms are easily accessible
> c) Ask the customer
> d) Ask the company (for unconnected and shadow endorser because customer's view can't be ascertained)

For ads refer the websites:
- https://www.afaqs.com/creative-showcase (TV, digital, OOH, print)
- https://www.adsoftheworld.com/
- https://www.warc.com/
- Company and brand websites
- YouTube videos of brand ads

[Professor's question – red text]: Difference between "not connected" and "shadow endorser"?

**Not Connected:**
- Parent doesn't want customer to know…the brand and the parent are connected
- Brands speak for themselves
- [Professor's emphasis – pink text]: Companies prefer the connection not seen

**Shadow Endorser:**
- Company doesn't communicate
- Customers get to know that the brand and parent are connected
- Parent doesn't mind it
- A remote connect might be even useful to the parent
- [Professor's emphasis – red text]: The difference between them is the intention of the parent

**Not Connected:** Parent doesn't want customer to know. Prefer you not to know the connect.
**Shadow Endorser:** Parent doesn't tell you, but you get to know. Company doesn't mind if you know the connect.

[Professor's emphasis – red text]:
> House of brands is in 2 types – i) unconnected ii) shadow endorser

**House of Brands – Unconnected:**
i) Natraj and Apsara unconnected to Hind Pencils
ii) Reckitt Benckiser's brands Dettol, Moov and Cherry Blossom do not mention it in ads
iii) Volkswagen India doesn't mention Audi

### [Professor's emphasis – green text]: Twin Pillar Architecture

**[Image Description – Kissan to Knorr Rebrand]:**
A "Before and After" comparison of two product packages (pizza and pasta sauce):
- **BEFORE (left):** A blue/light-coloured pouch labeled "Kissan Pizza & Pasta Sauce" with the Kissan brand prominently displayed at the top, featuring a tomato image and the sauce in an appealing visual.
- **AFTER (right):** A dark green/red coloured pouch labeled "Knorr Pizza & Pasta Sauce" with the Knorr brand prominently displayed, featuring a cooked pizza/pasta dish image in an appetizing photo.
Caption: Kissan – ketchup, jam (old) → Knorr – pasta, pizza sauce (new). This illustrates a brand transition where the product category was moved from the Kissan pillar (associated with ketchup/jam) to the Knorr pillar (associated with culinary/pasta sauces), demonstrating twin pillar brand architecture in practice.

### Brand Reinforcement – Managing Brands Longitudinally

PG: 557

**[Diagram Description – Managing Brands Longitudinally Table (Slide)]:**
A two-column comparison table titled "Managing Brands Longitudinally":

| Reinforcing | Revitalizing |
|---|---|
| Incremental | Evolving |
| Rin det. bar | My father's brand? Yours too! |
| Mohammed Ali | Leon Spinks |
| Conservative | Dynamic |
| Past and present | Present and future |

[Professor's emphasis – pink text]: Reinforcement-Revitalization-Repositioning is a continuum.

Managing longitudinally – over a long period of time.

[Page 36]

### [Professor's emphasis – red text]: Managing Brands Longitudinally

[Professor's questions – red/pink text]:
> In which page do you see the whole chapter summarized as an exhibit?

> Managing brands longitudinally means?
> Longitudinal – over a period of time (Kantor panel of 80K households)
> Cross sectional – at a given point time (market share of biscuit brands in FY 2021)
>
> Reinforcing – brand is good, re-emphasize old values (Pears)
> Revitalizing – brand is getting fuddy-duddy, add new values
> [YouTube link] [1]
> Vicco Vajradanti new look evoked 6 million views

### [Professor's emphasis – red text]: Reinforcement – Brand Consistency

Consistency has two aspects:

i) **Consistency of Marketing Support**
   - [Professor's emphasis – pink text]: Managing an FMCG launch – spend crores for 10 years, not bother
   - Prakash Chauhan, Parle, Pepsi and Coke

ii) **Consistency of Associations**
   - [Professor's question – pink text]: How much consistency is desirable?
   - A brand will and must change incrementally all the time
   - Extended identity changes, core identity remains
   - [Professor's example – pink text]: Pears – skin care remains; winter care, wearing soap might change

Go through brand reinforcement strategies

Pepsodent has been repositioned 7 times.

Interviews – Weekender CNBC18 – to understand companies

> No big new brands globally or India in several years

### [Professor's emphasis – red text]: Reinforcement – Supporting Marketing Program

Marketing programs should support 2 types of innovations:
i) Performance related associations ([Professor's emphasis – pink text]: product focused, internal)
ii) Imagery related associations ([Professor's emphasis – pink text]: user focused, external)

#### Performance Related Associations

i) [Professor's emphasis – pink text]: Levi's has 21 innovative waterless denim finishing techniques [9]
   - These techniques use 96% less water
   - This is part of their "Profits through principles" initiative

ii) [Professor's emphasis – pink text]: Facewash as a product looks simple, no product is btw
   How many benefits does a facewash have?
   1. Nourishes Skin
   2. Unclogs Pores
   3. Removes Pollutants
   4. Antimicrobial Properties Cleanse Skin
   5. Controls Excessive Oil
   6. Rejuvenates Skin
   7. Anti-Inflammatory Properties Sooth Skin
   8. Removes Dead Cells
   9. Prevents Outbreaks and Recurrence of Acne
   10. Prevents Wrinkles
   - For each of these you can have a variant
   - Each of them will count as a different parameter of performance
   - [Professor's emphasis – pink text]: No wonder, human "face" is the most expensive real estate in the world – in terms of money spent per sq. cm. decking it up

iii) [Professor's emphasis – pink text]: "Mortein Smart" has a pre-programmed chip [10]
   This alternates between high and low optimizing mosquito vapor release

Interview by TT Jagannathan – Prestige

[Page 38]

### [Professor's emphasis – red text]: Reinforcement – Supporting Marketing Program (continued)

iv) A consumer durable illustration [8]
   [Professor's emphasis – pink text]:
   > Prestige pressure cookers had complaints of explosion, guess why?
   > Fake spare parts
   > In addition to the weight valve & safety plug…
   > …they improvised gasket release system (GRS)
   > If there is too much pressure, the gasket pushes out through the lid
   > Releases pressure and saves life, fortunately even fake gaskets do the job
   > This innovation saved TTK as a brand
   > From 1978 till date, they didn't have a single accident
   > From there to….. "Jo biwi se kare pyaar,…………."

[Professor's question – red text]: Your example of a performance related association?

Example: Gillette – now very functional

Image related associations – not about the product, it's about me.

All these are about the customer.

### 2.0 Revitalization

[Professor's emphasis – red text]:
> There are 2 paths to revitalization:
> i) Expanding breadth and depth brand awareness
> ii) Improve strength, favorability and uniqueness of brand associations

#### 2.1 Expanding Awareness

[Professor's questions – pink text]:
> Usually fading brands do not have "depth of awareness" issue, meaning?
> Recognition, recall are not an issue.
> Breadth of occasions means what?
> Range of purchase, usage occasions in which it is thought of.

1. Moov (pain specialist)
2. "Ek baam, teen kaam", which brand? (headache, cold, joint pain)
3. Dettol's 6 uses behind the bottle (cuts, shaving, nappies, surgery etc)
4. Colgate Total's 8 benefits listed on the pack

[Professor's emphasis – pink text]: You can see how brands expand the scope of usage (1 to 8), how far?

[Page 40]

### [Professor's emphasis – red text]: Revitalization

Two ways to increase consumption:

a) First is to increase the occasions, as shown in Moov, Zandu, Dettol

b) Find new uses for the product:
   1. [Professor's emphasis – pink text]: Hair dye used on buffaloes to make them look more black!
   2. Baby milk powder used for making sweets in Western India
   3. Washing for making lassi in lungars [18]

Read up the rest from the textbook.

---

### [Professor's emphasis – blue text]: What is a Global Brand?

**Define a global brand?**

> Brands whose positioning, advertising strategy, personality, look and feel are in most respects the same from one country to another …are global (Aaker and Joachimstahler, 1999)

> ["Global"] is defined as the multi-market reach of products that are perceived as the same brand worldwide both by consumers and internal constituents (Johansson and Ronkainen, 2005)

> Global brands are those that have global awareness, availability, acceptance, and desirability and are often found under the same name with consistent positioning, image, personality, look, and feel in major markets enabled by standardized and centrally coordinated marketing strategies and programs (Özsomer et al., 2012)

Please read the chapter.

**How global brands navigate their positioning:**
Example: Coke – story of happiness remains the same, but 4Ps change.

---

### [Professor's emphasis – blue text]: What Changes in a Global Brand

[Professor's emphasis – pink text]:
> A global brand has similar or same positioning all over the world.
> Then how do you accommodate changes every country asks for?
>
> Positioning is strategic, that remains.
> For e.g., if Coke's positioning is "happiness" that stays.
> But 4Ps are tactical, they can and do change.
> Positioning is strategic, 4Ps are more tactical.
>
> What changes is execution, meaning 4Ps:
> a) For instance, in India the product was probably made stronger – that appeals to the Indian palette
> b) Pricing sometimes tweaked through offerings like "Chota Coke"
> c) Indian distribution is more bottle driven & less dispenser driven
> d) Finally, having local communicating in a local idiom – Aamir Khan variously appeared as a Gorkha, Bihari…

**Global Product**

What called global branding is global marketing.

"Take any MNC or Indian brand from the Indian market."

Describe in one line each its i) functional ii) emotional iii) self-expressive benefits respectively (all the three must be for the same brand).

---

# Brand Management – Lecture Slides
## Comprehensive Notes (All 24 Pages)

---

## Page 1 – Digital Engagement Metrics

### Digital Engagement Metrics

**How to assess an e-commerce website's engagement?**

Aswath Damodaran says [2]:

How much are your users' worth depends on:

1. **How many users?**

2. **How engaged are they?**
   i.e. how many minutes or hours they spend on your website

3. **How loyal are they?**

4. **How sticky is the barrier to exiting the network?**
   Twitter customers don't stay for too long, they also exit.
   It is weak, because it is a snacking place.

---

## Page 2 – Summary: Search → Experience → Credence (Brand Transitions)

### Summary: Transitioning from Search (Friz) through Experience (Bank) to Credence (IIM)

**Brand as product**
- Product – Inseparability of product and manufacturer/service provider increases
- Price – Price estimation becomes difficult, therefore charging premium becomes easy
- Place – Location becomes less of an issue
- Promotion – Communication becomes less, experience becomes more important
- Physical evidence – ability to provide physical evidence decreases

**Brand as process**
- Process – becomes more intense, customized, anxiety causing

**Brand as organization**
- Culture – becomes more intensely knowledge and skill driven

**Brand as person**
- Disposition – from basic to complex problem solving, more thinking embedded in doing

**Brand as symbol**
- Semiotics – from mundane and celebratory to deep and reflective

---

## Page 3 – Brand as Process

### Brand as Process

**Why is process so important in services?**
- For replicability and scaling
- The process of interaction between a customer and a retail bank is usually clear
- The same is not true between a patient and a doctor or a client and a lawyer
- Much more complex, why?

How many things do we understand in the Union Budget?
- Swaminathan Aiyar, Mythili, Rathin Roy, Pronab Sen comments on the budget
- How many things like "basal ganglia" do you understand?
- It is the brain tissue that preserves habits [2]
- There is some amount of "anxiety" surrounding a doctor's or a lawyer's service
- Process of interaction of a customer and a credence good – "tense" and "intense"

---

## Page 5 – Product: Perceived Risk & Search/Experience/Credence Spectrum

### Product – Perceived Risk?

Perceived risk is a subjective assessment of what can go right or wrong.

**What are the different dimensions of perceived risk?**

There are 6 dimensions to perceived risk:
1. **Functional risk** – this laptop is not functioning
2. **Physical risk** – this laptop is damaged in delivery
3. **Financial risk** – this laptop ate up too much of my money
4. **Social risk** – this laptop's speed is embarrassing
5. **Psychological risk** – what if this laptop stops suddenly
6. **Time risk** – what if the laptop doesn't come to me on time (p188, Kotler and Keller)

---

### Diagram: Search Goods → Experience Goods → Credence Goods Spectrum

A horizontal spectrum diagram running from left ("Predominantly Product") to right ("Predominantly Service"). It shows three zones:

- **SEARCH GOODS** (left zone) – easily evaluated before purchase
- **EXPERIENCE GOODS** (middle zone) – evaluated after purchase/use
- **CREDENCE GOODS** (right zone) – difficult or impossible to evaluate even after use

Along the bottom axis, specific examples are placed at different points:

| Category | Product/Service | Ratio |
|---|---|---|
| FMCG | Soap, Shampoo | 100:0 |
| Durable | Car, Friz | 80:20 |
| B2C Service | Restaurant, Bank | 50:50 |
| B2B Service | Subcontracting, Software | 30:70 |
| Knowledge Goods | Consultancy, IIM | 20:80 |
| Spirituality | Vaishno Devi, Jaggi Vasudev | 0:100 |

The product-to-service ratio shifts from 100:0 (pure product) at the left to 0:100 (pure service/credence) at the right.

---

### SERVICE MARKETING MIX Table

| | Search Good | Experience Good | Credence Good |
|---|---|---|---|
| Typical e.g. | **Durables** – Scooter, Car, Drilling m/c | Airline, restaurant, movie in multiplex, bank, theme park, software | **Experts, Super specialists** – Doctor, Consultant, Lawyer, Specialty hospital |
| Product to Service ratio | 80:20 / 100:0? | 50:50 / 30:70? | 20:80 / 0:100? |

---

### Summary (Visual Experience, Brand Dominance, etc.)

- **Visual experience vs verbal prompt**
- b) **Brand Dominance** (the only brand recalled)
- c) **Brand Knowledge** (I know what the brand stands for)
- d) **Brand Opinion** (I have a view on the brand)

**Market share**
- Value or volume share
- Market share, profit share, category substitution

**Distribution**
- a) The percentage of stores carrying the brand
- b) The percentage of people who have access to it

---

## Page 7 – Summary: Perceived Value, Brand Personality, Awareness

### Summary

**Perceived value**
- a) Is this brand good value for money?
- b) Whether there is a reason to buy this brand over others?

**Brand personality**
- a) This brand has a personality (it is distinct)
- b) This brand is interesting
- c) Clear image of the person who uses the brand (self image?)
- Definitions from brand identity classes seem better
  - a) What happens when the brand becomes a person?
  - b) Sincerity, excitement, competence, sophistication, ruggedness

**Awareness**
- a) Recognition vs recall
- Heard of IIM? vs aided and unaided memory
- In store vs outside store?

---

## Page 8 – Summary: Price Premium, Satisfaction/Loyalty, Perceived Quality & Many Faces of Loyalty

### Summary

**Price premium**
- a) How much more can be paid for brand A compared to brand B?
- b) After what price drop of competitor will you shift to him?
- c) At this price will you buy Brand A or Brand B?

**Satisfaction/Loyalty**
- a) Would you buy it next time? (weak predictor)
- b) Is it one of the 2 or 3 brands you like? (Repertoire loyalty)
- c) Attitudinal loyalty more important than behavioral loyalty
  - Advocacy, search energy, readiness to wait – better indicators

**Perceived quality**
- a) High quality v/s low quality
- b) Consistent vs inconsistent
- c) Best v/s ok v/s worst
- Perceived quality is not objective, extrinsic + intrinsic cues

---

### Many Faces of Loyalty

Items marked red are strong indicators:

i) Customer Involvement
ii) Customer satisfaction
iii) Commitment
iv) Repeat purchase
v) Proportion of purchase
vi) Sequence of purchase
vii) Willingness to defect
viii) **Recommendation probability (advocacy)**
ix) **Willingness to pay more for a brand** (not all brands)
x) Money spent during a given period
xi) **Net Promoter score**

---

## Page 9 – Aaker's 10 – Price Premium

### Aaker's 10 – Price Premium

Suppose customers expect brand A to charge 20% less than brand B.
If it is priced 25% less, it has a negative price premium.
Despite being low priced, customers endow with equity.

**Example:**
- Will you buy Forest Essentials lip stick at Rs 1295 or Biotique Diva pout lipstick at Rs 800?
- Suppose your answer is Biotique.
- Then you ask: will you buy it for Rs 1000?
- Suppose the answer is yes.
- Next question: will you buy it at Rs 1100?
- If the answer is no, then…
- Biotique can go up to Rs 1000, or you could say:
- **It has a 200 Rs negative price premium**

---

## Page 10 – Identity vs Equity (Table) & Summary: Brand as Person, Symbol, Value Proposition

### Identity vs Equity

A two-column table comparing Brand Identity and Brand Equity:

| Brand Identity | Brand Equity |
|---|---|
| Feel | Measure |
| Qualitative | Quantitative |
| Strategy | Outcome |
| With company | With customer |
| Cause | Consequence |

---

### Summary

**Brand as person** – Jennifer Aaker's 5 dimensions:
- Sincerity, excitement, competence, sophistication, ruggedness

**Brand as symbol** – connecting to the customer semiotically:
- Logo, lettering, color, sound, tagline, mascot, jingle, program

**Box 3 – Value proposition**
- Aaker sees value proposition as a bundle of benefits
- Benefits can be rational or non-rational
- Rational benefits – functional, economic, use, exchange, instrumental
- Non-rational benefits – emotional, symbolic, social, intellectual, sensory
- How to create an experience through these benefits

---

## Page 11 – Summary: Aaker's Framework & Kapferer's Prism

### Summary

**Chose 3 boxes in the framework to elaborate:**
- Box 1 – Is this market space attractive for this brand?
- Box 2 – Is this brand strong?
- Box 3 – What does this brand offer?

**Aaker's 3 boxes** when you want a detailed understanding of brand identity.
**Kapferer's prism** when you want a quick and dirty, commonsensical understanding.

- Box 1 is similar to Porter's market attractiveness framework, left it
- Box 1 maps roughly to Kapferer's prism

**Box 2 – brand identity system:**
- Brand as product – how to connect with the customer through the product
  - Product scope, attributes, quality, uses, users, COO
- Brand as organization – how organizational values impact the brand

Two columns listing benefits:

**Rational:**
- Functional
- Economical
- Use

- (others)

**Non-rational:**
- Emotional
- Symbolic (self expressive)

- Aesthetic

---

### Summary – Definitions (Kapferer's Brand Identity Prism)

- **Physique** – Unique physical properties of your brand
- **Personality** – Convert your brand into a person
- **Culture** – Amul = inclusive, Reliance = aggressive, your brand = ?
- **Self image** – what sort of customer chooses your brand? ("what I see myself as")
- **Reflection** – what the customer wants to become through using your brand ("what I want others to see me as")
- **Relationship** – Friend? Brother? Big brother? Advisor? Uncle?

|---|---|---|
| **Self image** | I see myself as… / Internal motivator | What I am? / Pen picture of real user |
| **Reflection** | I want to be seen as… / External motivator | What I want to be? / Pen picture of ideal user |

## Page 14 – Customer Based Methods (Mental Maps, Y&R) & Keller Pyramid

### Customer Based Methods

Customer based methods could be:
i) **Quantitative** – Price premium, price above generics, market share/price equalization
ii) **Qualitative** – Mental maps and many others

**What are mental maps?**
- A mental map is a collection of all TOMA (Top-of-mind associations) of a brand (p326)
- Top 3 unaided associations of insurance for Indian customers?

---

### Mental Map, Core Association, Concept Map

How is the mental map (Fig 9-3 A) different from the brand concept map (Fig 9-4)?
- The brand concept map is not only an aggregation of individual responses…
- …but also shows the linkages between different associations
- In sum, all mental maps start with the individual map…
- ……and aggregate them to arrive at the brand map
- The method in practice is much more complicated as seen in Deborah Roedder John et al. article [14]
- Take a look at the Rolex brand audit between P346 and 354

---

### Customer Based Methods – Y & R

- Young and Rubicam asset evaluator (see Exhibit 1)
- What are D, R, E, K?
- **Brand strength** (Differentiation and Relevance – D, R)
- **Brand stature** (Esteem and Knowledge – E, K)
- That is a position to be aspired for by brands
- Brands move typically from new to niche to leadership…
- …to declining, eroding, confused

---

### Keller Pyramid (Customer-Based Brand Equity Pyramid)

A triangular pyramid diagram divided into four horizontal levels (bottom to top) with labels on the right side:

- **Bottom level (widest): SALIENCE** → 1. IDENTITY: "Who are you?"
- **Second level: PERFORMANCE (left) | IMAGERY (right)** → 2. MEANING: "What are you?"
- **Third level: JUDGMENTS (left) | FEELINGS (right)** → 3. RESPONSE: "What about you?"
- **Top level (narrowest): RESONANCE** → 4. RELATIONSHIPS: "What about you & me?"

The pyramid is labeled "FIGURE 1: CUSTOMER-BASED BRAND EQUITY PYRAMID."

---

## Page 15 – Customer Based Methods (Park-Srinivasan / Subjective) & Keller Pyramid (repeat)

### Customer Based Methods – Subjective Methods (Park-Srinivasan)

- Mask the brand name
- Ask the respondent to rate the 2-wheeler on: Appearance, mileage, maintenance, resale value, initial cost, etc.
- Tell him/her the brand name
- Get the ratings on the same attributes
- Difference between the two is…
- What is contributed by the brand name
- That is the measure of brand equity

**Difference between preference at brand level overall and …the [attribute-level] preference**

---

## Page 16 – Financial Value / Multiple Market Perspectives & Interbrand Method

### Financial Value – Multiple Market Perspectives

Financial value is termed as "market perspective" hereafter.
There are several ways to look at corporate financial valuation:

i) Book value of the company
ii) The valuation banks desire to lend to the company
iii) The value of the licenses held by the bank/company
   - Google retained Motorola's tech licenses and sold the Co.
iv) The value of partial sale of assets
v) The value in case of a forced sale
   - Yahoo and Microsoft
vi) The value in case of merger/takeover
   - Adani's acquisition of ACC and Gujarat Ambuja

---

### Market Based – Interbrand Method

- Interbrand method puts a Re. value on the brand, meaning?
- **Price at which the brand can be sold to some other company**
- Old method – post tax profits * (P/E) * k
- See file "Interbrand method – valuation"
- **Brand value depends on 4 components, p6 (2020 – method changed):**
  i) Brand strength (Macro factors – Brand – Tesla the brand)
  ii) Brand risk (based on history? < 1?)
  iii) Role of the brand (Micro factors – product – Tesla the car)
  iv) Economic profit (Financial analysis)

**Brand value = F(Brand strength, Role of the brand, Economic profit, Brand risk)**

**Brand Strength measures ability of the brand to create loyalty**

---

### Interbrand Method – Brand Strength (Part 1)

i) **Clarity**
   About brand's positioning within the company (Oppo & Vivo?)

ii) **Commitment**
   Willingness in the company to invest time, money and resources

iii) **Protection**
   Protecting the brand legally, its design & IP, scale, scope and geographical spread

iv) **Responsiveness**
   Ability to respond to market challenges and opportunities
   Ability to evolve and retain a sense of leadership

v) **Authenticity**
   The brand can deliver what it claims or even better them

---

### Interbrand Method – Brand Strength (Part 2)

vi) **Relevance**
   It fits with customer needs, relevant across demographics and geographies

vii) **Differentiation** (with competitors)
   Degree to which customers perceive the brand as being different

viii) **Consistency** (with itself)
   Degree to which brand experience is the same in all touchpoints

ix) **Presence**
   Degree to which a brand is rated positively by consumers, opinion makers in both traditional and social media

x) **Understanding**
   Brand is not just recognized
   Customers have an in-depth knowledge of its characteristics

---

## Page 17 – Co-Branding

### What is Co-Branding?

i) Co-branding allows brand assets to be leveraged with other brand names to derive a brand value that is greater than both the individual parts (Rao & Ruekert, 1994)

ii) Co-branding occurs when 2 or more brands come together for a joint offering in some form (Keller et al., 2011)

iii) Co-branding alliances are described as multiple brands forming a separate and unique product, with their success being reliant on producing positive consumer evaluations (Dickinson & Heath, 2008)

**What are the other words/phrases associated with co-branding?**
Strategic alliances, joint marketing, joint branding, joint promotion & composite brand extension

---

### 4 Levels of Co-Branding

What are the 4 levels of co-branding (CB)?

1. Reach/Awareness CB
2. Values endorsement CB
3. Ingredient CB
4. Complementary competence CB

What increases as we move up?
Seriousness of an engagement (implied by the slide)

---

### Value Endorsement Co-Brand

In value endorsement credibility and associations are shared.
More complementary values and a few supplementary values.

**What are complementary and supplementary values?**
- Complementary – get new values; supplementary – reinforce old values
- Complementary values – PoDs the brands borrow from each other
- Supplementary values – PoPs for the customer to see associations
- All the examples in the article have many complementary values

Example: **Ariel and Whirlpool** – "Whirlpool Recommends Ariel advert"

---

## Page 18 – Secondary Associations, Brand Extension (Good & Ugly)

### Summary: Secondary Associations

**Prerequisite for using secondary associations?**
Awareness, Knowledge & its meaningfulness and transferability

- **Meaningful** – relevant to the brand?
- **Transferability** – does the association add something desirable?

**Secondary associations are leveraged in 8 ways as follows:**

i) **Within Company** (through branding strategies)
   Brand extensions, siblings, corporate associations

ii) **Countries** (through country-of-origin)
   Wine famous because it hails from Champagne in France

iii) **Channels** (through type of channel used)
   Unlike the West, organized retail is not well developed in India

iv) **Other brands** (through co-branding)
   a) Reach b) Value endorsement c) Ingredient d) Complementary comp.

v) **Characters** (through licensing)
   Don't go overboard licensing

vi) **Spokespersons** (through endorsement)
   Pluses and minuses

vii) **Events** (through channel strategy)
   Google Bus (self) or Kumbh Mela (others)

viii) **Third Party sources** (awards)
   Deming Prize (Tata Steel, Rane Brake, SRF, several TVS group companies)

ix) **Influencers** (micro-celebrities)
   Careful, cut both ways

---

### Brand Extension – Good (Summary)

**Brand associations**
7 ways in which a strong brand helps with associations

**Quality associations**
Park Avenue dressing seen as good, therefore grooming works

**Awareness**
Maruti's awareness high, thus helps in building Nexa

**Trial purchase encouraged by brand extension**
L&T has credibility, extensions likely to do well in B2B

**Enhancement of core identity (more good)**

---

## Page 19 – Brand Extensions (Ugly, Evaluation Grid, Principles)

### Brand Extensions – Ugly (Summary)

**Parent's brand name/quality image is damaged**
Not much evidence in India (Maruti, L&T)

**Undesirable associations created**
ITC, UB, CMC

**Existing associations weakened**
What does the brand stand for? Cloth line simile

**Disaster occurs**
ADAG's reckless diversification

**Brand franchise is cannibalized**
Design for 20% cannibalization, overall volumes should go up
Better I cannibalize than allow competitors to cannibalize

---

**What is strength of the grid?**
Fairly detailed

**Any pointers missed?**
Managing 4Ps of the extension (how is this pointer different?)

**Can we find a better heading for external aspects?**
Growing market? Competitor strength? Easy to copy?
Porter – Market attractiveness (plus value chain)

**Focus on 6 broad issues in brand extension:**
i) Market attractiveness
ii) Brand value consistency
iii) Organizational strengths
iv) Internal-external fit
v) Customers
vi) Managing 4Ps
