| | Local | Global | Standard |
|---|---|---|---|
| Product variety | One per country (Many) | Basic Platforms (A few) — *Ohmae* | Same product (one) — *Levitt* |

Are some products more globalizable than others? Why?

| Market | | |
|---|---|---|
| | Glass syringe | Disposable [9] syringe |
| | 2 wheeler lubricant | Ship lubricant |

---

### Global Pricing [Page 64]

**Global pricing**

i) Global brands are priced at a premium usually
But premium not possible in every country, what to do then?
You can be premium compared to local brands
But you may not get the premium you want
Almost all multinational brands dropped their price in India
Several MNC car brands failed in India due to this reason

ii) Can you set different prices in different countries?
Price corridor, no country goes beyond the band

iii) Different products/brands for different countries, e.g?
Crest not there in India, but available in the West

iv) Developing new products ground up, "Reverse innovation"
GE Mac ECG in India at 1000$ (one half of US cost) [7]

---

### Global Communication – When Standardized? [Page 65]

**Global communication – when standardized?**

i) When a standard ad works?
Ad is translation, everything other than language is the same
P&G ads in India in early 2000s had S.E. faces!

ii) Simple tag line ("The real thing")

iii) Similar concerns (God, I am getting old!)

iv) Same ad agency (rationalization vs localization)

v) What is this ad an example of?
https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=Bczjj6hHjKA
DHL Ad bird and cat
https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=7Qe7LN3dzS0
DhI TV spots bird vs cat
Culture free ads, hence. *Ref: Tepstra and Sarathy, p455*

---

### Global Communication – When Customized? (Part 1) [Page 66]

**Global communication – when customized?**

a) Single biggest factor that forces customization?
Language difference – "Nothing sucks like an Electrolux" [8]
How to ensure this doesn't happen?
Retranslate!

b) Govt controls – Pepsi's ad in Middle East
3 types of ads
Export ads, prototype ads, guideline ads [4]
i) Export ads (standardized) – French fmcg bottle in French
Distinction French cosmetics have that other countries don't?
They are premium simply because they are French
ii) Prototype ads (hybrid) – principles laid down, compliance depends on local conditions
iii) Guideline ads (localized) – only guidelines given, ad developed locally

---

### Global Communication – When Customized? (Part 2) [Page 67]

**Global communication – when customized?**

c) Economic differences (how much opulence to show in ads)

d) Media Availability (internet availability – online ads)

e) Local distributors (favoring a certain kind of communication)

f) Consumer taste/attitudes *(single biggest stumbling block?)*
i) Volkswagen ad in Italy – this car is different [3]
Showcased as a black sheep in a herd of normal ones
In Italy the color stands for independence, so msg goes through
In other countries black sheep means something else!

ii) Kit Kat is positioned in UK as ("have a break, have a Kitkat)
In Norway it is positioned as *Kvikk Lunsj* ("quick lunch")

---

### Global Distribution [Pages 68–69]

**Global distribution**

i) US (85%) and UK (80%) retailing is organized [5]
India is splintered like many developing countries (9%)
How many shops in India stock FMCG goods?
1 crore

ii) In US Starbucks is a hangout
In China, if you don't drink coffee after a while… asked to leave!

iii) Japan's distribution is very peculiar and complicated
Small retailers are protected by law
Distribution is also many layered, price paid by customers is high
Small stores a/c for 59% food sales in Japan (37% in US) [6]

iv) In Africa Nestle distributes by foot, by bike, by taxi [6]

---

*(Page 69)*

v) In UK, Tesco likely to provide retail banking in its stores
vi) In Brazil, Carrefour reduced its SKUs
vii) Morocco & Italy use specialty stores with narrow product lines
In Finland it is the opposite
viii) Mitsukoshi in Japan attracts 1,00,000 customers a day
Ibadan market in Nigeria has 3000 one or 2 person stalls
ix) In South Africa 1000 stores control 60% of grocery trade
The other 40% is spread over 30000 stores, single stick sales!

Conclusions?
Distribution is always local, much as you may try to std.ize it

---

### Global Branding – Conclusion [Page 70]

**Conclusion**

How to manage a global brand? want to summarize?

i) Positioning is same or similar all over the world
ii) Globalizing product – how to platformize?
ii) Globalizing price – Managing the price corridor
iii) Globalizing promotion – prototype ads, principles laid down
iv) Globalizing place – localize distribution

## SESSION: RETAIL BRANDING

Summarize the diagram in a couple of lines?

|---|---|

Du Pont analysis of a retail chain

Summarize the article in a couple of lines?
BO leads to PA, PA leads to performance, true?

### Distinctiveness [Pages 72–73]

**Distinctiveness**

3 broad types of retail experience (Pecoraro & Uusitalo, 2014)
i) Pragmatic store ii) Romantic store iii) Modern store

**Pragmatic store** is like a library (Metro)
It is functional, with virtually no help, emotionally neutral
uniform graphics, ordinary furniture, watchword – "figure out"

**Romantic store** is like grandmother's house (Fab India)
It is symbolic, a few friendly staff, emotionally loaded
Colorful, eco-friendly, traditional furniture, watchword – "celebrate"

**Modern store** is like a health resort (my e.g.) (Decathlon)
It is both functional and symbolic, professional staff
Minimalist graphics, modern furniture, watchword – "add value"

It basically gets 80% of its revenue offline [Q]
It earns more revenue than Nike, Adidas and Reebok together in India
Their affordable products and disrupted unorganized market
Endowment effect – if you touch and feel, you feel like an owner…
…and become one
37% of D's customers are walk by, JLA buyers
Their employees are sports enthusiasts who do not hard sell
Products made within the country, no fancy endorsements
Products technically, atleast incrementally better, 50 gm hiking T-shirt
A camp tent that can be put up in 2 sec, unlike 20 min, 10 yrs back

How a sweet shop in Bombay is trying to differentiate

### Retail Branding – Summary [Page 80]

**Retail branding – summary**

Three components to retail branding (Fig 1)
i) Brand orientation ii) Pos. advantage iii) Organizational performance
The above three components have 9 sub-components
How do we know the definitions of components and sub-components?
Check Table 4

Brand orientation – what the brand can do for the store? (strategic)
Positional advantage – What the store can do for the brand? (tactical)

Org. performance = financial performance + strategic effectiveness
Financial performance is short term
Strategic effectiveness is long term
Financial performance = RoI, RoA, Gross Margins, shop level metrics
Strategic performance = Financial objectives + Strategic objectives
Both objectives are defined from long term point of view

## SESSION: CULT BRANDING

### Glossary – Cult Branding Terms (Part 1) [Pages 81–82]

**Glossary**

New words encountered in the cult branding article?
We will get them out of the way
Then I will tell you the big story of cult branding

**i) Boundaries?**
Membership of a cult brand is restricted among the followers, eg?
Only those who read all Harry Potter books are called Potterheads

**ii) Ideology**
A belief strongly held, eg?
A&F is for beautiful people

**iii) Spotlight?**
It conducts dramatic events to hold your attention, eg?

*(Page 82 adds:)*
Woodstock for Rock, Madras Music Academy for Carnatic Cla[ssic music] [text cut off]

---

### Glossary – Cult Branding Terms (Part 2) [Page 83]

**Glossary**

**iv) Lovebombing?**
Once you are inside the fold its followers shower love on you, eg?
H.O.G. and how they help each other

**v) Pyramid?**
Among the followers all are equal, but some are more equal, eg?
Sudhir Choudhary gives 1000 litchis to Sachin every year
Sachin sponsors his tickets for every match of his

**vi) In-the-know MARCOMM?**
Communication is scarce, deliberate and carefully targeted, eg?
Warren Buffet writes annually one letter to his shareholders
It is eagerly awaited and goes viral almost as soon as it is aired
What is the price of one share of Berkshire Hathway?
$702,250.00 – just one share! (NYSE, after close, Nov 13, 5:05:01)

---

### Glossary – Cult Branding Terms (Part 3) [Pages 84–85]

**Glossary**

**vii) Turn tables?**
If something goes wrong, it is always the customer's mistake…
Not the brand's!, eg?
Starbucks suddenly closes down, says…
"We are training to make perfect coffee, wait…."
And customers wait
Bullet gets you to wait for the next vehicle and you do

**viii) Big Brother?**
There is always some enemy waiting to malign the cult…
Or so it is said, eg?
How dare you blame Narendra Modi?
Demonetization? GST botch, 6 year stagnant exports

*(Page 85 – blurry version adds:)*
Maybe he forgot us…(migrants who walked back)

---

### 5 Stages of a Cult Brand [Page 86]

**5 Stages of a cult brand**
i) Brand consideration
ii) Brand affiliation
iii) Brand admiration
iv) Brand adoration
v) Brand obsession

What I am showing you is what I learnt after reading 115 articles

---

### Stage 1: Brand Consideration [Pages 87–88]

**1. Brand consideration**

What in your view is step 1 for a cult brand?
The brand has "extreme differentiation" (Modi, Buddha, IIT)
The brand displays a very different DNA
2 distinct markers of cult brand in "brand consideration"
**Without these 2, the brand can never grow into a cult brand…**
**…no matter what it does later**

i) Extreme differentiation
ii) Unafraid of taking risks

*(Page 88 adds:)*
Beatles performed in Shea stadium ….
…though it was unsuitable for music, it was a sell out!
iii) Sells a lifestyle and not a product
Who is Barbara Millicent Williams?
Barbie not just a doll
It is w[hat you wan]t to be, when you grow up [text cut off]

---

### Stage 2: Brand Affiliation [Page 89]

**2. Brand affiliation**

**ii) Formation of brand community**
Brand Community is *"a specialized, non-geographically bound community"* (Muniz and O'Guinn, 2001)
It has "influentials" (donors of opinion) who communicate and "susceptibles" (receivers of opinion) (Aral & Walker, 2012)
This leads to word-of-mouth communication

**iii) Brand communication**
A brand should communicate its ideology and identity
In the initial stages the company takes lead in forming groups
"My Starbucks Idea" networks with customers to source ideas from them for the brand
H.O.G. has a million Harley Davidson members

---

### Stage 3: Brand Admiration [Page 90]

**3. Brand admiration**

2 constituents of brand admiration
i) Generation of positive word-of-mouth (wom)
ii) Followers becomes fans

**i) What triggers positive wom, getting the message viral?**
The message needs to be
a) Useful/ perceived to be useful (heart attack!)
b) Teaser (are you in a hurry, see this list of 35 things?)
c) Fun (Ice bucket challenge, Kolavari)

---

### Stage 4: Brand Adoration [Page 91]

**4. Brand adoration**

Brand adoration stage is marked by the following
a) Customers spend time, money, energy for the brand (attachment)
b) They openly flaunt consumption of the brand

**i) Brand community becomes sub-culture**
A sub-culture is a set of people strongly focused on a matter of common interest, in this case the brand (Schouten & McAlexander, 1993)

**ii) Brand language. E.g.?**
Group members develop a private language of their own
i) IIMC students call themselves "Jokars"

---

### Stage 5: Brand Obsession [Pages 92–93]

**5. Brand obsession?**

I) Obsession is a passionate pursuit that doesn't care about results
It is characterized by
i) an unconditional pursuit
ii) uncharacteristic passion
iii) consumption as a quasi-religious experience

II) This obsession manifests itself as memorabilia, e.g.?
i) building shrines (221 B Baker's Street)
ii) Going on pilgrimages (Oscar ramp walk)
iii) designing tightly defined rituals (licking cream of Oreo biscuits)
iv) celebrating brand myths (Marlboro and number of cowboys)

*(Page 93 – clearer version adds:)*
v) making consumption a sacred act (HD Manual – Forbes 2001 art.)
vi) Fans erected a 30ft-tall cut-out of Lionel Messi in a river in K'kodo
vii) Strange, but true. A bunch of people married their Mazda Miatas [1]

---

### Cult Brand vs Good Brand (Part 1) [Pages 94–95]

**Cult brand vs good brand**

Are cult brands a more advanced form of a good brand?
Or are they basically different?

| Good brand | Cult brand |
|---|---|
| Offers more functional benefits | Offers more self-expressive and emotional benefits |
| First stage | Final stage |
| Loyalty, Trust | Obsession |
| Word of mouth | Evangelism, Fanaticism |
| Customer [cut off] | Owner [cut off] |

*(Page 94 is a blurry version of this slide; page 95 is the clearer version.)*

---

### Cult Brand vs Good Brand (Part 2) [Pages 96–97]

**Cult brand vs good brand**

*(Page 96 – extended comparison table)*

| Good brand | Cult brand |
|---|---|
| Good Connect | Separation anxiety |
| Happy, satisfied | Proud, Uplifted, Transcendent |
| Fans | Fanatics |
| Secular rituals | Sacred rituals |
| Community | Sub culture, Tribalism |
| Delightful experience | Transformative experience |
| Customers love it | Customers own it |

---

*(Page 97)*

**Cult brand vs good brand**

| Good brand | Cult brand |
|---|---|
| Customers of brand | Trustees/guardians of the brand |
| Strong engagement with brand | Deep engagement with brand |
| Typical eg? Amazon | Typical eg? Harley Davidson |

Good brand =
Differentiated offering + Distinct positioning + Loyalty

Cult brand =
Maverick offering + Provocative positioning + Obsession

---

### Cult Brand vs Good Brand (Final) [Page 98 / Page 144]

**Cult brand vs good brand**

Cult brand =
Reputed/well-known/good brand + Faith/Obsession/Devotion/fanaticism

Sacralization of a good brand is a must for it to become a cult brand

In sum
A cult brand is a secular substitute for a religious symbol

## [SENIOR EXAM FEEDBACK]
Source: senior_feedback.md
---
The BM paper will be open internet, I think. Honestly, there isn’t much use in studying; even I hadn’t prepared that much. What really helps is understanding the kind of questions he might ask and preparing accordingly. It will be a long paper, around 3 hours, but you probably won’t finish it. I didn’t even complete half of it, as far as I remember.

The questions are usually theoretical but need to be explained using egs. Like - Explain XYZ with the help of an eg. He also expects references and citations in the paper...you have to write the link, page number, etc., which takes a lot of time. What helped me was preparing a list of unique brand egs in advance and taking that with me. I had also uploaded articles, slides, and the textbook on NotebookLM. When a question came, I first used it to understand the theory. For eg, if the concept was brand resonance, I would first understand the concept there and then I would look at my list of egs and see which one could fit. After selecting one, I would find supporting evidence online, such as an article discussing that example, and cite it.

The examples were the most helpful part, because it’s hard to think of good ones during the exam. Also, he doesn’t like common examples like Amul or Maggi, and some examples are explicitly banned, so you need to know which ones you cannot use. So I would strongly suggest taking a well-prepared list of examples.

Studying the concepts in depth beforehand isn’t very useful because you don’t know what he will ask. But take the textbook with you. The index or keyword list at the back can help...if NotebookLM doesn’t give you an answer, you can find where the keyword appears in the book, read that section to understand the concept, and then attach an eg.

---
## [KAPFERER BRAND IDENTITY PRISM]
Source: knowledge_base/reading_materials/brand_identity_prism.md
---
# Brand Identity Prism

**Author:** Dr. Y. L. R. Moorthi, Professor (Marketing), Indian Institute of Management, Bangalore
**Based on:** Jean Noel Kapferer's article on the brand identity prism
**Assistance:** Ms. Smriti Subramanyan, FPM student 2016
**Original filename:** Brand Identity Prism.pdf

---

[Page 1]

## BRAND IDENTITY PRISM

Kapferer's model of the brand identity prism consists of the following six faces:
1. Physique
2. Personality
3. Culture
4. Self-Image
5. Reflection
6. Relationship

It contends that any brand can be expressed as a sum of these 6 faces. The six faces collectively configure a brand and help forge a bond with the customer.

### The Prism Diagram

The six faces are arranged as a hexagon (prism shape):
- Top-left: **Physique**
- Top-right: **Personality**
- Middle-left: **Relationship**
- Middle-right: **Culture**
- Bottom-left: **Reflection**
- Bottom-right: **Self-image**

This article concisely explains the six faces of the prism with examples.
(Note - It is best read in conjunction with watching the ads mentioned in the references).

---

[Page 1–2]

## 1. Physique

The distinct, salient or characteristic physical attributes of a brand constitute its physique. Physique is the collection of features evoked in a consumer's mind when the brand is mentioned. Consider the distinct physical attributes of the following brands:
- **KitKat** chocolate has fingers. The brand communication highlights how the fingers can be used to consume the product [1].
- **Five Star**, a competitor chocolate, looks very different — it is a rectangular bar.
- **Toblerone** has a unique, serrated, mountain range-like shape [2].
- **Doy baby soaps** have distinct animal shapes that set them apart from other soaps [3].
- **Tata Nano** has a unique compact form [4].
- **Maruti Wagon-R** boasts a signature "tall boy" design [5].
- **Fevi-quick**, the commonly used super-glue, has a small package — a screwing tube with a distinctly sealed cap.

Physique, thus, is the summation of the physical features that stand out.

Besides, there are also **emerging features**. Some features may not be prominent now but might be emerging — these are also treated as part of physique. For instance, a cell phone was merely an instrument to make and receive calls. Its physique today is way beyond that: it is a camera, a search engine, a payment mechanism and more. It graduated from a calling device to a personal assistant. In future it might have artificial intelligence or even become a body implant. This is the emerging aspect of the physique.

---

[Page 2–3]

## 2. Personality

Brand personality is looking at a brand as a human being. It answers the question: "What happens when this brand becomes a person? How does s/he look?" This is achieved by converting the attributes of the brand into equivalent attributes of an associated human form.

Examples:
- **Dettol** could be seen as a nurse who enforces measures for the prevention of infections [6].
- **Bosch's** automotive components are produced to perfection — Bosch can be seen as a perfectionist [7].
- **Ambuja cement's** communication suggests the brand is a man with well-built strong arms who protects your asset [8].

Some brands use celebrities for endorsement. The celebrities or brand ambassadors should reflect the personality of the brand. However, celebrities should not be confused with brand personality. Different brands endorsed by a celebrity might be using different aspects of his/her disposition.

Example — **Sachin Tendulkar**:
- **Pepsi** uses the celebrity aspect of his to play on the fun quotient.
- **Adidas shoes** use the sportsman aspect [9].
Thus, while in one case he represents celebration and frolic, in the other he stands for discipline and achievement. So celebrity is quite distinct from the brand's personality.

**Advantages and pitfalls of celebrities:**
A celebrity does give instant recognition to a brand. Consumers typically go through the **AIDA (Awareness, Interest, Desire, and Action)** cycle in their purchase. A celebrity increases "awareness" and stirs up "interest" or "desire." But there is "action" (purchase) only when the communication is backed by an effective marketing program. An important fact: a handful of celebrities endorse most of the brands in India. Therefore celebrities can cause confusion to the customer because of their association with multiple brands. They should be used judiciously.

**Brand personality vs. user personality:**
Brand personality should not be confused with the user's personality. The user characteristics might be quite distinct from personality (though they sometimes overlap).
- IBM hardware users are young as well as old. But the brand IBM itself is seen as mature and middle-aged.
- Apple users might be young or old, but Apple itself is seen as young.

Therefore user profile (characteristics of the target segment) and brand personality are two different entities.

---

[Page 3–4]

## 3. Culture

Culture is the set of values and principles that hark back to the roots of the brand. Brands should therefore be representatives of their culture.

There are **3 distinct ways** in which culture impacts brands:
1. Through the brand's values directly
2. Through the parent company's culture
3. Through the values associated with the brand's country-of-origin

### i) Culture and Brand Values

What are the values of **Pepsi**? Rebellion and adolescence. These values will probably not change — they are difficult to change. However there are secondary values (humor, celebration, and funkiness). While funkiness (close to rebellion) is part of Pepsi's long-standing culture, humor and celebration are part of the brand Pepsi only when they are whacky, irreverent or impertinent. The invariant value is funky. The values that could moderate or change may be celebration (which in fact belongs more to Coke) and humor.

**Axe** — sensuality ("the Axe effect") [10] is central to the brand. Axe may not want to touch that value. Other values like humor could change.

### ii) Culture and Company

Culture may also stem from the company. The organization places bounds on what a brand can do or cannot do. For instance, **ITC** is in cigarettes business but it doesn't show women smoking in its communication. None of their brands aim that segment. Therefore a competitor like GTC can launch a woman's cigarette (as they did with Ms. — the brand failed though). But even if the segment was profitable, ITC would probably not launch it. ITC's culture therefore implicitly limits all its brands.

On the positive side: **Godrej Aer** is a car deodorant brand. The name Aer when backed by Godrej gives added confidence to the customer — Godrej is a long-standing brand with a footprint in consumer products (Cinthol), appliances (refrigerators) and services (Godrej properties). The values of the corporation can thus bring to bear a positive impact on the brand.

**Toyota's** "customer orientation" impacts **Lexus** even if the brand is not endorsed by the parent. Toyota is seen to have a better connect with customers than many competitors. Therefore even when a parent does not directly endorse a brand, its culture could impact it.

### iii) Culture and Country-of-Origin

A third axis of culture is country of origin. Brands tend to derive some of their culture from their home country:
- **German brands** are seen as technologically superior and robust.
- **Korean brands** are seen as aggressive and competent.
- **India** is seen as positive in IT services but not in several other categories. That is why **Hidesign**, the Indian fashion accessory brand, plays down the India connection.

Historical context: In the 1950s Japanese products were seen as shoddy. They painfully reinvented themselves to build a reputation in durables from cars to cameras. Korea copied Japan's obsession for product quality and compulsive work culture — they too experienced a steady learning curve. Korean brands like Komatsu didn't sell even in Korea initially; Samsung microwave ovens didn't sell in Korea because the market for such appliances was small — they were forced to seek foreign markets. Today both Korean and Japanese brands are well established and accorded respect.

The Chinese began their efforts in the late 1970s. By 2000 almost every product that costs less than $10 was a Chinese product. They acquired a reputation for mass production on an unprecedented scale — still making their transition from products to brands. However, some of their brands like **Huawei** are globally seen as competent today.

Countries acquire a reputation for quality (or lack of it), competitiveness or competence over time. These perceptions directly impact their home-grown brands in global markets. Companies often seek to associate their brands with the positive values of their nation, province or place of origin:
- **Mysore Sandal soap** tracks itself back to the sandalwood fragrance from the forests of Mysore.
- **Leo Coffee** draws its inspiration from the South Indian tradition of "perfect" filter coffee [11].
- **Patanjali's** early success is rooted in its emphasis of Indian culture — drawing from the principles followed by yoga masters, Patanjali fuses tradition and convenience to deliver affordable ayurvedic products.
- **Amul** (Anand Milk Producers' Union), a successful farmer's cooperative that produces several dairy products, lays claim for being the "taste of India" [12].

Note: the cultural cues in the above cases are respectively sub-regional (Mysore Sandal, Karnataka), regional (Leo, South India) and national (Patanjali and Amul, India).

Of the three sources of culture, a brand's values are the closest to its own culture. The company's values come next, followed by the countries. Each of them impacts the identity of a brand directly or indirectly.

---

[Page 5]

## 4. Self-Image

Kapferer defines self-image as the picture the customer has of himself when he consumes the brand. That is, the consumption of the brand transforms the image of the customer in his own eyes. For instance, if you buy an Apple phone it might push up your self-esteem — you see yourself as smart, sophisticated and contemporary. But then this is what you want others to think about you as well (that aspect is defined by Kapferer as "reflection" in the neighboring face). Therefore self-image probably needs a different definition.

Besides, there are also brands which do not necessarily improve your view of yourself post consumption. For instance, buying a low cost detergent powder or travelling by a budget airline doesn't necessarily get one to "feel" different. They are convenient in a matter-of-fact way. This is true of most functional products. Kapferer's definition of self-image thus unwittingly causes confusion with the other face: reflection.

**Alternative definition:** Self-image can be defined as the profile of the "current user." This brings clarity to the definition.

Examples:
- The current user of a fashion brand like **Zara or Prada** might indeed be seeking esteem in his/her own eyes.
- A user of **Bullet** might see himself as "macho." But the more interesting part is presently he may or may not be "macho" himself — this is why college goers are now buying Bullet (in the hope of feeling "macho").
- **Santoor soap's** user would like to see herself as an empowered person [13]. But her user profile tells us she is an Andhra/Telangana/Karnataka mother, stretched for time and money, saddled with children's home work and waiting to get liberated.
- **Complan's** customer (child) might want to grow tall — but presently s/he is short.
- **Stayfree** sanitary napkin user wants emancipation from public embarrassment during the cycle — but currently she is a worried person.

In other words, **the best way to understand self-image is to interpret it as the profile of the target segment.** It describes the characteristics of the present user.

---

[Page 5–6]

## 5. Reflection

If self-image is what the customer **is**, reflection is what s/he **wants to be**. Therefore "macho" is a Bullet buying collegian's reflection. Achiever and professional is the reflection of the overburdened mother who is a Santoor user. A metaphor should probably explain: **if self-image is pupa, reflection is butterfly.**

As mentioned already, target consumers may be markedly different from their reflection. But reflected image is their goal — that motivates them to buy the brand.

Examples:
- **Aachi masala** transforms the housewife into an expert cook, because Aachi has the ingredients that make food perfect [14].
- **Mangaldeep agarbatti** (incense stick) is the ideal accessory to prayer — it helps you forget everything else and concentrate on God [15].
- **Close Up toothpaste** is what endows you with "social confidence" — its fragrance helps you get close to the opposite gender.
- Drinking **Bournvita** makes a kid brainy [16].
- **Nescafe** is easy to make — it is instant coffee. Making it isn't laborious like making tea. So it is smart people's choice [17].

In all the above cases, the brand promises to transform the consumer (Close Up) or the consumption experience (Mangaldeep Agarbatti). Some brands promise to free you from drudgery (Aachi masala, Nescafe). Others transform you to smart (Bournvita) or likeable (Close Up). In all cases they empower you in one way or another.

**Reflection for the user is therefore "what s/he wants to become through using the brand."**

---

[Page 6–7]

## 6. Relationship

Kapferer defines a brand essentially as a relationship. Two of his examples seem apt: **Moulinex**, the kitchen appliance maker, is seen as a "woman's friend." Similarly **Laughing Cow** the cheese brand is like a mother.

A simple way of understanding relationship: To arrive at brand personality, we converted the brand into a person. Suppose we ask the question: how is this person related to the customer? Is s/he a friend, a brother, a protector, an assistant, an advisor or an elderly relative? This query leads us to the meaning of "relationship."

Examples:
- **Cello** (household appliances, furniture, glassware, thermalware) is a lifelong companion [18].
- **Johnson baby soap** is the mother herself — she is all care and love, soft on the baby's skin [19].
- **Kotak bank** is an omnipresent friend — you will see a Kotak bank wherever you go ("Kona Kona me Kotak" — Kotak in every corner) [20].
- **Ponds** is your beauty consultant — she is the to-go person for all your skin care needs. She advises you on everything from clearing facial spots to getting rid of pimples.

---

[Page 7]

## Summary Table: The 6 Faces of Brand Identity

| FACE | SUMMARY | ILLUSTRATION |
|------|---------|--------------|
| **Physique** | Unique physical attributes of the brand | KitKat – finger shaped slab; Toblerone – mountain shaped serrated bar; Doy (baby soap) – animal shaped soap; Fevi-quick – unique packaging with a dispenser |
| **Personality** | Brand personality converts the brand into a human being | Dettol – Doctor; Bosch – Perfectionist engineer; Ambuja Cement – Well-built man with exceptionally strong arms |
| **Culture** | Culture is the set of values that hold the genes of the brand | Pepsi – Rebellion; Axe – sensuality; Patanjali – Traditional Indian; BRU coffee – South Indian |
| **Self-Image** | Who is the typical customer for this brand? | Johnson & Johnson – baby; Complan – growing children; Stayfree – worried woman |
| **Reflection** | What the customer wants to become through using the brand | Aachi masala – makes you smart cook; Close-Up toothpaste – gets you close to the opposite gender; Bournvita – makes you a brainy kid; Nescafe – makes you a smart person |
| **Relationship** | The handshake between the brand and the customer | Cello – lifelong companion; Johnson's baby soap – loving mother; Kotak Bank – omnipresent friend |

---

[Page 8]

## Discussion Questions

i. Does everything physical become part of physique?
ii. When can you be sure you have understood a brand's personality well?
iii. How do you know if a brand characteristic is part of culture or not?
iv. Draw the brand identity prism for a: i) consumer product, ii) consumer durable, iii) a service, iv) an IT product/company, v) a b2b product/company.

---

[Page 8–9]

## [AAKER BRAND IDENTITY PLANNING MODEL]

# Brand Identity Planning Model

**Author:** Dr. Y. L. R. Moorthi, Prof (Marketing), Indian Institute of Management, Bangalore
**Assistance:** Ms. Smrithi Subramanyan, FPM student 2016 batch
**Publication:** IIM Bangalore (working paper / teaching note)
**Year:** circa 2016
**Original Filename:** BRAND IDENTITY PLANNING MODEL.pdf

---

## Overview

This article explains the Brand Identity Planning Model, a framework that guides strategic brand analysis and brand identity construction. The model is structured around three major components: Strategic Brand Analysis, the Brand Identity System, and Brand Identity Implementation. The final output is the Brand Position, which is communicated through Brand-Building Programs and tracked over time.

---

## FIGURE 1 — Brand Identity Planning Model

The model is a vertically integrated diagram with the following layers, flowing from top to bottom:

### Layer 1: STRATEGIC BRAND ANALYSIS
A top-level box divided into three columns:

| Customer Analysis | Competitor Analysis | Self-Analysis |
|---|---|---|
| Trends | Brand image/identity | Existing brand image |
| Motivation | Strengths strategies | Brand heritage |
| Unmet needs | Vulnerabilities | Strengths/strategies |
| Segmentation | Positioning | Organization values |

### Layer 2: BRAND IDENTITY SYSTEM — BRAND IDENTITY

A large box containing three concentric ellipses (innermost to outermost):
- **Brand Essence** (innermost)
- **Core** (middle ring)
- **Extended** (outer ring)

Below the ellipses, four pillars extend downward:

**Brand as Product** (items 1–6):
1. Product scope
2. Product attributes
3. Quality/Value
4. Uses
5. Users
6. Country of origin

**Brand as Organization** (items 7–8):
7. Organization attributes (e.g., innovation, consumer concern, trustworthy)
8. Local versus global

**Brand as Person** (items 9–10):
9. Personality (e.g., genuine, energetic, rugged)
10. Customer/brand relationships

**Brand as Symbol** (items 11–12):
11. Visual image and metaphors
12. Brand heritage

### Layer 3: VALUE PROPOSITION and CREDIBILITY

Two boxes side by side:

**VALUE PROPOSITION** (left):
- Functional benefits
- Emotional benefits
- Self-expressive benefits

**CREDIBILITY** (right):
- Support other brands

### Layer 4: RELATIONS

A horizontal bar labeled RELATIONS, receiving arrows from both the Value Proposition and Credibility boxes.

### Layer 5: BRAND IDENTITY IMPLEMENTATION SYSTEM

### Layer 6: BRAND IDENTITY ELABORATION

### Layer 7: BRAND POSITION

> The part of the brand identity and value proposition that is to be actively communicated to the target audience

### Layer 8: BRAND-BUILDING PROGRAMS

### Layer 9: TRACKING

---

[Page 1]

## 1.0 Strategic Brand Analysis

Strategic brand analysis is the first step. The brand manager needs to understand three entities: customers, competitors, and the brand itself (self-analysis).

### 1.1 Customer Analysis

#### 1.1.1 Trends
Trends refer to changing consumption patterns in a market. Examples include the shift in automobile buying from diesel cars to petrol/electric vehicles. In the Indian context, following a Supreme Court order curbing diesel SUVs in Delhi, small diesel car sales fell sharply. Trends, therefore, reflect macro-level directional shifts that affect which products and brands are relevant.

#### 1.1.2 Motivation
Motivation is what prompts a consumer to buy a product. Two types exist:
- **Functional motivation**: buying a product for what it does (e.g., buying a car for transportation).
- **Lifestyle motivation**: buying a product for what it says about the buyer (e.g., buying a certain car to win peer group approval). The desire to win approval of the peer group is what prompts an individual to adopt a certain lifestyle.

#### 1.1.3 Unmet Needs
These are consumer needs left unaddressed. Smart brands identify and satisfy these needs. For a long time, no brand addressed the problem of "cracked feet." Krack cream did, and became a successful brand. Polishing shoes is not a pleasant chore. Most of us would like to skip it. Often we are reminded of it in the last minute. Kiwi therefore introduced a brand named Express Shine Sponge which gives instant shine to shoes. That makes it quicker. There are always unmet needs like this in the market that brands can fulfil.

#### 1.1.4 Segmentation
Segmentation is partitioning of the market logically based on criteria such as benefits sought, price sensitivity, brand loyalty, application, etc.

---

### 1.2 Competitor Analysis

#### 1.2.1 Brand Image
Brand image is what the customer has in his/her mind when s/he thinks of the brand. Scooty is seen as a teenage or feminine brand. Pears is seen as a medicinal soap. Lux is seen as cosmetic. Apple is seen as sophisticated. SBI is seen as "safe", ICICI is seen as "aggressive" and HDFC is seen as "smart". All the three are banks. But each is perceived differently. Brand image therefore is the perception a customer holds about a brand in his/her mind.

#### 1.2.2 Strengths/Strategies
Strengths are the positives associated with a brand. IBM's strength lies in its ability to provide an end-to-end IT solution. It can configure an IT architecture that specifically addresses the IT needs of a firm. Other IT companies may or may not have the ability to deliver end-to-end. For instance a TCS or an Infosys do not deal in hardware. Horlicks can be mixed directly with hot water and consumed as a beverage. Other beverages need milk as an additive. That is Horlicks' unique strength.

Strategy is the long term vision for a brand. For instance, Dr Reddy Labs wants to be in complex generics while the rest of the Indian pharma companies focus on generics per se. Infoedge not only owns the biggest job market website Naukri but also educational, matrimonial and realty sites. Besides, it invests in and mentors several start-ups. Thus its long term charter seems to be turning into a one stop shop for all services delivered online.

[Page 2]

#### 1.2.3 Vulnerabilities
These are the weaknesses of the brand. Asian Paints is weak in industrial paints while Kansai is weak in decorative paints. Alto is weak on style while Siemens is weak on price (seen as high priced). Indian brands tend to be weak on technological upgradation while multinational brands tend to be weak on local connect.

---

### 1.3 Self-Analysis

#### 1.3.1 Existing Brand Image
This is the customers' current perception about the brand. Brand image has already been discussed in the previous section.

#### 1.3.2 Brand Heritage
Brand heritage is the tradition that a brand represents and the values it historically stood for. Swiss watch brands stand for craftsmanship. Wells Fargo has a heritage of helping small and medium enterprises (pioneers, merchants and ranchers). Starbucks was inspired by the espresso coffee bar culture of Italy. Johnson and Johnson with products for women and kids always stood for the word "caring". The ruggedness of Levi's jeans goes back to the inspiration it draws from miner's clothes.

#### 1.3.3 Strengths/Capabilities
This was already discussed under the section titled "competitor strengths and strategies".

#### 1.3.4 Organization Values
Values are beliefs central to the organization that are communicated implicitly in the activities related to the brand. Values go deeper than anything else in the organization. People, practices and products change. Values do not change often. They are cherished and held on to. Infosys believes in ethics. Benetton believes in "clothes for humans". Every occasion demands a different type of clothing. In European winter people brave biting cold. In extreme cold, in some countries kids wrap themselves up in animal skin. In others they dress up as animals for Christmas. Benetton claims to have clothing for every occasion. They see themselves as "the clothing people".

---

[Page 3]

## 2.0 Brand Identity

### 2.1 Core Identity
The essence of the brand stays with it almost throughout its life. All products of the brand should evolve from and connect with its essence. Core identity is like the social security number in US or Aadhar number in India. For a citizen it is unique. A human being can change locations, jobs, professions, even beliefs. His security number doesn't change (like his DNA). Santoor toilet soap's core identity is "achiever mother". Kellogg's is strongly identified with breakfast cereal that is "healthy". Thums Up stood for "adventure" for the last three decades. Core identity is relatively permanent.

### 2.2 Extended Identity
Extended identity is the detailing of the essence of the brand. The extended elements are not central to the brand. They change with time. That is how a brand remains true to itself and yet changes over a period of time. Suppose the core identity of a brand is "strong". That in itself does convey much to the customer. There are several ways of defining strength. For instance "strong" can mean unyielding (e.g. "unbreakable glass"). Strong can mean that it "bends but does not break" (eg a tyre that gets punctured but still runs another 50 km). Strong can mean ability to fight adversity (e.g. a tooth paste that helps your teeth survive the assault of tartar). Thus while core identity announces the intent, extended identity develops it.

[Page 4]

### A. Brand as a Product

#### a. Product Scope
It is the class of products with which a brand is associated (Eg: Reynolds – pens, Nilkamal – furniture, Amul – milk products). Product scope can be summed up in 4 queries:

i. When I mention the product class what brand comes to your mind? (When the product category "chocolates" is mentioned, do you think of Cadbury's or Nestle? When I say bearings do you remember SKF or FAG? When someone says talc do you remember Ponds or Johnson?)

ii. When I mention the sub-product class what brand comes to your mind? (When someone mentions buses an Indian might recollect Tata or Leyland but when "multi axle buses" are mentioned Volvo might be recollected. Similarly when the category "soaps" is mentioned several soaps might be recollected but when the category is "healthy soap" Dettol or Lifebuoy might be remembered).

iii. What else can the brand become? (A Leyland can become a high end truck. But can it become an ice cream? Can a Ponds or a Johnson become a screw driver? Thus there are natural limitations to product scope because of brand heritage, history and tradition).

iv. What else should the brand become? (Should Google which owns the Android operating system become a phone as it did with Pixel? It will then compete with mobile set companies like Samsung with whom it was so far collaborating as an operating system supplier. Sunfeast brand stands for biscuits now. Should it enter snacks or other food categories? Is it feasible or wise or necessary or sustainable?)

#### b. Product Attributes
Product attributes are features or benefits that are strongly linked with the brand. For instance, Bosch auto components are associated with high quality and product consistency while LIC is associated with safety and security. Ola cabs are synonymous with convenience while Fabindia shirts are seen as ethnic.

[Page 5]

#### c. Quality/Value
Quality is the perception of how "good" the product is. Quality dimension can be captured by asking the question "Is it the best one can get in the product category?" Value can be ascertained by asking "Is it the best one can get at this price?" An Ariel is identified with quality while a Ghari detergent is associated with value.

#### d. Uses
Brands typically get associated with an occasion of use. Quick-fix is useful for instantly fixing broken items. Clothes typically smell as they dry. Comfort fabric conditioner gives clothes a fragrance that makes the chore of washing more pleasant. Iodex balm is meant to be used for all body pains whether shoulder, neck, back or feet. Moov however is associated with back pain alone.

#### e. Users
Brands associate themselves not just with uses but also users. While PC brands are more for a broader set of users, XBOX and Playstation specifically aim gamers. Fevicol targets carpenters. Vicks was strongly identified with kids and cold in the past. Today the brand addresses adults as well. Fair and Lovely sees women as its customers while Fair and Handsome aims men.

#### f. Country of Origin
Some brands associate themselves with their country of origin to signal certain values. Italy is known for fashion. India is known for spirituality. A brand like Urban Yoga that specifically aims exercising therefore gains from the spontaneous Indian association of Yoga with exercise. So also several Indian brands use the description "basmati rice" to cue premium Indian rice. Foster's sees itself as "Australian for beer".

---

### B. Brand as an Organization
Brand as organization is the second pillar of brand identity system. The organization has a direct impact on the functioning and well-being of its brands. Even if a brand is strong, its performance can suffer due to an indifferent or a crisis ridden parent. For instance, TCS is a strong brand. In fact it is one of the best performing brands from the Tata stable. But the Tata group itself is seized with governance issues. This may not immediately affect TCS's performance. But it can if the issue were to linger on. Thus an organization casts a long shadow on its brands.

#### a. Organization Attributes
What flows from the organization to the product through its principles, beliefs and programs impacts all brands. A culture that is say proactive at the organizational level is more difficult to imitate than the same at the product level. Amazon pioneered several products and services like online selling, book reader, cloud services and the use of drones to deliver packages. Such accomplishments across product lines are difficult for competitors to replicate. It is difficult to be inventive in multiple domains unless the inventive gene goes back to the roots of the organization. Organizational attributes like originality, consumer connect, quality driven mind set, dependability, reputation and technology upgradation are more resilient than their counterparts at the brand level. How many organizations can launch moon shot projects like smart contact lenses that monitor sugar levels, internet providing balloons and an initiative to fight aging as Google does? How many web sites are as dependable as IRCTC? It is not necessarily smart. But it is very reliable. How many organizations can boast of the level of technology upgradation that Gillette offers through its Mach 3 or Fusion? These virtues go back to the organization. They are more resistant to mimicking than product or brand level innovations.

[Page 6]

#### b. Local vs Global
Whether a brand is local or global depends on the organizational charter. Some companies like Wipro, Bharat Forge and Lupin are global by the very nature of their products. There are others who go global because it improves their reach, competitiveness and learning. IBM has its foot print in 190 countries because of its capabilities. The Aditya Birla group diversified globally to escape the restrictions of the licence raj in India. However global presence itself is not good enough as many Indian conglomerates are learning. It is more important to run those ventures profitably, learn from the best practices there and transmit them to the rest of the geographies. In this respect multinationals have been more patient and persistent than Indian companies.

Local brands are usually smart and quick. Unlike multinationals they need not get clearances from headquarters. Several Indian companies stand testimony to this. Asian Paints has been able to outwit the multinationals in the Indian market. Voltas put in a stand out performance in air conditioners. Godrej rebounded in refrigerators despite stiff competition from multinationals. Thus some Indian companies have much better local connect than their global counterparts. This is clearly reflected in their brands.

---

### C. Brand as a Person
Brand as a person is also referred to in literature as "brand personality". This results from converting the brand into a human being. This is done by mapping specific brand attributes to human qualities. For instance, Indian Oil is seen as a dependable businessman (owns a large number of petrol pumps and doesn't cheat since he is a PSU) while Reliance is seen as an aggressive businessman (opened petrol pumps, closed them and reopened them rapidly). Café Coffee Day may be seen as "young", while Baidyanath may be seen as "old". Jennifer Aaker in her article on dimensions of brand personality lists 5 factors that constitute a brand's personality scale namely sincerity, excitement, competence, sophistication and ruggedness. But does a brand need to score on all the 5 dimensions? Is it possible? There are brands like BHEL that are sincere and competent but may not be seen as exciting, sophisticated or rugged. On the other hand ABB or Siemens might be seen as both sophisticated and competent. A brand like Ceat might be more rugged than sophisticated while an Iphone might be more sophisticated than rugged. Thus different brands map to different sets of human qualities. This contributes to their distinctiveness.

[Page 7]

### D. Brand as a Symbol

It is the symbolic aspect of the brand that makes it distinct. Some brands merely have their names written in a certain fashion like Cisco or Siemens. Others have specific symbols like GE's meatball, the circle in which the distinctive letters G and E are written. So also recollect the symbols like "rainbow flower" of Wipro or Thumbs Up sign of Thums Up. Or a distinctive tagline like Surf's "Daag acche hai" ("Stains are good") or Kerala's "God's own country" that position the brand uniquely. It could be a political slogan like "Quit India" (Indian freedom movement) or "Make in India". It could be a campaign ("Swachch Bharat" or "Clean India"). It could be an evocative pictorial representation or a metaphor like LIC's "lamp protected by hands" or SBI's keyhole which represents safety and security (signifying probably that your money is safe). It could be a mascot like Nirma Girl or "Shera", the cheerful tiger of Asian Games-2010 dressed in sports shorts and shoes and welcomes the athlete guests with traditional Indian "Namaste". It could be the signature colours of a brand like Himalaya's "green and teal" or Pepsi's "Red, white and blue". It can be a jingle (Intel, Britannia, Titan) or a program ("Tata Tea's Jago Re" or "TCS Marathon") strongly identified with the brand. Customers tend to identify brands by their sights, sounds and smells.

---

[Page 8]

## 3.0 Value Proposition

The final output of the analysis of the previous two stages (strategic brand analysis and brand identity) is the value proposition of the brand. This consists of the functional, emotional and self-expressive benefits of the brand.

### 1. Functional Benefits
Functional benefits are based on the specific utility provided to the customer by a brand. These are benefits that can be verified. They connect with the "head". They appeal to logic. For instance, cars promising to give a certain mileage. Nicotinell, the nicotine repelling over-the-counter product is available in several product forms like lozenge and gum. It promises to produce repulsion in the user for smoking in 2 seconds. Fogg claims to be a body spray "with all perfume and no gas". TVS Jupiter promises "Jyaada" (More). It promises more of everything (More storage, more convenience because the fuel tank can be filled without lifting the seat, more comfort from softer seat, more leg space and space to keep things, more mileage - best in class 62 km per litre).

**Some examples of functional benefits:**
- Vehicle – Touches 80 kmph in no time
- Tickets - Zero waiting time
- Courier – no extra charges
- Liquid fund – more benefit, less cost
- Pizza – money back if delivered late
- Cold cream – 30% improvement in skin texture
- Supermarket – dead cheap
- Website – delivers goods to your doorstep
- Mobile – intuitive icons
- Laptop - ease of use
- Car – high performance
- Bank loan – affordable instalments
- Housing - economical

[Page 9]

### 2. Emotional Benefits
Emotional benefits address the heart. They are characterized by words like happiness, sentimentality, nostalgia, fun, celebration, peace of mind etc. In fact any of the "Nava Rasas" (the Sanskrit word for the spectrum of 9 emotions ranging from eroticism and happiness to amazement and anger) can be used for connecting with the customer. Flipkart ad has kids speaking like "adults" contributing to the perception of cuteness/fun. Brooke Bond Red Label ad shows how a tea breaks barriers between cultural stereotypes of Hindus and Muslims and addresses the emotion of empathy. Honda tells you that its brands have been used by a crore people already in India. This plays to peace-of-mind. So did ICICI, which in the past created a character called "Chintamani" (a typical middle class Indian). ICICI's promise to this archetypal Indian was "No 'Chinta', only money" (no worry, only money). Communication in Vodafone's retirement farewell, Titan's gifting, Google's cross border friendship ads is wrapped in sentimentality.

Food products usually highlight gourmet, deliciousness and other sensory appeals. Sanitary napkin and anti-dandruff communication usually centres round the avoidable emotion of embarrassment. Even non-positive emotional cues like irritation can be used. For instance, Onida's "Neighbor's Envy, Owner's Pride" is an attempt to stand out by contrast, using negative emotions. It was very successful in its communication. Till determined multinationals entered and offered superior offerings Onida could charge a premium compared to Indian brands like Videocon or BPL.

**Some examples of emotional benefits:**
- Movie - It is a laugh riot
- Hospital - no post-operative complications
- Bank - zero tension
- Vacation – away from day to day worries
- Online – cash on delivery
- Retail – no questions asked replacement
- Resort – Near divine experience

[Page 10]

### 3. Self-Expressive Benefits
Self-expressive benefits play to ego. The words associated with self-expressive benefits are demonstrativeness, badge value, snob value, display, elitism, exclusivity and pride. An example of this is, being proud of owning a BMW, a Harley Davidson, a Rolex or a Vertu mobile service. Why is the name HP or Dell written upside down on your laptop? It is to indicate to the others that you are smart (because you bought HP). Why is Ray Ban written on your spectacles so prominently? It is to demonstrate to others that you are a proud Ray Ban user. An American Express Centurion Card more popularly called Amex black card is a by-invitation only card. It is given to a select set of wealthy customers. Some housing societies are closed to outsiders. They play up exclusivity and snob value. Close Up toothpaste promises to make one attractive to the other gender. Old Spice hands out the allurement "smell like a man". Tattooing is meant to cue to others that you are "cool". Furniture in the hall is meant to demonstrate to others that you are tasteful. Bose speakers are meant to signal the refined taste of the owner. Sleek products like Curved LED Samsung TV, HP Envy, Lenovo thin laptop allow the user to bask in the reflected glory of their sleekness. Transmission Audio has speakers that go all the way up to 2 million $. Often such high end gadgets are custom built. They are meant to showcase the discerning buyer. In an ultimate compliment to self-expressiveness, Tony Curtis, the Hollywood actor declared that he wanted to be buried with his Iphone. In sum, self-expressive benefits underscore what psychologists call "self-concept" (one's own sense of self) and how it relates to others.

**Summary Table — Three Types of Benefits:**

| Benefit | What it addresses | Typical e.g. | Other e.g.s |
|---|---|---|---|
| Functional | Head | This chair is "comfy" | Makes my life simple |
| Emotional | Heart | This is my grandfather's chair | Makes me happy/sentimental |
| Self-expressive | Ego | This chair has been imported from Germany | Makes me proud |

**Finally:** Brand identity is how the strategist wants the brand to be seen by customers. Brand image is how it is seen by customers.

---

[Page 11]

## Case Application: Colgate vs. Dantkanti (Toothpaste)

Given below is the comparison of Colgate and Dantkanti toothpastes based on the "brand identity planning model" framework.

| | **COLGATE** | **DANTKANTI** |
|---|---|---|
| **Customer Analysis** | | |
| Trends | Looking for trendy products | Looking for traditional products |
| Motivation | One stop shop for all dental needs like paste, mouth wash, floss etc | No side effect, ayurvedic, natural product with convenient packaging |
| Unmet Needs | Almost none. Caters to all dental requirements | Not reached the vast masses it wants to cater to yet |
| Segmentation | All members of family | Those with inclination for natural products |
| **Competitor Analysis** | Sensodyne | Dabur |
| Brand Image/Identity | Dentist recommended medicinal paste for sensitive teeth | An ayurvedic brand becoming an FMCG brand (like Patanjali) |
| Strengths/Strategies | A niche that has not been exploited till recently by other toothpastes | Established distribution especially in the East |
| Vulnerabilities | It is a niche, can the brand go beyond specialty application? | In ayurvedic space it has competition from Patanjali and in FMCG it has competition from HUL. Its positioning falls between the two. Competition is more sharply positioned. |
| **Self-Analysis** | | |
| Existing Brand Image | High quality cleaning gel – one of the best known in the market | Strong association with Baba Ram Dev, the founder |
| Brand Heritage | Decades old yet modern toothpaste | Indian ayurvedic formulation in convenient form |
| Strengths/Capabilities | Long term association with Indian customers | Aims the traditional Indian mind-set |
| Organization Values | High-tech, modern, multinational | Ayurvedic, Ethnic |

[Page 12]

| | **COLGATE** (continued) | **DANTKANTI** (continued) |
|---|---|---|
| **Brand Identity** | | |
| **Brand as a Product** | | |
| Product Scope | Total teeth, gum and mouth-care | Variety of toothpastes on herbal range. Can expand to same level as Colgate |
| Product Attributes | Tested and proved chemical composition for total mouth care | Completely natural composition with no side effects |
| Quality/Value | Reasonable price value equation | Great price value equation |
| Uses | Total mouth care | Teeth-cleansing |
| Users | Looking for holistic benefits | Looking for herbal benefits |
| Country of Origin | New York, modern | India, traditional |
| **Brand as an Organization** | | |
| Organizational Attributes | High-tech testing, modern scientific approach | Striving to bring back indigenous knowledge and healthcare |
| Local Vs. Global | Global | Local |

[Page 13]

| | **COLGATE** (continued) | **DANTKANTI** (continued) |
|---|---|---|
| **Brand as a Person** | | |
| Personality | Modern, hygiene focused Indian | Traditional, trustworthy, simple but adaptive Indian |
| Brand-Customer Relationship | Doctor-patient relationship | Old friend relationship |
| **Value Proposition** | | |
| Functional Benefits | Cleans teeth/mouth | Cleans teeth |
| Emotional Benefits | Feel totally protected | Feel connected with nature |
| Self-expressive Benefits | Modern | Ethnic |

---

## Discussion Questions

ii. Which of the 3 types of benefits are signalled by the following statements:
- a) I like this brand
- b) I like this model
- c) This has better service than others
- d) I buy because I can show off
- e) I buy because this is easy to use
- f) I buy because it has less call drops
- g) I buy because it is cute
