## [CULT BRANDING - HOW CULTS SEDUCE]
Source: knowledge_base/reading_materials/how_cults_seduce.md
---
# How Cults Seduce — And What Marketing Can Learn From Them

**Authors:** John Grant and Alex Wipperfürth
**Publication:** Plan B (independent marketing consultancy paper)
**Year:** 2002
**Original Filename:** How cults seduce.pdf
**Publisher Note:** Alex Wipperfürth (alex@plan-b.biz) and John Grant (thejohngrant@btclick.com). John Grant's book *After Image* was published in Europe in May 2002.

---

[Page 1]

## How Cults Seduce — And What Marketing Can Learn From Them

> **Ever wondered what would happen if you didn't send the Jehovah's Witnesses away, when they came knocking?**

Eventually they would invite you to join a meeting at Kingdom Hall. But they wouldn't do that until you were good and ready. They would come and visit you a number of times first, to talk about Christ Jesus, New Light and the Ark of Salvation.

If you were observant, you'd notice a pattern in these visits. Each time the same lead Witness would bring along a different assistant. You'd probably not even pick up on this, or you'd rationalize that it was just random variation. But — according to accounts from people who have left the sect — it's quite deliberate and it's designed to get you hooked.

The rotation ensures that, when you finally visit Kingdom Hall for the first time, you will see quite a few familiar faces in the crowd. And so you'll immediately feel that you belong here.

Most people find cults scary. But that's to the cults' advantage. Wherever there is resistance, there is an unconscious factor. None of us are as rational as we'd like to believe. Cults live on the irrational fringe. It's not about mass coercion. It's about things barely glimpsed from the corner of your eye. Cults have fine-tuned their marketing to hook into nuances of human nature.

---

[Page 2]

### Cults are like the "I Love You" computer worm.

Ask yourself: what other message would have millions of wary people opening a '.exe' attachment from someone they know only vaguely? In London, the BBC (whose reputation as "luvvies" precedes them) was totally overrun by this epidemic. With cults and similar phenomena, some audiences are more susceptible than others.

Cults are a common research subject for social scientists, like Aaron Lynch (author of "Thought Contagion") because they are good 'test tubes' for human nature. Like many extreme phenomena, they are enlightening about what makes us tick.

But for marketers, cults can feel like a taboo topic.

Cults have a bad name, for being overly manipulative in attracting, retaining and profiting from members. Marketing has drawn similar criticisms, albeit in a milder form (e.g., "No Logo"). Surely studying cult techniques could lead us into yet more hot water?

**Yes and no.**

Christianity was originally a cult. So, in a lay form, are Alcoholics Anonymous, Amnesty International and Weight Watchers. **Ethics should be judged by the result, not just the act.**

As a marketer in today's environment, given a potential backlash, you'd have to be a bit desperate to play the cult card. And "desperate times" is indeed when cult marketing is called for.

Cults thrive under conditions where most conventional organizations would curl up and die. They are repressed, at times actively persecuted, even today (e.g., Fulan Gong in China). They are damned in the media and met with suspicion on every corner.

It's when you get into dire straits with a brand, that cult tactics become relevant.

We came to study cults when working with Napster, through their darkest days of 2001. Napster had transitioned from being the fastest growing technology in history into a black hole legal case, with many music files barred and a temporary shut-down of their service imminent. All they had left was a hard core of fanatics, who'd do anything to keep Napster going.

---

[Page 3]

## PART 1 — The Anthropology of a Cult

Our investigation of the marketing tactics of cult organizations — the Scientologists, Jehovah's Witnesses, Moonies, Maharishi's and others — relied on eye witness testimonies from past cult members, as well as reports by cult watchers. The evidence reveals a high degree of consistency between cults in their overall pattern of activities, which divide into four key areas.

### The Four Key Areas of Cult Activity

These four areas are depicted in a circular diagram with IDEOLOGY at the centre, surrounded by an outer ring labelled CUSTOMS, and four quadrant labels positioned at north, south, east, and west. Small house/community icons are arranged around the circle at each compass point and in between, representing community nodes. Each quadrant represents one of the four key activities:

---

#### 1. Boundaries (West)

**Cults, unlike most modern social phenomena, have a closed boundary. You're either in or out. This creates passionate solidarity.**

> "She wasn't my daughter any more," the mother of a Moonie recruit alleged. "They took her over — body and soul. She was swallowed up."

---

#### 2. Initiation (East)

**It's actually not that easy getting into a cult. You can't "walk in". There is a definite process to joining. This converts people's whole outlook.**

> Maharishi recruitment "is like one of those large, funnel shaped nets, that's dragged along by a trawler. The further down the funnel you go, the more intense it gets."

---

#### 3. Customs (South)

**Cults are a parallel social universe with their own rituals, relationship structures and experiences. This binds individuals to the cause.**

> It is forbidden to wear gold rimmed glasses in the Jehovah's Witnesses.

---

#### 4. Ideology (Centre/South-West)

**Cults program what members think and do. They have strong central ideology and leadership. This fosters alignment and clarity.**

> In Scientology anything written or said by L. Ron Hubbard is referred to as "the tech" (as in: "psychiatry doesn't work because they don't have the tech.")

---

> **NOTE** — Our study was largely based on the reports and testimonies of ex-cult members. It's not intended as criticism, or claiming to be unbiased. These were the only sources we could find that were candid about what cults actually do, and we've had to take their word for it.

---

[Page 4]

**It's hard to read reports of cult activity without feeling morally squeamish.** In our analysis we kept focused on a simple question: what is it in this mystical fog that is relevant to marketing?

Each of the four areas of activity is there to sustain and develop the cult in the face of resistance. It's in this role that the tactics of cults are illuminating for brands in our turbulent times.

---

### THE TACTICS: 1. Boundaries — THE MOONIES

#### THE FIREWALL
- Cults build a protective barrier
- Only those committed and close to the core are fully in the know
- Outsiders are kept largely in the dark

> *The Moonies are defended by religious status, secrecy, armies of lawyers and lobbyists, and powerful links in the Washington establishment. Responding to criticisms of brainwashing and hard-sell tactics, they have rebranded themselves "The Family Federation for World Peace and Unification" and are even applying for state funding for some of their social projects.*

---

#### "BIG BROTHER"
- Cults define 'what we're not'
- A common tactic is to define a big enemy in the outside world
- Attacks from the outside only reinforce the paranoia
- The firewall is also an information firewall
- Only those inside know the truth. Outsiders should not be listened to

> *The Unification Church's main enemy is Christianity. They proclaim that "Moon's Church will replace Christianity in America" and that "Jesus is not in the kingdom of heaven" (because he "did not succeed in his mission"), and that "the blessed children (of Moonies) are better than Jesus."*

---

#### INTERNAL VS EXTERNAL COMMUNICATIONS
- Members have privileged information
- Communication is intimate, extensive and frequent
- Outsiders are often fed a completely different story

> *The Moonies control internal communication rigorously; for instance their rule "no first weekers talking to first weekers" (ie sharing doubts). Externally they often use affiliates; for instance supplying sex education programs to public schools ("Total Abstinence"). They also own The Washington Times.*

---

#### THE SPOTLIGHT
Cults provide selective glimpses of their parallel universe, by staging their own dramatic news, events and experiences.

> *The Moonies stage ecstatic public rallies and mass weddings. They court celebrity endorsement through "charity awards dinners" attended by politicians at the highest level. High profile converts are prized — for instance in 2001, Catholic Archbishop Emmanuel Millingo.*

---

[Page 5]

### THE TACTICS: 2. Initiation — MAHARISHI MAHESH YOGA

#### SELECTING RECRUITS
- Cults focus not on 'who we can get' but 'who is ready'
- People going through dramatic life change make excellent recruits; new students, recent divorcees...

> *85% of people who join a cult do so through a friend or acquaintance. The Premie (Prempal Rawat a.k.a. Maharaji) followers make this their hunting ground. Cult members are charged with getting a friend, colleague or family member to come to a special introductory video screening or to "witness a ceremony" (when there, they find everyone must join in).*

---

#### BAITING THE HOOK
- No-one wakes up one day and says "I know, I'll join a cult"
- Cults use bait which invite you in for an innocent, worthwhile purpose with high propensity for repeat visits — like a course
- During this course, you are initiated to a world view that says 'learning the ways of this cult is the real purpose of my life'

> *The Premie's bait is "Transcendental Meditation"™ consisting of traditional meditation techniques, a "Science of Creative Intelligence" and the 'Puja' modeled on Hindu religious ceremonies. This was a winning combination in the hippy-spiritual 1960s and 70s and TM became so widespread it had to be excluded from being taught in public schools.*

---

#### LOVE BOMBING
- A key human need which cults meet is belonging
- Imagine stumbling across a weird and wonderful community where everyone instantly loves and accepts you!

> *Only those cult members who have shown an aptitude for attracting and reassuring potential recruits are included in these introductory sessions; and a relationship is built across a series of convivial social meetings.*

---

#### MATRICULATION
- This is a critical process and is carefully managed
- You can't just walk in and 'join' — you have to be invited and experience an initiation
- This is the "brainwashing" stage; intense, isolating, aimed at breaking you down, getting you to internalize and identify with certain problems, then building you back up as a believer

> *After the introductory sessions an instructor interviews you and decides if you're ready for a private session. The aim then is to get the recruit to declare themselves 'ready for the knowledge' and to accept the Maharaji as the only source of this 'inner truth'. When you 'pass', you get to the 'knowledge session' where you are shown the techniques.*

---

[Page 6]

### THE TACTICS: 3. Customs — JEHOVAH'S WITNESSES

#### PYRAMID
- Cults know that treating everyone the same means making no-one feels special or motivated
- They act as an emotional pyramid scheme, self-sustaining and administering through many levels
- Communication is mainly vertical

> *The Jehovah's Witness organization is known as the Watchtower. Each Kingdom Hall is led by elders. Witnesses are not allowed to take any initiatives of their own, but in all matters must await orders from their immediate superior; 'meat in due season'. Anyone who questions an order is kicked out for 'disfellowship'.*

---

#### DUTIES
- Cults keep people very busy (too busy to stop and think)
- Cults are about far more than fundraising — every member has many basic duties: do the leg work: recruit, improve whatever needs fixing, sell crafts…
- Members get promoted through hierarchies as reward for loyalty and achievement

> *Idleness is a sin! The most common complaint of ex-cult members is not the spiritual content, so much as that all their spare time is devoted to work, like mending the Kingdom Hall roof and trudging from doorstep to doorstep.*

---

#### PSYCHOLOGICAL TWISTS
- Cults are fuelled by commitments of time, money, activities…
- Contrary to their image, they seldom coerce members into this
- Rather they create a context where there is competition (and strong peer pressure) to have your commitments accepted
- If there is resistance they turn this on the individual; it's clearly something they need to work on!

> *Jehovah's Witnesses live by an exceptionally strict code. They must avoid "bad association" (non Witnesses) and cannot lie or cheat or drink… The psychological twist is called "Theocratic Strategy"; which means they can for instance lie for the cause, to anyone who doesn't 'have the right to know'.*

---

#### MOMENTUM
- Cults stay relevant and vibrant through momentum
- If there is nothing much happening, cults rely on apparently random acts of insanity and tests of loyalty
- Deadlines and dates are common tactics

> *The cases of Witnesses refusing medical treatment are famous. And the Watchtower has been saying that Armageddon (the end of the world) is "soon" since 1884.*

---

[Page 7]

### THE TACTICS: 4. Ideology — THE SCIENTOLOGISTS

#### THE LEADER/THE APOSTLES
- Cults tend to be personality cults
- The leaders' story is the story of the cult, their word is law
- Access to the leader is an exclusive reward to the higher ups
- These act as apostles, evangelists and gate-keepers

> *L. Ron Hubbard is revered in the Scientologists to the extent that everything he says is taken as the literal truth; his writings are described as "data". We see here a sophisticated conceptual branding lexicon. Which is another fascinating feature of cult identity…*

---

#### THE WORD
- Cults have their own vocabulary, way of speaking
- This has a powerful effect; words are concepts are thoughts
- Cults also foreclose awkward debate with 'wise sayings'
- There is implicit social pressure; to speak like us is to belong

> *Scientology has developed an extensive proprietary language system. LRH's techniques are "the tech." The hierarchy is "the gradient." Progress is across "the bridge." Recruitment is "stats." Disciplinary action is "ethics"…*
>
> *The lexicon is designed to convey scientific authority and foreclose debate. (A bit like business jargon really).*

---

#### BELIEF SYSTEM
- Cults are masters of disciplined, simple formatting
- Part of their appeal is a simple black-and-white view of reality, far from the mess of real relationships and life conflicts
- This is pinned to a general vision few would argue like Peace or Clarity
- A constant repetition of the same format displaces all else with this simple core; chanting, reciting, committing...

> *The clarity of Scientology is based on the fact that it is 100% right and if you don't get it you need "handling." If you don't absorb something then you have to resit a course at your own expense. If this fails you have do "ethics." It's your fault.*

---

#### THE RULES
- Arbitrary restrictions and rules, oddities and experiences reinforce a way of life "through the looking glass"
- And ritual experiences underline the 'otherness of this world'
- Cutting ties with your mundane old existence is key to making this alternate reality the reality

> *Scientologists are not supposed to wear spectacles (because L. Ron Hubbard once asserted that "the tech" could cure vision defects). Scientology is anti-psychiatry and once even deliberately orchestrated a Prozac health scare.*

---

[Page 8]

## PART 2 — The Sub-Conscious Techniques of Cult Brands

The link between the activities of secretive religious cults and those of respected, household-name brands may not be immediately apparent. But there are some examples from the 'innocent' world of branding that do show a remarkable similarity to the four key activities of cults.

### The Four Techniques of Cult Brands (Circular Diagram)

The diagram mirrors the cult anthropology model from Part 1. At the centre is **BRAND RELIGION**, surrounded by **PASSIONATE COMMUNITY** (equivalent to Customs in the cult model). The four quadrant positions map cult activities to brand equivalents:

---

#### 1. "In-the-Know" MARCOM (West — equivalent to Boundaries)
**Cult brands communicate with the market in unusually scarce, deliberate and very targeted marketing, PR and promotions. This adds mystique to the brand and rewards those on the inside.**

*Brand example shown: Linux (Tux the penguin logo)*

---

#### 2. Committed Customer Acquisition (East — equivalent to Initiation)
**Cult brands are an exclusive club. They deliberately sacrifice a larger market for a smaller inner circle that is taken through an elaborate "initiation" process to create committed solidarity.**

*Brand example shown: Harley-Davidson*

---

#### 3. Passionate Community (South — equivalent to Customs)
**A cult brand's loyal usership is bound to the brand's mission. It is a community with its own rituals, vocabulary — and often — hierarchy. They become not mere users, but crucial ambassadors for the brand — in effect unpaid employees.**

*Brand example shown: eBay*

---

#### 4. Brand Religion (Centre/South-West — equivalent to Ideology)
**Each brand acts like it is "on a mission from God". With a strong, central ideology and leadership. This gives the brand much larger meaning.**

*Brand example shown: Apple*

---

[Page 9]

## Translating Cult Tactics into Marketing Tactics

Cults are religious sects. Companies on the other hand are places people work and others buy stuff from. It doesn't do to get too carried away with the analogy (although John Grant's former agency St Luke's went further than most!)

That's why our analysis centres on the social activities (rather than the religious themes); to ask if there are analogous strategies in the marketing realm that could be deployed? And at this level it's quite easy to see the possibilities.

### Master Mapping Table: Cults → Some Marketing Equivalents

The following table maps each cult tactic to its marketing equivalent, organized by the four cult dimensions and their corresponding brand dimension:

| Dimension | Cult Tactic | Marketing Equivalent | Brand Dimension |
|---|---|---|---|
| **Boundaries** | FIREWALL | MEMBERS-ONLY | "In-the-Know" MARCOM |
| **Boundaries** | BIG BROTHER | DAVID & GOLIATH | "In-the-Know" MARCOM |
| **Boundaries** | INT/EXT COMMS | INT/EXT PR | "In-the-Know" MARCOM |
| **Boundaries** | SPOTLIGHT | LEAKS AND STUNTS | "In-the-Know" MARCOM |
| **Initiation** | SELECTION | INNER CIRCLE TARGETING | Committed Customer Acquisition |
| **Initiation** | BAIT THE HOOK | RALLYING CRY | Committed Customer Acquisition |
| **Initiation** | LOVE BOMBING | CHARISMATIC EVENTS | Committed Customer Acquisition |
| **Initiation** | MATRICULATION | DRIVING LOYALTY | Committed Customer Acquisition |
| **Customs** | PYRAMID | LOYALTY TIERS | Passionate Community |
| **Customs** | DUTIES | PARTICIPATION MARKETING | Passionate Community |
| **Customs** | PSYCH TWISTS | TURNING THE TABLES | Passionate Community |
| **Customs** | MOMENTUM | BRAND NEWS | Passionate Community |
| **Ideology** | LEADER/APOSTLES | FOUNDER & ADVOCATES | Brand Religion |
| **Ideology** | THE WORD | BRAND VOCABULARY | Brand Religion |
| **Ideology** | BELIEF SYSTEM | BRAND MANIFESTO | Brand Religion |
| **Ideology** | THE RULES | RITUALS & PRINCIPLES | Brand Religion |

---

[Page 10]

**Full cult marketing is not for the faint-hearted. It is a last resort, because like major surgery, the 'cure' has its own dangers.**

That's not because of the "cult" stigma necessarily. Just because some accuse cults of abusing their members, it doesn't mean their tactics are in themselves evil. There's a simple answer to the stigma; don't tell anybody "this is cult marketing." Just do it.

The reasons why cult marketing is a last resort are:
- it excludes the majority of your potential audience
- it is slow
- it is difficult to transition out of again

> When **Playstation** created a waiting list for PS2 ("Turning the tables"), or **Disney** limits the times that videos of their films are available ("Deadlines"), nobody accuses them of "brainwashing."

It is these very factors that have made the cult phase a millstone for Harley in later life. Now it's struggling to transition to a more mainstream clientele, because diehard HOGs are scaring off new white-collar customers, dismissing them as "rubbies," rich, urban, riders.

### Cult Marketing Is Indicated For:

- A brand which is in a life or death situation
- They have a genuine ideology (you can't fake this)
  - …but this is out of sync with the current context
  - …and/or they have a crisis which has put them on the defensive
  - …and/or they have a product that isn't working fully yet
  - …or a product that's fallen behind
- They are small enough to be the underdog (but not so small that they're not worth fighting for)
- The cult activity is for a limited period, to buy time
- All other avenues have been exhausted

**That was pretty much where we found ourselves with Napster.**

> **Even then we knew this was a last resort strategy. The risks — of e.g. alienating the record labels forever — were sky high.**
>
> **As it turned out, we managed not to implement a full cult marketing program ("help us to save Napster") because a route to relaunch was found instead.**

---

[Page 11]

## Cult Case 1: "IN-THE-KNOW" MARKETING COMMUNICATIONS — LINUX

"The Open Source" movement was a reaction from within the geek community to the commercial empires being built on what used to be common land. The O/S had always just been shared as free source code until then. The movement's manifesto was the famous white paper titled "The Cathedral and the Bazaar." The Free Software Foundation, IRC and other initiatives soon followed.

So what would it take to build a new operating system? Who could take on Microsoft head-to-head? The only chance you'd have is to **compete outside of capitalism**; with no financial interest yet with moral superiority. That's what makes it cult; the early Christians vs the Roman Empire.

The cult founder came in the unlikely figure of Linus Torvalds; a shy, young student at Helsinki University. He was trying to improve an existing OS with a passing resemblance to Unix, as a project at Helsinki University. Linus put the result on a bulletin board for reviews and feedback. And Linux was born…

**i. Members-only.** The Linux community for years extended as far as other hackers, and no further. It was buried in the bulletin boards and campuses. Even now that there are 15 million Linux users, they have a mystique and solidarity which comes from their invisibility. For the establishment it was seen no threat if seen at all. It was like Hannibal coming over the Alps with elephants!

**ii. David vs. Goliath.** Linux is a lot like Unix, only free. This limited it to powerful servers and expert coders. But the spiritual enemy has always been Microsoft. And this has finally become a real confrontation, as Linux is being touted as the affordable alternative to XP for small businesses. Microsoft executives certainly recognize that this is a revolt. One, Jim Allchin said: "I'm an American. I believe in the American Way. I worry if the government encourages open source…"

**iii. Internal vs External PR.** On the internet no-one knows you are a moonlighter. The Linux code was developed in spare time by people whose every waking thought was officially contracted and owned. There's a story that one Silicon Valley CTO sent an anonymous note about a secret Linux meeting at his company, to find that 100 people turned up, including some of his direct reports!

**iv. Leaks & Stunts.** When it went public, it went PUBLIC. Remember the open source movement had its share of Silicon Valley veteran insiders. They knew that the one event that could put Linux on the map was a "coronation" on Wall Street. So with the cult's blessing, companies like Red Hat raised billions to take the Linux dagger to Microsoft's throat.

---

[Page 12]

## Cult Case 2: COMMITTED CUSTOMER ACQUISITION — HARLEY-DAVIDSON

Harley was the stuff of history. And that's where they were heading in the early 1980s after the Japanese motorbike invasion. Faced with bankruptcy, decades behind on quality, the turnaround started with a management buyout and, in 1983 the formation of a bikers cult; the Harley Owners Group or "HOG."

As a result of these initiatives, improved quality and retro design, Harley Davidson has enjoyed twelve continuous years of growth. HOG is also the largest company-sponsored motorbike club in the world with 365,000 members in 940 chapters.

**i. Inner-Circle Targeting.** The initial HOGs were mostly Hells Angels. They drew their ranks from blue collar America, the very group whose jobs were threatened by Japanese imports. They also drew on the patriotism, pride and disenfranchisement of a generation. One HOG went as far as to thank Rich Teerlink the Harley CEO…*"For giving me the parade I never got when I returned from Vietnam."*

**ii. Rallying Cry.** They had a simple, powerful message; "Help Harley Survive." This transcended the usual 'them and us' marketing divide and brought HOGs into a shared cause. There were strong overtones of "Save America," the Harley symbolizing an industrial nation's plight in chrome and steel. In true cult style they added innocent elements like fundraising for the Muscular Dystrophy Foundation.

**iii. Charismatic Events.** Harley rides and rallies (already a feature of life in the Hells Angels) were the social glue. They culminated in five-year anniversary rallies, which made even Moonie mass weddings look low key.

**iv. Driving Loyalty.** One tactic the company uses to lure new riders is called Rider's Edge. This three day driver's training course is organized by dealers for new motorcycle riders. Lara Lee, director of Rider's Edge sees it like this: "We wanted to take the person who felt like an outsider and turn them into an insider." Harley also introduced a Ride Free exchange program where, if you bought the starter model (Harley Sportster) then you could trade it in at full value a year later for a bigger bike.

---

[Page 13]

## Cult Case 3: PASSIONATE COMMUNITY — E-BAY

In 1997, e-Bay found itself in a full nelson, when major fraud scandals threatened their credibility and business model. They were in danger of turning from something that felt like a neighborhood car boot sale into a nasty and risky black market. But if they introduced draconian regulations they would kill that special, folksy vibe even quicker. So they turned to their community instead and established a whole architecture of trust.

**i. Loyalty Tiers.** The more you trade, the higher you get in the hierarchy, via a rating scheme. And if you cheat or defraud, you get thrown out. Sellers with a higher rating can actually command a premium of up to 30%. Some people make a living just selling on e-Bay and this is supported through an extra tier — the Power-Selling program — with added services.

**ii. Participation Marketing.** The average user spends 3.5 hours on the site (longer than any where else in e-commerce). You have to rate the other party after every sale. Some members also took it on themselves to coach newbies. One called "Uncle Griffiths" was so active in this that they were hired by Omidyar to be the company's first customer support rep. eBabes, a club of eBay sellers have meetings to discuss strategies and issues.

**iii. Turning the Tables.** The eBay business model turns e-commerce into a gambling game. Instead of getting in and getting out, the bidding process encourages multiple visits as buyers check the status of their bids. There's a thrill to winning a bid and people get addicted. As Nancy Wilson, self-proclaimed e-Bay Queen put it: "I've never had anything I enjoy doing as much as doing this. I watch the bidding every day like a little kid."

**iv. Brand News.** In quick succession they introduced: the ratings system, insurance of up to $200 against fraud, a seller identity verification service ($5, by Equifax), a ban on sellers bidding their own products, a ban on buyers who win the auction but do not complete the sale. They also cleaned the site up, banning firearm sales and relegating anything questionable to an Adults Only area.

e-Bay has also created their own news. Like the entire disgruntled Silicon Valley IT department who auctioned themselves off to another company. Or the woman who recently put her hand in marriage up for bids.

---

[Page 14]

## Cult Case 4: BRAND RELIGION — APPLE

If one company can lay claim to inventing cult marketing, internally and externally, it's Apple. And for nearly thirty years they've proved — to our principle that cult marketing is a only ever short term measure — that every rule has an exception.

We can only plead that the computing industry has properties in common with the spaces studied by Stephen Hawking, and a few black holes and neutron stars are the least you could expect.

**Apple ticks every box on the cult scorecard.**

They've done **Boundaries** with the only proprietary system to go it alone against Windows and shown IBM that open platforms are the house red wine, to their champagne. They have played on "Big Brother" as a constant refrain. They've also drawn a fence around profitable niches like the whole multimedia community.

They've done **Initiation** in the extreme. OSX broke every rule of product evolution (one of the authors is into his second year of waiting for music sequencing software to get with the program) but boy does it over-deliver, once you've got up the learning curve and found some OS10 drivers!

They've done **Customs** by changing the relationship between human and box. Their designs are rivaled only by Sony in their aesthetics and accessibility. And that pays. In a market that is supposed to be waiting for the renewal cycle to restart, 40% of new i-Mac orders came from first time computer buyers.

Not that they've neglected the core. Mac geeks still have a partisan spirit that's reminiscent more of fringe sects than hardware retailing. And more than anything that's down to the **Ideology** — or in marketing terms: **Brand Religion**:

**i. Founder & Advocates.** Steve Jobs. The man who returned to save the company on a salary of $1. It was Jobs who started the cult, with the secretive Mac team and their skull and cross bones emblem, a mystique which spilled over into the launch. And the apostles are hardly less impressive. Like Guy Kawasaki whose smart, empassioned articulation of the vision made him the original corporate evangelist. And Jonathon Ives, designer of i-Mac, Cube, T4… who is fast accomplishing a one man Bauhaus.

---

[Page 15]

*(Cult Case 4: Apple — continued)*

**ii. Brand Vocabulary.** Apple invented (or at least popularized) the whole language of graphical interface computing; folders, icons, mouse, double click.

They have also been masterful meme merchants; giving their loyal followers the ammunition to bring down their deluded pc-user acquaintances:
- "C:/ONGRTLNS.W95"
- "WINDOWS 95 = MAC 89 + 8MB"

> "Saying Windows 95 is equivalent to Macintosh is like finding a potato that looks like Jesus and believing you've witnessed the second coming!!!"*
>
> "Macintosh. A religion, a way of life."*

*\*It's noticeable that the cult principles of the company have seeped into their language*

**iii. Brand Manifesto.** Many Apple people genuinely believe they are making the world a better place. This was reinforced by the company's initiatives in education and with disadvantaged groups.

And they have used cult media, like collectible t-shirts, to constantly refresh the Apple allegiance (you wear your commitment on your chest). As one contractor put it: "Apple is the only place where you have to put your t-shirt size on your job application."

**iv. Rituals & Principles.** Jobs introduced the ultimate counterpart to corporate normality; compulsory anarchy. They embraced diversity and soft values, but never lost sight of results aggression. As they say at Apple: "Great Developers Ship."

---

[Page 16]

## From Cult Marketing to Global Religion?

### So where is it all heading?

In an age when even the world's hottest brands are often to be found facing a firing squad — Coca-Cola (European dioxin scare), Nike (Child Labor), Gap (badly out of fashion) — it seems cult marketing is set to grow.

Cult marketing is an antidote to those fat, lazy years of "nice ads, steady growth."

It is unusual because it is defensive (in a world of offensive marketing).

It is also pointing to a new killer positioning: **non-premium exclusivity**. That's why it's also the natural tactic of companies like IKEA, whose exploits include banning customers with beards from their Bristol store, customer sleep-overs in Stockholm to launch IKEA beds and America's own DYKEA lesbian action group.

The broadest influence may be on mainstream marketing campaigns; cult as tactic, not strategy. Rather than the whole 16 point program, companies can cherry-pick the cult tactics:

- Brand Religion for internal fanaticism. Like the Nike Ekins sales force, with their swoosh tattoos.
- "In-the-know" MARCOM for niche audiences like developers, pharmacists or journalists. Like Volvo who sampled their new S60 to beautiful people, along with reservations at valet-parking restaurants.

But "cult" will never become a marketing "global religion" like brand image, because it only works in from the fringe. And some of us, who have caught the cult marketing bug, think that's fine.

---

*Alex Wipperfürth (alex@plan-b.biz) and John Grant (thejohngrant@btclick.com) are known to drink their own Kool-Aid. John's new book "After Image" is published in Europe in May 2002.*

*© All rights reserved. Plan B, 2002.*

Brand Management  
 
Framework for Cult Brands 
PGP 2016-18 Term III Brand Management Group 2 
Page 3 of 12 
1. Abstract  
This report contains an initial discussion on what a brand is. It goes on to 
inform what differentiates an iconic brand from a cult brand and finally gives 
rise to a cult-branding framework. The methodology used to derive the 
framework involves research-using keywords like brand in conjunction with 
salvation, devotion, myth, faith, etc. With the help of the framework 
developed, building a framework which draws on the brand equity model of 
Aaker, and finally gives an exhaustive list of parameters on which we can 
judge if a brand is a cult brand or not. This framework can also be used to 
determine how a brand goes from being an iconic brand with high brand 
equity to being a cult brand. 
2. Objective  
We aim to achieve the following two-point objective while developing a 
comprehensive framework explaining Cult branding: 
 To define and determine if the brand in contention is a cult brand or not 
 How iconic brands differ from cult brands 
3. Introduction 
3.1. Brand 
According to Kotler, a brand can be defined as “a name, sign, symbol or blend 
of these which can be used to identify the goods and services of a company 
and to differentiate them from those of competitors”1 
3.2. Iconic Brand 
As per Mark Batey2, iconic brands combine heritage, unique iconography and 
a compelling narrative. Also, he mentions that it is important to note that 
certain brands can have some iconic elements which help make the brand 
very recognizable. Yet they need not be an iconic brand. An iconic brand also 
needs to stand for something meaningful and admirable. 
D.B. Holt3 says, that an iconic brand reaches the identity value of the 
cultural icon. We can say, thus, that iconic brands have a very high equity 
which makes them likable and recognized. It can be measured by David 
Aaker’s brand equity model using the 10 parameters that he has mentioned. 
3.3. Cult Brand 
According to Matthew W. Ragas and Bolivar J. Bueno4, a cult brand is 
differentiated, courageous, fun, listens to its consumers, creates consumer 
communities, is open/inclusive and promotes personal freedom. As per 
(Atkin, 2004), a cult brand has four major aspects5: great exclusive devotion, 
distinct ideology, well-defined community and voluntary advocates.  


[Page 4]
Brand Management  
 
Framework for Cult Brands 
PGP 2016-18 Term III Brand Management Group 2 
Page 4 of 12 
Thus, a cult brand is an exemplary brand, which relates to the consumer is 
ways more than any traditional brand or even for that matter an iconic 
brand. A cult brand is not just a product or service; it is a way of life. 
As for example, Harley Davidson is a cult brand, which is a way of life for the 
riders. It goes beyond being just a motorcycle to being a community, a part of 
one’s self and the consumers have a relationship with it.  
4. Distinction between iconic and cult brands 
Using Aaker’s brand equity model, an iconic brand can be determined by 
measuring the brand on the parameters of Awareness, Perceived Quality, 
Consumer Loyalty and Association/Differentiation. For a cult brand, however, 
these parameters cannot be used since a cult brand shall rank high on such 
parameters. Thus, in building a cult branding framework, we are drawing on 
these parameters and using surrogates for the same. The process is given 
below: 
4.1. Awareness -> Evangelism 
Awareness 
In the brand equity model, awareness is defined as the salience of the brand 
in the customer’s mind. There can be different levels of awareness such as 
recognition, 
recall, 
top-of-mind-awareness, 
brand 
dominance, 
brand 
knowledge and brand opinion. 
Evangelism 
According to Kawasaki (1991, p. 3) evangelism is to convince people to believe 
in a product or idea as much as you do. But the point to be noted in the case 
of cult brands is that the consumers are the evangelists and the entire 
community of users does the marketing for the brand. 
Another concept of brand evangelism is described as a committed way of 
disseminating positive opinions and trying ardently to persuade others to get 
involved with the same brand.6 For cult brands, we can find that evangelism 
is a core behaviour displayed by the consumers. 
4.2. Perceived Quality and leadership-> Transformational quality 
Perceived Quality 
The qualities of being the best, having consistent quality compared to other 
brands can makes a brand iconic. Also, the qualities of being an innovator in 
a category, or bring in-trend contribute to an iconic brand. 
Transformational quality 
Cult brands are timeless and they own space where they are never compared 
to regular brands of that category. This is because of the aura that surrounds 


[Page 5]
Brand Management  
 
Framework for Cult Brands 
PGP 2016-18 Term III Brand Management Group 2 
Page 5 of 12 
a cult brand. The consumers believe that a cult brand has transformational 
qualities. A cult brand can help a consumer achieve the reflection (Kapferer’s 
prism) and bridge the gap between a self-image and reflection in a superior 
manner. 
The transformational power of a cult brand is what draws consumers to the 
brand in droves. It is a strong motivator that binds the followers of the cult 
together and inspires non-followers to join the cult. 
4.3. Loyalty -> Fanaticism 
Loyalty 
Brand loyalty is defined as conative loyalty or the degree to which the 
customer is dedicated to repurchase7 of the brand (Oliver, 1999).  Aaker, 
1996 in his article mentions that loyalty is an important aspect of brand 
equity. An iconic brand will have a high loyalty which can be estimated by 
high price premium and high level of consumer satisfaction. 
Fanaticism 
In case of cult brand the loyalty translates into a severe degree of fanaticism. 
This may be represented by the aversion of an opposing brand, or 
“oppositional brand loyalty” (Muniz and Hamer 2001; O’Guinn and Muniz 
2005). Consider the football club followers, every fan of Man-U will hate the 
other clubs and will strongly voice their opposition and vice versa.   
4.4. Associations/Differentiation -> Exclusive Community 
Associations/Differentiation 
Various perspectives regarding a brand as a product, a person or an 
organization lead to associations made with a brand. These associations if 
unique lead to a point of differentiation against competing brands. These 
attributes help a brand gain high equity to become iconic. 
Exclusive Community 
For a cult brand, the strongest association is the fanatic community of 
devotees. Exclusive membership of that community, such as the HOGs 
(Harley Owners Group), or the “ferraristi” is what adds to the allure of a cult 
brand. Members flaunt their ownership and non-members want to become 
members to flaunt it. 
It is the community that helps make the brand more than an inanimate 
object. The three-way relationship among the consumer, the community and 
the brand reinforce each other. The community promotes evangelism. It also 
indulges in myth building and sustaining the myth. 


[Page 6]
Brand Management  
 
Framework for Cult Brands 
PGP 2016-18 Term III Brand Management Group 2 
Page 6 of 12 
5. The Cult Branding Framework 
5.1. Framework 
The main attributes the framework derived 
from the brand equity model are: 
a. Evangelism 
b. Transformational quality 
c. Fanaticism 
d. Exclusive community 
There are several factors, which contribute to 
these attributes which are detailed below: 
5.1.1. Evangelism 
a. Active recruitment of non-users/positive 
word-of-mouth: 
The consumers of a cult brand are actively 
involved in positive word of mouth and 
active recruitment of non-users to the 
brand. Positive word of mouth is defined as 
the extent to which the consumers praise 
the brand to other people.8 Activities such 
as positive word of mouth and active 
recruitment 
represents 
the 
consumers’ 
willingness to speak up or stand up for the brand. This kind of active 
support for the brand channels the consumer’s commitment towards the 
brand. Consumers of a cult brand rate high in positive word of mouth and 
active recruitment for the brand which can be associated to the high degree 
of emotional attachment they share with the brand. The consumers act as 
missionaries of the brand propagating the benefits of the brand and 
convincing non-users to try their brand9. The consumers act as devotees of 
the brand and the brand is their object of devotion. 
b. Merchandising10: 
Consumers of cult brand are differentiated from other brand consumers as 
they are devoted to buying the merchandise and accessories of their brand 
i.e. various extensions of the brand. The loyalty that these consumers have 
for the brand drives them to own items attached to the brand, For eg fans of 
IPL teams in India are seen to own the various merchandise of their teams 
such as caps, T-shirts, equipment sold officially by their respective teams. 
These brand extensions are seen as sacred by the consumers and it is 
highly important that they own them, as it shows their commitment and 
allegiance to the brand. These items hold a special place in the consumer’s 
life and can be seen in cases of cult brands where the users are seen to have 
Evangelism 
 
Active recruitment 
 
Merchandising 
 
Display behaviour 
 
Brand extension 
Transformational qualities 
 
Benefits 
 
Inspirational 
 
Imitation/Sell Lifestyle 
Fanaticism 
 
Addiction 
 
Brand forgiveness 
Exclusive Community 
 
Brand-as-partner 
 
Community-brand-consumer 
 
Rituals and traditions 
Figure 1: Cult branding parameters 


[Page 7]
Brand Management  
 
Framework for Cult Brands 
PGP 2016-18 Term III Brand Management Group 2 
Page 7 of 12 
dedicated special rooms and areas in their home where they keep all their 
brand merchandise. The consumers revel in their collections as it helps 
them to objectify the brand and enhance their association with the brand. 
c. Display behaviour (show off logo)  
Displaying association to the brand be it in the form of having brand logos 
stuck to your car, wearing brand merchandise etc are an important aspect 
followers of cult brand showcase. About display behaviour, Richins (1994) 
states that displaying their association with the brands helps them to 
express themselves. These associations act as an extension of the consumer 
and help them to project and maintain their identity. This emotional bond 
with the brand which connects with the consumer as part of his/her 
identity is what makes the brand into a cult. 
d. Brand Extension11:  
Brand extension is done by brands to launch new products in a completely 
different category as compared to the product category in which they are 
currently in. Brands which can leverage their current brand reputation, 
image and equity to introduce a brand extension and has the majority of 
users under their parent brand purchasing their products under the 
extension is a good sign that the brand is a cult brand. This is because 
followers of a cult brand view the extension as a part of the original brand 
and it is an opportunity for them to maintain and enhance the bond they 
have with the brand. 
5.1.2. Transformational Quality 
a. Benefits:  
The benefits to be considered for a brand to become a cult lie beyond 
functional benefits to be offered by the brand.12 Functional benefits can be 
easily copied by competitors and hence are not a source of sustained 
advantage for a brand, thus for a brand to differentiate and become a cult, 
emotional, self-expressive and social benefits that the brand offers are 
looked into. 
Emotional Benefits are the capability of the brand to invoke feelings in the 
person while consuming the brand. For eg, Volvo invokes the feeling of 
safety, Tata invokes the feeling of trust and ethics etc. 
Self-expressive Benefits in a brand exist when it provides a way for the 
consumer to express him/her, thus improving the bond and the connect the 
consumer feels with the brand. For eg, People feel adventurous while driving 
a Harley etc. Social Benefit is the ability of a brand to establish a community 
of like-minded people and provide the user of the brand a means to relate 
with that community of people. For eg you can share the ics of your last ride 
on Harleys website. 
A cult brand rates high in all these three benefits offered and it is when all 
these three exist a brand can become cult. 


[Page 8]
Brand Management  
 
Framework for Cult Brands 
PGP 2016-18 Term III Brand Management Group 2 
Page 8 of 12 
b. Inspirational –  
Cult brands drive inspiration for its followers. The passion to follow the 
brand is self-evident from their actions. They inspire people to set goals 
resonating with attaining cult brand personality.  Cult brand personality is 
an ideal state that every cult brand devotee wishes to achieve or possess. 
Cult brands like Apple inspire people to innovate and consistently 
differentiate self from others. 
c. Imitation/Adoption/sell lifestyles –  
Cult brand practices are highly subjected to imitation and adoption by cult 
brand devotees. Cult brands in a way sell lifestyles and influence the way 
people make choices. Salman Khan followers after the release of his movie 
started adopting the iconic hairstyle which became a rage. The “Being 
Human” charity of Salman Khan promotes a philanthropic lifestyle and is 
followed widely by its followers engaging them in doing the same. 
5.1.3. Fanaticism 
a. Addiction/Refusal to switch: 
The next important aspect is the addiction to the brand i.e. refusal to 
switch from the brand to another brand. In case of an iconic brand the 
search-energy will be low as compared to a cult brand where the search 
energy would be extremely high. 
Consider the example of Royal Enfield Thunderbird 350, a hallowed cult 
brand product. When it was launched, the waiting time for the product 
varied from 45 days13 to 14 months and was only reduced from 6-814 
months when another plant was commissioned because of the excess 
demand. All these instances validate the idea that for a cult brand the 
search energy is very high which can easily be translated to long waiting 
time /pre-order etc. 
b. Brand forgiveness: 
A Cult brand also shows significant Forgiveness behaviour. Consider the 
example of Salman Khan’s alleged act of rampant driving which killed 
people. The fans are of one of the two opinions, either he hasn’t done that 
or even if he is guilty he can be forgiven; various arguments are made 
stating15 he is vehemently championed across various social networking 
portals and public portals. This forgiveness attribute lies in core of 
devotional behaviour. 
5.1.4. Exclusive Community 
a. Brand-as-partner16 (relationship dyad): 
Consumers tend to think of a cult brand as a humanized, animated and 
personalized entity. The consumers interact with the brand as if it is a vital 
object and thus develop a relationship with the brand. This relationship 


[Page 9]
Brand Management  
 
Framework for Cult Brands 
PGP 2016-18 Term III Brand Management Group 2 
Page 9 of 12 
extends into the everyday life of the consumer and reinforces the love and 
passion that the consumer feels for the brand. 
b. Community-brand-consumer (relationship triad): 
The community of devotees of a cult brand provide a strong sense of 
belongingness to the consumer. It provides an avenue for self- expression 
to the consumers. Also, the relationship among the community members 
reinforces the consumer-brand dyad and that in turn reinforces the 
consumer-community relationship in a virtuous cycle. 
c. Rituals and traditions: 
According to Elisabeth A. Pichler and Andrea Hemetsberger,17 rituals are a 
set of activities, which have a dramatic script, and the followers of cult 
brands perform them repetitively over time. Such rituals lend a structure 
to the everyday lives of consumers and thus, the brand becomes a part of 
the consumer’s daily life. 
 
5.2. Methodolo
gy 
The 
idea 
of 
developing 
a 
framework is to 
build a useful 
structure 
to 
help 
grasp 
a 
conceptual 
understanding 
of 
cult 
branding 
in 
general. 
The 
value 
of 
the 
framework 
is 
helping 
us 
make 
judgements 
about qualification of a brand as a cult brand and differentiate cult brands from 
other brand types specifically iconic brands.  
In the framework building methodology, we 
have tried to develop on the idea of cult 
brand from its very root of branding. We 
believe that a brand undergoes a series of 
transformations before becoming a cult 
brand and our framework captures those 
Step 3 
 
Cult Brand 
 Step 2 
 
Iconic Brand 
  Step 1 
 
Basic Brand 
Figure 2: Cult branding framework 
Figure 3: Stages of Branding 


[Page 10]
Brand Management  
 
Framework for Cult Brands 
PGP 2016-18 Term III Brand Management Group 2 
Page 10 of 12 
stages. A pyramid like structure explains the incremental growth of a brand 
wherein the base of the pyramid identifies the basic attributes of a brand (Step -
1). As we go one level higher the brand acquires certain values of attributes 
developed on the basis of the brand equity model which make it an iconic brand 
(Step-2). One step higher, Iconic brands develop over time to attain certain 
qualities among its followers to be named as cult brands (Step-3).  The 
attainment of cult brand status is the pinnacle of branding and rightly acquires 
the top of the pyramid in our framework.  The brand hierarchy used is depicted 
in adjoining figure.  
The factors considered at every step of the pyramid are exhaustive in nature and 
define a set of attributes that relate to a brand. As we go up the pyramid slope, 
the attributes with a brand is recognised become a niche and start differentiating 
itself from other brands.  For a brand to be qualified as a cult brand it needs to 
tick down majority factors on the pyramid on successive test levels.  
6. Framework Validation 
The outcome of the above designed framework is to justify the two objectives 
stated in the beginning. The validity of our framework argument for cult and 
iconic brands can be tested by taking examples of each and showing how an 
iconic brand fails to be a cult brand after failing to satisfy the factors mentioned 
in the top half of the pyramid. 
Example to Test the hypothesis 
Coca Cola in India is an iconic brand but it cannot be categorized as a cult 
brand. This can be hypothesised using the framework.  Coca Cola owing to its 
long stranded presence in Indian market and strong consistent advertising over 
the years has strong brand association and high brand awareness. The perceived 
quality of the brand and its products is pretty high in addition to a large quantity 
of consumers who are loyal to the brand. Thus Coca Cola qualifies well on the 
step 2 of the framework.  Moving to step 3 of cult branding, Coca Cola followers 
are not brand evangelists neither are they brand fanatics. The bonding 
relationship attributes of Coca Cola brand followers isn’t strong enough to build 
a devotee community and the transformational qualities of Coca Cola as a 
product a perceived to be weak. Hence, the framework clearly refutes Coca Cola 
as a cult brand. 
On the other hand, Rajnikanth the famous South Indian actor is an iconic as 
well as a cult brand. In addition to qualifying brand equity model attributes of 
step -2, the brand stands true for Step -3 as well. For instance, Rajnikanth has a 
large community of followers who are not only brand evangelists but brand 
fanatics as well. The Rajnikanth style defines a distinctive way of living 
religiously followed and practiced by brand devotees. Anything Rajni does 


[Page 11]
Brand Management  
 
Framework for Cult Brands 
PGP 2016-18 Term III Brand Management Group 2 
Page 11 of 12 
become a rage and is subject to imitation and adoption by the brand community. 
Thereby, Rajnikanth is a cult brand. 
Cult branding for services 
Cult brands in services are rare to the extent of our knowledge such as 
Southwest Airlines or Salvation Army.  Let us consider Southwest airlines to 
ascertain the feasibility of our framework in branding services. The brand has an 
extensive community fan page and promotes stories18 and supports community 
behaviour by organizing donation drives. The brand considers people as its prime 
element which it supports. The donation and philanthropist acts from entire 
team southwest promotes a lifestyle and has a self expressive value proposition 
which promotes a minimum wastage and lean approach. Southwest store has a 
fair range of merchandizes such as apparels, toys, drinkwares, accessories etc. 
and the community actively indulges in display; which indicate a evangelist 
attitude. The users of southwest have strong resistance to switch to any other 
airlines, and a have a strong brand affection.  Thus, the brand covers the aspects 
of the framework and can safely be categorized a cult brand. 
7. Limitations/Critique of Cult Branding Framework 
Just like any other branding framework, our framework too has some guiding 
limitations and a possible critique to it is as follows: 
 Our framework is a near approximation and requires thorough testing / 
validation by trying out with a different variety of cult brands spanning 
across different categories. 
 Our framework is silent on the quantitative aspect of cult branding in 
general. There is no metrics to measure the degree of strength of a cult 
brand through the framework. Assigning weights to each parameter of the 
pyramid to arrive at a quantitative score is an activity not supported by the 
framework. 
 The framework is not suited to do a comparative analysis within cult 
brands. There is no measure to distinguish one cult brand from the other 
across categories. 
 Though our framework successfully identifies cult branding services (e.g. 
the Salvation Army, South West Airlines), the idea of branding services as 
cult is still an amateur proposition as per the framework attributes. 
8. Summary 
Through this exercise of developing a framework for cult brands we have tried 
to decipher a few key factors that differentiate a basic brand and an iconic 
brand from a cult brand. An attempt has been made to a define cult brand 
based on the published literature adding into account our understanding of 
the concept. Besides the framework also helps suggest a few critical attributes 


[Page 12]
Brand Management  
 
Framework for Cult Brands 
PGP 2016-18 Term III Brand Management Group 2 
Page 12 of 12 
that help in transformation of brands to cult brands. The pyramid framework 
developed follows an incremental approach to understanding branding. The 
framework is validated by using examples of iconic and cult brands to test our 
hypothesis. The final outcome of the pyramidal structure is in lines with the 
objectives mentioned. Understanding the utility of the framework an attempt 
has also been made to critique our own framework by highlighting the 
limitations of the proposed arrangement.

## [PROOF AS 8TH P OF SERVICE]

# Is proof the Eighth P of Service A Conceptual Model and Propositions

Is “proof” the Eighth “P” of Service? A Conceptual
Model and Propositions
Y. L. R. Moorthi & Subhadip Roy
