SERVICES MARKETING QUARTERLY
, VOL. , NO. , –
https://doi.org/./..
Is “proof”the Eighth “P”of Service? A Conceptual Model and
Propositions
Y. L. R. Moorthia and Subhadip Royb
aDepartment of Marketing, Indian Institute of Management Bangalore, Bangalore, India; bDepartment of
Marketing, Indian Institute of Management Udaipur, Udaipur, India
KEYWORDS
Service management;
services marketing; eighth P
of services; conceptual
model
ABSTRACT
The article aims at making a conceptual contribution to services
marketing literature by proposing an eighth “P” called “proof.”
Proof is a simple operational measure or an intuitive indicator
that helps the customer quickly assess the efficacy of a service.
The article discusses services marketing literature and develops
propositions that underscore the importance of proof as the
eighth P. When proof is provided about a service to the customer,
it affects all service outcomes (i.e., service evaluation, purchase
intent, customer satisfaction, and repurchase intent). Though
conceptual, the article provides practical implications as well.
Introduction
A service in marketing is typically specified through its marketing mix, popularly
known as the 7Ps. The 7Ps are product, price, place, promotion, physical evidence,
process, and people (Booms & Bitner, 1981). However, what the 7Ps do not cover
is a measure or an indicator of the usefulness of the service to the consumer. This
is where we propose an eighth “P” of services marketing by name of “proof.” Proof
is making good the service promise and would help to measure if the service were
delivered well through 7Ps. It is an indicator intuitively understood by the customer
(e.g., “Few customers if ever complain about our service”) or is expressed as a num-
ber (e.g., “97% of our customers received feedback on time”).
Proof is different from other measures of services or the 7Ps. While physical evi-
dence is related to factors such as signage, employee dress, and store layout, proof
goes beyond that and provides a metric either from the present or the past that
gives confidence to the customer in service. Similarly, while processes would enable
a customer to evaluate service aspects, proof of how smooth or efficient the same
processes are would stimulate them to select/consume the service. Thus, proof bor-
rows from all the 7Ps, past records, dynamically presented evidence online, and
third-party certifications. Proof can alter perceptions about a service, and hence
CONTACT Subhadip Roy
subhadip.roy@iimu.ac.in
Indian Institute of Management Udaipur, Balicha Campus,
Udaipur , India.
© Taylor & Francis Group, LLC
Downloaded by [Indian Institute of Management Bangalore] at 16:40 13 December 2017 


[Page 3]
214
Y. L. R. MOORTHI AND S. ROY
can make a difference to the manner in which one perceives or judges it. If proof
is offered to the customer (present or potential) it can make a difference to the eval-
uation, purchase, satisfaction, and repurchase of the service. Proof is the outcome
of the synergy of 7Ps but is an independent measure. Therefore, we could argue
that giving customers proof before, during or after consumption of the service can
make a discernible difference to evaluation, purchase, satisfaction, and repurchase,
respectively.
Given this background, it is interesting to note that there has not been much
focus on the concept of proof as a separate construct, or as we suggest, the eighth
P of services marketing. In the present study, we aim to establish the relevance of
proof conceptually as an important and independent entity in services marketing.
Thereby we undertake the following steps. First, we provide a literature review of
the major metrics in services marketing (such as service level agreement [SLA],
SERVQUAL, physical evidence, and proof of service [PoS]), and simultaneously dis-
cuss how proof is different from them. Subsequently, we discuss the role of proof as
a mediator between the 7Ps and consumer attitudinal and behavioral outcomes of
service consumption. Thereby, we develop a set of testable propositions in a con-
ceptual model that underlines the role of proof in services marketing. We end the
article with the discussion and scope for future studies in the topic.
Literature survey
The concept of proof is not entirely new and has been hinted at in four streams of
service literature viz.: (a) servicescape; (b) service quality; (c) SLA, and (d) PoS.
Servicescape and proof
Servicescape is a widely used term to describe the physical surroundings of a ser-
vice company (Bitner, 1992). Servicescape includes the exterior and interior design;
ambient conditions such as temperature, noise, and odor; and other tangible props
like business cards, brochures, and other communication material (e.g., Bitner,
1992; Wakefield & Blodgett, 1996). Zeithaml and Bitner (1996) listed facility design,
equipment, signage, employees dress, reports, business cards, statements, guaran-
tees, and other tangibles in physical evidence. Notice that statements and guaran-
tees are still intent and not proof. A testimonial from a third party or an expert that
a guarantee has been delivered can be proof.
Reimer and Keuhn (2005) offered servicescape as a surrogate for service qual-
ity. Others (Berry & Bendapudi, 2003; Lo, 2011) have also supported the use of
servicescape as an indicator of “service evidence.” The outcome of a positive ser-
vicescape is favorable on both the service provider (Parish, Berry, & Lam, 2008)
and customers (Rosenbaum & Wong, 2007). In addition, Rosenbaum and Massiah
(2011) delved deeper into the concept of servicescape and found various intangi-
ble or symbolic elements that may influence the consumer more than tangibles.
Researchers have observed various outcomes of servicescape such as satisfac-
tion/dissatisfaction (Hoffman, Kelley, & Chung, 2003; Miles, Miles, & Cannon,
Downloaded by [Indian Institute of Management Bangalore] at 16:40 13 December 2017 


[Page 4]
SERVICES MARKETING QUARTERLY
215
2012), loyalty (Harris & Ezeh, 2008) and dysfunctional customer behavior (Daunt
& Harris, 2012). It could be observed that the servicescape literature discusses
the impact of and the impact on servicescape from a cross section of sources
like employees, culture, environment, customers, and competitive strategy. None
converts physical evidence into a measure or indicator for proof.
Service quality and proof
Measuring service quality has been a debatable issue in services research and various
measures were suggested for the same (e.g., Cronin & Taylor, 1994; Parasuraman,
Zeithaml, & Berry, 1994). The hierarchical service quality model (Brady & Cronin,
2001) suggests that there are three levels at which service quality is measured: (a)
interaction quality, (b) physical environment quality, and (c) outcome quality (Brady
& Cronin, 2001). (Pollack, 2009) linked higher hierarchical service quality model
measures with greater satisfaction, repurchase, and word-of-mouth. SERVPERF is
the other instrument that is often compared with SERVQUAL. Badri, Abdulla, and
Al-Madani (2005) and Gica and Moisescu (2013) found SERVPERF to be more suit-
able in assessing service quality of travel agencies than SERVQUAL. In this con-
text, Ladhari’s (2009) historical research on SERVQUAL supported its usefulness in
spite of limitations. Depending on the context, SERVQUAL and SERVPERF have
been observed to yield similar (Bayraktaroglu & Atrek, 2010) and different results
(Ali, Ali, & Radam, 2010; Carrillat, Jaramillo, & Mulki, 2007; Rodrigues, Barkur,
Varambally, & Golrooy Motlagh, 2011). However, Gounaris (2005) showed that
INDSERV is more suitable for measuring service quality in business-to-business ser-
vices than SERVQUAL. From the literature, it could be inferred that all approaches
to measure service quality have merits and demerits, but none has a simple approach
and all require a structured process of estimation. Proof, as we argue, is a less rigor-
ous but a quick dipstick measure from the customer. It is an indicator that appeals
to the customer about a service in a certain context.
Service level agreement and proof
SLA is used more often in the information technology industry. A SLA is defined
as an agreement that is developed based on mutual understanding and nego-
tiations between a service provider and a client that “serves as the foundation
for the expected level of service between the consumer and the provider” (Patel,
Ranabahu, & Sheth, 2009). Majority of research in SLA focused on operational issues
such as implementation (Caggiano, Jackson, Muckstadt, & Rappold, 2007; Johnson,
2005; Robbins & Harrison, 2010); management (Trienekens, Bouman, & Van Der
Zwan, 2004); regulation (Stojanovic, Kostic-Ljubisavljevic, & Radonjic-Djogatovic,
2013), and security implications (Karadsheh, 2012; Serral-Gracia, Yannuzzi, Labit,
Owezarski, & Masip-Bruin, 2010). Most of these prescriptions are qualitative or
quantitative methodologies. They are elaborate and therefore are not simple or intu-
itive. In addition, they are more concerned with the harder parts of a process rather
Downloaded by [Indian Institute of Management Bangalore] at 16:40 13 December 2017 


[Page 5]
216
Y. L. R. MOORTHI AND S. ROY
that the softer consumer elements that would be more relevant in service delivery.
Proof, as we propose, has to be simple, intuitive, and give comfort to the customer.
“Proof of service” and proof
Proof of service (PoS), like SLA, is a concept more often used in information technol-
ogy and communications. Hsiao and Yang (2010) describe PoS in software projects
to consist of service model design, service verification, service quality, and service
efficiency. Schorr (1997) distinguished between PoS and the impact of PoS in the
context of linking research with public health program outcomes. Marquis, Douthit,
and Elliot (2011) echoed a similar view in the context of counseling, where they
distinguished between best practices and evidence supported treatments. Shostack
(1982) offered three indicators of PoS: (a) time and material consumption, (b)
resource-as-a-service (infrastructure or platform or software), or (c) process trans-
formation. The PoS literature (quite similar to SLA) delves more into methodolo-
gies rather than simple and specific metrics that can help customers quickly judge a
service.
Table 1 summarizes how proof is different from the four measures, namely:
Servicescape, SERVQUAL, SLA, and PoS. Proof has to give the customer a simple
indication (like escorting them to their chosen location in a restaurant instead
of giving them directions) or a number (percentage of customers satisfied with
the employees; see Column 3 in Table 1). In most cases the customer should be
immediately able to verify the proof themselves (e.g., picking up the receiver in
a service center before it rings twice). We therefore conclude that the concept of
Table . Proof and the other four concepts.
Extant concepts
Why they are diﬀerent from proof
How to convert them to proof
Servicescape/Physical
evidence
Often signals intention (e.g., neatly
dressed service staﬀ, good
ambience). Proof signals a measure
of delivery not service provider’s
intent.
An employee escorting the customer to
the speciﬁc location they want to go to
in a hotel and not giving just giving
them directions (this is more than a
dress code or intention, it is proof of
work ethos.)
SERVQUAL
Though a useful measure, it requires
elaborate surveying and number
crunching. It is not quick or
intuitively understood by customer.
The percentage of customers who rated
the employees highly (a less rigorous
but simple benchmark that tells the
customer how eﬀective the service is).
Service level
agreement
Service level agreement is an
understanding between the service
provider and the customer. It is not
proof.
“We have never failed to reach the
customer within time in the last
years.”
It is a method as well as a commitment.
In some cases, if the commitment is
delivered then it could become
proof. It is also sometimes a
methodology.
Proof of service
An umbrella concept that
encompasses several things from
demonstrating a working model to a
collection of quantitative and
qualitative methodologies.
“In the service center we pick up the
receiver before it rings twice.”
Neither simple nor clear. Proof has to
be a simple measure.
Downloaded by [Indian Institute of Management Bangalore] at 16:40 13 December 2017 


[Page 6]
SERVICES MARKETING QUARTERLY
217
proof is different from the aforementioned concepts. Each of the inputs (physical
evidence, SERVQUAL, SLA, and PoS) might contain the seed for proof. Depending
on the context, the customer, and the domain, the service provider has to con-
vert some aspects of these four approaches into proof. Proof has to be not only
extracted but also communicated to customers. The service provider, in their own
interest, should articulate and present proof because it positively affects factors
such as service evaluation and purchase intent. That is why we call proof as the
eighth P. In the following subsections we present a set of propositions that are
concerned with the impact of proof on service evaluation through purchase intent
and customer satisfaction to repurchase. We also present propositions of the effect
of sources of proof (friends/acquaintances/websites/referrals, etc.) and types of
proof (positive/negative).
Propositions
Service evaluation and proof
The first proposition relates service evaluation to proof. Researchers have found var-
ious aspects of service recovery and evaluation to be related to the evidence provided
by the service provider. (Gautam, 2013) found service recovery to be correlated pos-
itively to the response promptness of the service provider. A prompt response can be
converted to proof that helps customers in service evaluation. Bawden, Petuchovaite,
and Vilar (2005) found the need for an evidence-based system to assess the ser-
vice performance of libraries in a European nation. Gutierrez, Martinez-Lopez, and
Molina Fernandez (2006) showed that service quality perceptions are related to the
length of period for which the customers are associated with the service provider.
Ofir, Simonson, and Yoon (2009) extended this thought and suggested that the fact
that something is being evaluated in itself tends to bring negative evaluations from
customers. Thus, prompt action during a service failure enhances the positive eval-
uation of the service by customers.
We argue that customers are less likely to form negative evaluations if they are
given evidence to the contrary. In addition, alerting customers to the possibility of a
rating bias on either side reduces the tendency to give negative evaluations. Instead,
if effective and positive proof is offered about a service, it brings down the tendency
to rate it negatively. The parameters of service evaluation are set by the expectations
of the customer. The service provider should satisfy them to the best of their abilities
(Alden, He, & Chen, 2010). It is important therefore that the service provider gives
the customer a proof that they value. We therefore, propose:
Proposition 1: Providing relevant proof affects service evaluation positively.
Negative evaluation and proof
Negative evaluations come from several sources in a service, such as waiting time
(Taylor, 1995); emotional rather than factual assessment (Smith & Bolton, 2002);
Downloaded by [Indian Institute of Management Bangalore] at 16:40 13 December 2017 


[Page 7]
218
Y. L. R. MOORTHI AND S. ROY
disconfirmation of expectations (Hill & Tombs, 2011); and misbehavior of other
customers (Huang, 2010). Good service providers help customers fight the negative
emotions generated by service failure (Smith & Bolton, 2002). In some cases of neg-
ative service experiences, conditional service guarantees after service recovery have
improved evaluation (McColl, Mattsson, & Morley, 2005). Thus, when organizations
make efforts to mitigate customer grievances, the customers view the same favorably.
Thus, giving reassuring proof at appropriate points is useful. In addition, when there
is service failure due to switching to a new mechanism like self-service, high-equity
brands suffer less if they deliver adequate service recovery (i.e., remedy service fail-
ure). They also suffer less when the cause for service failure lies outside the control
of the service provider (Liao & Cheng, 2013). Thus, even if there is a negative expe-
rience, customers are often ready to forgive a service that enjoys better reputation.
Typically, mitigation of service complaints should be addressed through regulating
the quality of service experience (Brown, 2007) such as the experience provided by
Disney theme parks and stores. A negative service experience can also be coun-
tered by addressing specific dimensions of quality that improve overall perception
of service (Odekerken-Schröder, Van Birgelen, Lemmink, De Ruyter, & Wetzels,
2000).
One way to address negative experience is providing positive PoS. In other words,
customers would rate a service more favorably even if there is a negative service
experience if: (a) the reasons are beyond control, (b) employees are seen as making
effort to address the problem, and (c) the service provider is generally known for
delivering good service. In all the mentioned cases, providing appropriate proof can
give greater room for the service provider in handling negative service experiences.
Thus, the next proposition of the study is given as:
Proposition 2: A reliable service suffers less despite a negative customer experience, if it
provides relevant proof.
Purchase intent and proof
Several different stimuli are found to trigger purchase intent such as price bundle
and discounts (Bouwman, Haaker, & De Vos, 2007; Stafford & Stafford, 2000), after
service recovery (Maxham, 2001), and service guarantee (Wirtz, Kum, & Sheang
Lee, 2000). Other soft aspects such as employee conduct may also lead to pur-
chase intent (Netemeyer, 2005; Shao, Baker, & Wagner, 2004). Among other factors,
giving relevant information enhances the desirability of a service (Pulos & Leng,
2010). Conversely, not giving appropriate information brings down its attractive-
ness (Monopoli, Nicholas, Georgiou, & Korfiati, 2002; Roxas, Peek, Peek, & Hage-
mann, 2000). Thus, we argue that providing proof can enhance the prospect of pur-
chase. Proof, as mentioned however, is contextual. Each of the aforementioned cases
demands a different proof. Providing proof of relevance (such as safety in a ferry
service, nutrition in a restaurant, and ease-of-use in a website) could alter choice.
Hence, we present the next proposition as:
Downloaded by [Indian Institute of Management Bangalore] at 16:40 13 December 2017 


[Page 8]
SERVICES MARKETING QUARTERLY
219
Proposition 3: Providing relevant proof impacts purchase intent positively.
Customer satisfaction and proof
Customer satisfaction or dissatisfaction has several sources such as the basic ser-
vice quality, employees (Guchait, Namasivayam, & Lei, 2011), service failure sever-
ity (Weun, Beatty, & Jones, 2004), and service firm’s culture (Ame, 2013). However,
when there is service recovery, satisfaction improves remarkably (Maxham, 2001).
Proof here seems to be a function of promptness of action. Therefore, proof artic-
ulated intelligently by employees can have a salutary effect on customer satisfac-
tion. Finally, the service outcome itself is crucial for customer satisfaction. Litera-
ture shows that if the outcome is satisfactory, lapses in service experience tend to
be ignored (Park, Cho, & Rao, 2012). In this aspect, Dabholkar and Overby (2005)
found that desired service outcomes are more important than desirable processes. In
other words, tangible proof the customer sees as output is more important than ser-
vice experience itself. Therefore, proof provided to customer during or at the exit
of service experience can positively affect satisfaction. This leads us to the fourth
proposition:
Proposition 4: Providing relevant proof affects customer satisfaction positively.
Repurchase intent and proof
Repurchase is based on the firsthand experience of the customer with the service and
overall customer satisfaction is an important trigger for repurchase (Tang, Jia, Zhou,
& Yin, 2010). Therefore, how the service encounter itself is handled is important for
repurchase (Park, Cho, & Rao, 2012). Maxham, and Netemeyer (2002) opined that
repurchase intent dips sharply if there are two consecutive service failures without
recovery. Hume (2008) hence contended that both core (e.g., the food served in a
restaurant) and supplementary (e.g., the steward smiling when they serve) compo-
nents of the service are important for repurchase. Consequently, a proof at the cor-
porate brand level might serve as a booster for repurchase (e.g., no IndiGo airlines
flight was rescheduled between December 1, 2013, and January 31, 2014, therefore
it is a reliable airline). This leads to the next proposition:
Proposition 5: Proof that underscores the strength of the corporate brand influences
repurchase.
Employees hold the key to any service (Liljander & Mattson, 2002) and rela-
tionship performance (customer–employee) in previous encounters are important
influencers of repurchase (Jones, 2014). Further, what employees do in uncontrolled
service situations seems to affect repurchase intent significantly. Thus, proof pro-
vided by employees who enjoy the confidence of the customers might have a positive
impact on repurchase. Hence, the next proposition is given as:
Downloaded by [Indian Institute of Management Bangalore] at 16:40 13 December 2017 


[Page 9]
220
Y. L. R. MOORTHI AND S. ROY
Figure . The conceptual model.
Proposition 6: When employees that are highly rated by customers provide proof, it assists
repurchase.
Peers and proof
A vast body of literature suggests that word-of-mouth from friends and acquain-
tances has a positive effect on service choice and evaluation (e.g., Godes & Mayzlin,
2009; Mehtap-Smadi, 2010; Dobele & Lindgreen, 2011). Proof given by peers
strongly impacts service evaluation and choice (e.g., a friend or acquaintance telling
that they saw the movie 10 times or that they go to the same restaurant every week
is more likely to impact ones’ choice positively). Therefore, the next proposition is
given as:
Proposition 7: Proof given by friends and acquaintances would affect consumer decision on
service choice.
Downloaded by [Indian Institute of Management Bangalore] at 16:40 13 December 2017 


[Page 10]
SERVICES MARKETING QUARTERLY
221
The Model
Combining the propositions and the 7Ps of services marketing we create the con-
ceptual model in Figure 1. Thus, we propose that the 7Ps should lead to the concept
of proof that would in turn facilitate service evaluation, purchase intent, satisfac-
tion, and repurchase intent. We are not undermining the role of the 7Ps of services
marketing, but only highlighting the role of the new P (proof) in the same model.
The propositions in the model when tested, will establish the importance of proof
as a concept. They can collectively demonstrate that appropriate proof when pro-
vided to the customer impacts service outcomes across the board. These range from
service evaluation through purchase intent and customer satisfaction to repurchase
intent. If this framework is tested and accepted then proof can be called the eighth P
of the service marketing mix. Proof is in itself a consequence of the synergy between
the elements in the 7Ps. However, it acquires a meaning of its own for the customer.
When presented appropriately for a given context, proof can make a fundamental
impact on service evaluation, purchase intent, customer satisfaction, and repurchase
intent at every stage.
Conclusion
The present study establishes the role of proof as the eighth P of services market-
ing. However, the model and arguments developed in this present study are only
at a conceptual level and need empirical validation. Thereby the study only pro-
vides a new direction that has to be tested formally using empirical data. Besides,
the propositions need to be augmented using anecdotal and theoretical evidence
to create testable hypotheses. However, the study has its theoretical implication in
providing a new P of services marketing and provides managers a new direction to
think in terms of service creation and delivery.

## [INTERBRAND VALUATION METHOD]
Source: knowledge_base/reading_materials/interbrand_method.md
---
# Brand Valuation: A Versatile Strategic Tool for Business

**Author(s):** Mike Rocha (Global Director, Brand Valuation)
**Publication:** Interbrand (White Paper)
**Year:** 2012
**Original Filename:** interbrand method - brand valuation.pdf

---

## [Page 1] — Cover Page

**Title:** Brand Valuation: A Versatile Strategic Tool for Business
**Publisher:** Interbrand
**Tagline:** Creating and managing brand value™

This is a practitioner white paper published by Interbrand, the global brand consultancy, authored by Mike Rocha, Global Director of Brand Valuation at Interbrand.

---

## [Page 2] — Introduction: Why Brand Valuation Matters

### What Is Brand Valuation?

Brand valuation is the process of determining the total financial value of a brand — that is, the economic worth that a brand name brings to its owner, over and above the physical or operational assets of the business.

Brands are among the most valuable assets a company can possess. However, unlike tangible assets, brands are intangible and their value must be derived through systematic analysis. Brand valuation bridges the gap between marketing and financial management, enabling brands to be treated with the same rigour as other business assets.

### Why Does Brand Valuation Matter?

Interbrand argues that brand valuation is a versatile strategic tool. It is not merely a financial accounting exercise — it has direct implications for business strategy, budgeting, investment decisions, licensing, mergers and acquisitions, tax planning, and internal management.

Specifically, brand valuation matters because:

1. **Brands drive business performance.** Strong brands attract and retain customers, command price premiums, and deliver higher returns to shareholders.
2. **Brands are significant balance sheet items.** In many industries, the brand is the most valuable single asset on the balance sheet.
3. **Managers need accountability.** Brand valuation allows marketing spend to be justified in financial terms, demonstrating return on investment.
4. **Strategic decisions require brand insight.** Licensing, joint ventures, mergers, acquisitions, and legal disputes all require a credible brand value figure.

---

## [Page 3] — Uses of Brand Valuation

Interbrand identifies multiple practical uses for brand valuation:

### Strategic Brand Management
Brand valuation provides a rigorous basis for brand investment decisions. By understanding what drives brand value — and what might erode it — managers can allocate resources more effectively. Brand value tracking over time shows whether brand-building activities are paying off.

### Mergers and Acquisitions
When a company is acquired, a significant portion of the purchase price is frequently attributable to intangible assets, particularly the brand. Brand valuation enables buyers and sellers to negotiate from an informed position. It also informs post-acquisition integration decisions, including which brand names to retain, merge, or retire.

### Licensing and Franchising
Where a brand owner wishes to license its brand to a third party (e.g., for use in a new market, on a new product, or through a franchise arrangement), brand valuation provides the basis for setting a fair royalty rate. Royalty rates are typically expressed as a percentage of revenue; the brand valuation process can help establish what that percentage should be.

### Legal Disputes and Litigation
Brand valuation is used in disputes over brand ownership, infringement, counterfeiting, and contract breaches. Courts and arbitrators require credible, defensible figures for brand damages.

### Tax Planning and Transfer Pricing
Where brands are held in one legal entity and licensed to operating entities in other countries, the royalty payments must be set at arm's length (i.e., at market rates). Brand valuation underpins this transfer pricing analysis.

### Internal Management
Brand valuation can be used internally as a management tool — for example, to track brand health over time, to allocate budgets across a portfolio, or to set performance targets for brand managers.

### Investor Relations
Communicating brand value to investors and financial analysts helps explain the sources of competitive advantage and justifies the market capitalisation of the business.

---

## [Page 4] — The Interbrand Methodology: Overview

Interbrand's brand valuation methodology is built on three core analytical components:

1. **Financial Analysis** — to determine the overall economic value generated by the branded business
2. **Demand Analysis** — to determine what proportion of that value is specifically attributable to the brand (as opposed to other factors such as price, location, or patents)
3. **Competitive Analysis (Brand Strength)** — to assess the risk profile of the brand, which is used to derive the appropriate discount rate

These three components feed into a final brand value figure, defined as the **net present value of future brand earnings**.

Interbrand was the first company to have its methodology certified as compliant with the requirements of **ISO 10668** — the international standard for monetary brand valuation — and played a key role in the development of that standard.

---

## [Page 5] — The Interbrand Methodology: Steps 1–3 in Detail

### Step 1: Segmentation

The first step is to divide the branded business into distinct segments where the brand may play different roles and have different values. Segmentation is typically done by:
- Geography (e.g., domestic vs. international markets)
- Product/service category
- Customer type or distribution channel

Each segment is analysed separately, with individual brand values then aggregated to a total.

### Step 2: Financial Analysis — Economic Profit

Within each segment, Interbrand identifies the **economic profit** generated by the branded products or services. Economic profit is not simply accounting profit; it is profit in excess of the cost of capital employed. This is computed as:

**Economic Profit = NOPAT (Net Operating Profit After Tax) − Capital Charge**

Where the capital charge = Invested Capital × Weighted Average Cost of Capital (WACC)

This ensures that only returns above and beyond those required to sustain the business are attributed to the brand.

### Step 3: Demand Analysis — Role of Brand Index (RBI)

Not all of the economic profit is attributable to the brand. Other factors contribute to customer purchase decisions, including price, physical availability, product specifications, and patents. The **Role of Brand Index (RBI)** measures the proportion of the purchase decision that is attributable to the brand alone.

For example, in a category where the brand is highly influential (such as luxury goods or spirits), the RBI might be 80% or higher. In a category where purchase decisions are driven primarily by price or technical specifications (such as commodity chemicals), the RBI might be much lower.

**RBI determinations can be derived in three ways (listed in order of preference):**

1. **Primary research.** Specifically designed research, such as choice modelling (although other techniques are available), where RBI is statistically derived from actual consumer data.
2. **Existing research plus Interbrand opinion.** Existing research addressing the relative importance of purchase drivers is combined with Interbrand's opinion on the extent to which the brand influences perception of how the product or service will perform against each driver.
3. **Qualitative assessment.** Based on management discussions and past experience. This is used where no market research is available.

RBI findings are cross-checked against historical roles of brand for companies in the same industry.

**Brand Earnings** are then calculated as:

**Brand Earnings = RBI × Economic Profit**

---

## [Page 6] — The Interbrand Methodology: Steps 4–5 and the Brand Strength Framework

### Step 4: Competitive Analysis — Brand Strength Score (BSS)

Brand Strength measures the ability of the brand to create loyalty and, therefore, to keep generating demand and profit into the future. Brand Strength is scored on a **0–100 scale**, based on an evaluation across **ten key factors** that Interbrand believes make a strong brand.

Performance on these factors is judged relative to:
- Other brands in the industry
- Other world-class brands globally

The strength of the brand is **inversely related to the level of risk** associated with the brand's financial forecasts: a strong brand creates loyal customers and lowers risk; a weak brand increases risk. A proprietary formula is used to connect the Brand Strength Score to a brand-specific discount rate.

### Brand Risk (Discount Rate)

The brand-specific discount rate is used to discount brand earnings back to a present value, reflecting the likelihood that the brand will be able to withstand challenges and deliver the expected earnings into the future. This is equal to brand value.

### Step 5: Brand Value

**Brand Value = Net Present Value of Brand Earnings**

The brand earnings (from Step 3) are projected forward, and then discounted back to a present value using the brand-specific discount rate (derived from the Brand Strength Score in Step 4). The result is the brand's total monetary value.

---

### Figure 3: Interbrand's Brand Valuation Methodology (described)

The methodology is depicted as a flow diagram with five numbered steps:

- **Step 1 (Segmentation)** feeds down into three parallel tracks:
  - **Step 2 (Financial Analysis)** → produces Economic Profit
  - **Step 3 (Demand Analysis)** → produces Role of Brand Index (RBI)
  - **Step 4 (Competitive Analysis)** → produces Brand Strength Score (BSS)
- Steps 2 and 3 combine to produce **Brand Earnings**
- Step 4 produces **Brand Risk (Discount Rate)**
- Brand Earnings and Brand Risk (Discount Rate) together feed into **Step 5 (Brand Value)**, defined as the **Net Present Value of Brand Earnings**

*Note: Interbrand was the first company to have its methodology certified as compliant with ISO 10668 — requirements for monetary brand valuation — and also played a key role in the development of the standard itself.*

---

## [Page 7] — The Ten Factors of Brand Strength (Figure 4)

Brand Strength is assessed across ten factors, divided into **four Internal Factors** and **six External Factors**.

### Figure 4: Brand Strength Factors (described)

The figure presents two columns — Internal Factors (left) and External Factors (right) — each listing four and six factors respectively with full definitions.

#### Internal Factors

**1. Clarity**
Clarity internally about what the brand stands for and its values, positioning, and proposition. Clarity too about target audiences, customer insights, and drivers. Because so much hinges on this, it is vital that these are articulated and shared across the organisation.

**2. Commitment**
Internal commitment to brand, and a belief internally in the importance of brand. The extent to which the brand receives support in terms of time, influence, and investment.

**3. Protection**
How secure the brand is across a number of dimensions: legal protection, proprietary ingredients or design, scale or geographical spread.

**4. Responsiveness**
The ability to respond to market changes, challenges, and opportunities. The brand should have a sense of leadership internally, and a desire and ability to constantly evolve and renew itself.

#### External Factors

**5. Authenticity**
The brand is soundly based on an internal truth and capability. It has a defined heritage and a well-grounded value set. It can deliver against the (high) expectations that customers have of it.

**6. Relevance**
The fit with customer/consumer needs, desires, and decision criteria across all relevant demographics and geographies.

**7. Differentiation**
The degree to which customers/consumers perceive the brand to have a differentiated positioning distinctive from the competition.

**8. Consistency**
The degree to which a brand is experienced without fail across all touchpoints or formats.

**9. Presence**
The degree to which a brand feels omnipresent and is talked about positively by consumers, customers, and opinion formers in both traditional and social media.

**10. Understanding**
The brand is not only recognised by customers, but there is also an in-depth knowledge and understanding of its distinctive qualities and characteristics. (Where relevant, this will extend to consumer understanding of the company that owns the brand.)

---

**Focus on: Brand Strength**

Interbrand's experience shows that brands that are best placed to keep generating demand and profit into the future are those performing strongly (relative to competition) across this set of ten factors.

Four of these factors are **internally driven**, reflecting the fact that great brands start from within. The remaining **six factors are more visible externally**, acknowledging the fact that great brands change their world.

---

## [Page 8] — Author Profile and Closing

**Mike Rocha**
Global Director, Brand Valuation, Interbrand

The white paper concludes with the Interbrand branding and contact information.

**© 2012 Interbrand**
www.interbrand.com
Interbrand — Creating and managing brand value™

---

## Summary of Key Concepts

| Concept | Definition |
|---|---|
| Brand Value | Net present value of future brand earnings |
| Economic Profit | NOPAT minus capital charge (returns above cost of capital) |
| Role of Brand Index (RBI) | Proportion of purchase decision attributable to the brand |
| Brand Earnings | RBI × Economic Profit |
| Brand Strength Score (BSS) | 0–100 score across 10 factors; inversely related to discount rate |
| Brand Risk / Discount Rate | Rate used to discount brand earnings; derived from BSS |
| ISO 10668 | International standard for monetary brand valuation |

### The Ten Brand Strength Factors

| Factor | Type | Core Idea |
|---|---|---|
| Clarity | Internal | Clear values, positioning, proposition shared internally |
| Commitment | Internal | Internal belief and resource support for the brand |
| Protection | Internal | Legal, proprietary, geographic security of the brand |
| Responsiveness | Internal | Ability to evolve and respond to market changes |
| Authenticity | External | Brand grounded in internal truth and heritage |
| Relevance | External | Fit with customer needs across demographics/geographies |
| Differentiation | External | Perceived distinctiveness from competition |
| Consistency | External | Uniform brand experience across all touchpoints |
| Presence | External | Omnipresence and positive word-of-mouth |
| Understanding | External | Deep customer knowledge of brand's distinctive qualities |

## [SERVICES BRANDING CROSS-TAB]

# Services Branding Framework — Summary

**Title:** Services Branding Framework — Expanded
**Author(s):** Not explicitly credited (internal course material, implied Prof Y L R Moorthi)
**Publication:** Course material for Brand Management
**Year:** Not dated
**Original Filename:** services branding framework - summary.pdf

---

[Page 1]

## Services Branding Framework — Expanded

This document presents a comprehensive framework in the form of a large table that cross-references the **5 elements of service branding** (drawn from the Brand Identity Planning Model adapted for services) with the **7Ps of services marketing** integrated into those 5 elements, and then maps how each element behaves across three categories of goods/services: **Search Good**, **Experience Good**, and **Credence Good**.

### Framework Description

The table has 5 columns:
1. **5 elements of service branding** (left-most column, with merged cells spanning multiple rows)
2. **7Ps integrated into the 5 elements** (second column)
3. **Search Good** (third column)
4. **Experience Good** (fourth column)
5. **Credence Good** (fifth column)

The three good types are defined as follows:
- **Search Good**: Quality can be assessed before purchase (e.g., a hardware store — you can examine the product)
- **Experience Good**: Quality can only be assessed after or during consumption (e.g., a restaurant, a movie)
- **Credence Good**: Quality cannot be easily assessed even after consumption; requires trust in the provider (e.g., a doctor, a lawyer, a financial advisor)

---

### The Full Framework Table

**Part 1 — Brand as product element:**

| 5 Elements of Service Branding | 7Ps Integrated into the 5 Elements | Search Good | Experience Good | Credence Good |
|-------------------------------|-------------------------------------|-------------|----------------|---------------|
| **Brand as product** | Product | Never fails | Convenient, pleasant | Enriching, Clarifying |
| (continued) | Physical evidence | Physically demonstrate | Delight | Build confidence |
| (continued) | Price | Going rate | Value for money | Perceived value |
| (continued) | Place | Close to the customer | Reasonable distance | Distance not an issue |

---

[Page 2]

**Part 2 — Remaining elements:**

| 5 Elements of Service Branding | 7Ps Integrated into the 5 Elements | Search Good | Experience Good | Credence Good |
|-------------------------------|-------------------------------------|-------------|----------------|---------------|
| (Brand as product, continued) | Promotion | Provide information | Word-of-mouth | General word-of-mouth |
| **Brand as process** | Process | Standardized, Some customization | Standardized, More customization | Standardized, Lot more customization |
| **Brand as organization** | People | Basic skills | More evolved skill set | Complex skill set |
| **Brand as person** | (no separate P) | Problem solver | Entertainer | Advisor |
| **Brand as symbol (should reflect)** | (no separate P) | Useful | Lively | Wise |

### Interpretive Notes on the Framework

**Reading the framework across rows:**

- **Brand as product — Product**: For a search good, the product should "never fail" (reliability is the key since customers chose it based on pre-evaluated criteria). For an experience good, it should be "convenient, pleasant" (since quality is evaluated during use). For a credence good, it should be "enriching, clarifying" (since the customer cannot evaluate the quality — the service must educate and add value).

- **Brand as product — Physical evidence**: For search goods, physically demonstrate the product's attributes. For experience goods, create delight through physical environment. For credence goods, use physical evidence to build confidence (e.g., a doctor's diplomas on the wall, a lawyer's tidy office).

- **Brand as product — Price**: Search good = going rate (customer comparison shops). Experience good = value for money (assessed retrospectively). Credence good = perceived value (customer has no reference point; perception of value is constructed).

- **Brand as product — Place**: Search good = close to the customer (accessibility is a competitive factor). Experience good = reasonable distance (customer will travel if the experience is worth it). Credence good = distance not an issue (for complex credence services like brain surgery, patients travel globally).

- **Brand as product — Promotion**: Search good = provide information (factual). Experience good = word-of-mouth (peer endorsement builds confidence). Credence good = general word-of-mouth (broader reputation signals trustworthiness).

- **Brand as process**: Search good = standardized with some customization. Experience good = standardized with more customization. Credence good = standardized with lot more customization. The logic: the harder quality is to evaluate, the more the customer expects the process to be tailored to their specific situation.

- **Brand as organization — People**: Search good = basic skills. Experience good = more evolved skill set. Credence good = complex skill set. Credence goods require the highest expertise since the customer depends entirely on the provider's competence.

- **Brand as person**: Search good = problem solver (functional role). Experience good = entertainer (relational/hedonic role). Credence good = advisor (consultative, trusted role).

- **Brand as symbol (should reflect)**: Search good = useful (functional symbolism). Experience good = lively (expressive/energetic symbolism). Credence good = wise (authoritative, trustworthy symbolism).

### Summary Insight

The framework reveals that as we move from Search to Experience to Credence goods, the nature of service branding shifts from functional performance and information provision towards emotional engagement and trust-building. For credence goods especially, the Brand as Person (as advisor) and Brand as Symbol (as wise) dimensions become critical because the customer cannot rely on direct evaluation and must instead rely on reputation, relationships, and signals of expertise.


---
## [EGP GLOBALIZATION FRAMEWORK]
Source: knowledge_base/reading_materials/three_paths_to_globalization.md
---
# Three Paths to Globalization

**Title:** 3 Paths to Globalization
**Author(s):** Based on: Balaji Chakravarty and Howard Perlmulter, "Strategic Planning for a Global Business", Columbia Journal of World Business, Summer '85
**Publication:** Course summary/reference card for Brand Management; original source: Columbia Journal of World Business, Summer 1985
**Year:** Original article: 1985; this summary: not dated
**Original Filename:** egp - 3 paths to globalization.pdf

---

[Page 1]

## 3 Paths to Globalization

This document presents a single comparative table contrasting three distinct organizational orientations or paths that a multinational company can adopt for its global strategy. These are: **Ethnocentric (Blinkered)**, **Geocentric (Consultative)**, and **Polycentric (Autonomous)**.

*Based on: STRATEGIC PLANNING FOR A GLOBAL BUSINESS: BALAJI CHAKRAVARTY AND HOWARD PERLMULTER, COLUMBIA JOURNAL OF WORLD BUSINESS, SUMMER'85.*

### The Three Paths to Globalization — Comparative Framework

The framework compares the three orientations across six dimensions: Goal Setting, Strategy, Product Planning, Marketing Mix Decisions, Structure, and Culture.

| Item | Ethnocentric (Blinkered) | Geocentric (Consultative) | Polycentric (Autonomous) |
|------|--------------------------|--------------------------|--------------------------|
| **Goal Setting** | Top Down | Negotiated at all levels of the corporation | Bottom Up |
| **Strategy** | Home Centered | Global Integrative & NR (National Responsiveness) | National Responsiveness (NR) |
| **Product Planning** | Product development focus home country needs/customers | Global product with local variations | Local product based on local needs |
| **Marketing Mix Decisions** | Made at headquarters | Made jointly after consultations | Made in each country |
| **Structure** | Hierarchical | Network of organisations | Hierarchy with autonomy to national units |
| **Culture** | Home country | Global | Host country |

### Detailed Explanation of Each Path

**1. Ethnocentric (Blinkered)**

In the Ethnocentric orientation, the home country is the centre of the universe. Goal setting is top-down — headquarters decides and subsidiaries execute. Strategy is home-centred, meaning the company extends its domestic strategy to foreign markets without significant adaptation. Product development focuses on home country needs and customers; foreign markets receive the same products. Marketing mix decisions are made entirely at headquarters. The organizational structure is hierarchical, with authority concentrated at the centre. The prevailing culture is the home country culture, imposed on all operations worldwide.

*The term "blinkered" in the document title signals that this approach is characterised by a narrow, home-country-biased worldview that fails to account for diversity in global markets.*

**2. Geocentric (Consultative)**

In the Geocentric orientation, the company attempts to balance global integration with national responsiveness. Goal setting is negotiated at all levels of the corporation — neither purely top-down nor purely bottom-up. Strategy is simultaneously globally integrative AND nationally responsive (dual imperative). Product planning produces global products but with local variations accommodated. Marketing mix decisions are made jointly after consultations between headquarters and local units. The structure is a network of organisations (rather than a hierarchy), reflecting interdependence. The culture is explicitly Global — transcending any single home or host country.

*This is generally considered the most sophisticated and difficult-to-implement orientation, requiring genuine collaboration across geographies.*

**3. Polycentric (Autonomous)**

In the Polycentric orientation, local subsidiaries are largely autonomous and self-directing. Goal setting is bottom-up — local units set their own objectives. Strategy emphasises National Responsiveness above all; each market is treated as distinct. Product planning produces local products based entirely on local needs. Marketing mix decisions are made independently in each country. The organizational structure retains hierarchy but grants significant autonomy to national units. The prevailing culture is the host country culture in each market.

*The term "autonomous" captures the defining feature: each national unit operates largely independently. This can lead to duplication of effort and inability to leverage global scale, but ensures deep local relevance.*

### Key Takeaways for Brand Management

- Ethnocentric firms tend to have uniform global brands (with no local adaptation), which may fail in culturally distant markets.
- Polycentric firms may end up with fragmented brand identities — each market develops its own version of the brand, losing global coherence.
- Geocentric firms attempt to build global brands that are coherent at the core but locally adapted at the periphery — what is often called "glocal" branding (global core + local expression).
- The choice of orientation has fundamental implications for brand identity, brand communication, and brand management structure.


---
## [CUSTOMER VALUE FRAMEWORK]
Source: knowledge_base/reading_materials/fundamentals_of_customer_value.md
---
# Fundamentals of Customer Value

**Author:** Dr. Y. L. R. Moorthi, Prof (Marketing), Indian Institute of Management, Bangalore
**Publication:** IIM Bangalore (teaching note / working paper)
**Year:** circa 2016
**Original Filename:** Fundamentals of customer value.pdf

> Note: The PDF rendered as visual images. The content below is reconstructed from the complete visual reading of all 5 pages.

---

## Overview

This article introduces the fundamental concept of customer value as the foundation of marketing strategy. It explains what value means, how it is created and communicated, and how the concept of value is central to brand building and brand management.

---

[Page 1]

## 1.0 What is Customer Value?

Customer value is at the heart of marketing. A brand's success ultimately depends on its ability to deliver value to the customer. Value is not just about the price paid — it is the customer's perception of what they receive relative to what they give up.

Value can be expressed as:

> **Value = Benefits / Costs**

Where:
- **Benefits** include functional benefits (what the product does), emotional benefits (how it makes you feel), and self-expressive benefits (what it says about you)
- **Costs** include monetary cost (price), time cost, effort cost, and psychological cost

A brand creates value when the customer perceives that the benefits received outweigh the costs incurred.

---

[Page 2]

## 2.0 Types of Benefits

### 2.1 Functional Benefits
These are the tangible, verifiable utilities delivered by the product. They answer the question: "What does this product do for me?" Examples:
- A car that delivers 25 km per litre of fuel
- A detergent that removes stains in a single wash
- A bank that processes loans in 24 hours

Functional benefits appeal to the rational mind. They are the easiest to communicate and the easiest to copy.

### 2.2 Emotional Benefits
These are the feelings a product evokes in the consumer. They answer the question: "How does this make me feel?" Examples:
- A greeting card that evokes nostalgia
- An insurance policy that gives peace of mind
- A movie that makes you laugh

Emotional benefits are harder to copy because they depend on the accumulated experience, heritage, and communication history of the brand.

### 2.3 Self-Expressive Benefits
These are the benefits a product delivers by saying something about the person who uses it. They answer the question: "What does this say about me?" Examples:
- Wearing a Rolex signals success and taste
- Driving a Tesla signals environmental consciousness and technological forward-thinking
- Using Apple products signals creativity and design consciousness

Self-expressive benefits are tied to the social and psychological identity of the user.

---

[Page 3]

## 3.0 Types of Costs

### 3.1 Monetary Cost
The price paid for the product or service. This is the most obvious cost and the one most commonly used in marketing communications around value.

### 3.2 Time Cost
The time the customer must invest to acquire, use, or learn a product. A brand that reduces time cost creates value. For example:
- Quick service restaurants reduce time cost compared to fine dining
- Mobile banking reduces the time cost of conducting financial transactions

### 3.3 Effort Cost
The physical or cognitive effort required to use the product. Products that are intuitive and easy to use reduce effort cost. Apple's "ease of use" is a classic example of minimizing effort cost.

### 3.4 Psychological Cost
The mental discomfort, anxiety or risk associated with a purchase decision. A new, unknown brand imposes higher psychological cost than a trusted, familiar brand. This is why brand building is important — it reduces psychological cost for the buyer by signalling reliability and quality.

---

[Page 4]

## 4.0 Value Creation and the Marketing Mix

Value is created through all elements of the marketing mix:

- **Product**: functional benefits are embedded in the product design, features, and quality
- **Price**: price signals value (too low signals poor quality; too high signals poor value for money). Pricing must be consistent with the value proposition
- **Place (Distribution)**: reducing time and effort costs by making the product available where and when the customer wants it
- **Promotion (Communication)**: communicating the value proposition clearly and credibly. If the customer is not aware of the benefits, value cannot be realized

The key insight is that value is ultimately **perceived** by the customer. A product may have excellent functional benefits, but if the customer is unaware of them or does not believe in them, value is not created in the customer's mind.

### 4.1 The Role of the Brand in Value Creation
A brand is a repository of value. Strong brands:
- Reduce search costs for the customer
- Reduce psychological risk by acting as a quality signal
- Add emotional and self-expressive benefits on top of functional benefits
- Command price premiums because customers are willing to pay more for trusted brands

Brands thus allow firms to extract more value from their products than would be possible without branding.

---

[Page 5]

## 5.0 The Value Proposition

A value proposition is the complete statement of value a brand makes to its target customer. It answers the question: "Why should you choose this brand over all the alternatives?"

An effective value proposition:
- Is relevant to the target customer segment
- Is differentiated from competitors
- Is credible and deliverable
- Encompasses functional, emotional, and/or self-expressive benefits

### 5.1 Value Proposition and Brand Identity
The value proposition is the output of the brand identity planning process. After conducting strategic brand analysis (customer analysis, competitor analysis, self-analysis) and defining the brand identity (brand as product, organization, person, and symbol), the strategist arrives at the value proposition.

The value proposition has three components:
1. **Functional benefits**: the rational benefits delivered
2. **Emotional benefits**: the feelings evoked
3. **Self-expressive benefits**: the identity signals conveyed

Not every brand needs to deliver all three. A budget brand may primarily deliver functional and emotional benefits. A luxury brand may primarily deliver emotional and self-expressive benefits.

### 5.2 Customer Value and Competitive Advantage
A brand that delivers superior customer value achieves competitive advantage. Superior value means:
- More benefits than competitors at the same cost, or
- The same benefits at lower cost than competitors, or
- Unique benefits (that competitors cannot replicate) at a justifiable cost

The ultimate test of brand success is whether the brand creates genuine, sustainable value for its customers — value that keeps them coming back, makes them willing to pay a premium, and turns them into advocates.

---

*Teaching note prepared at Indian Institute of Management, Bangalore.*


---
## [BIPM WORKED EXAMPLE - AIRTEL VS IDEA]
Source: knowledge_base/reading_materials/airtel_vs_idea.md

# Airtel vs Idea: Brand Identity Planning Model

**Title:** Airtel vs Idea — Brand Identity Planning Model
**Author(s):** Prof Y L R Moorthi (case written by); draws from project report of Marlene Solerod, Vijayalakshmi, Naggappan R, Gaurav Chauhan, Gaurav Kumar, Gokulram K, Mary Infanta X (PGP 2014-16 batch, under guidance of Prof Y L R Moorthi)
**Publication:** Internal case material for class discussion
**Year:** 2017 (dated 27 Sep 2017 in filename); financial data as of 2014-15/2016
**Original Filename:** Airtel vs Idea - 27 Sep 2017.pdf

---

[Page 1]

## Airtel vs Idea
## Brand Identity Planning Model

*This case draws from the project report of Marlene Solerod, Vijayalakshmi, Naggappan R, Gaurav Chauhan, Gaurav Kumar, Gokulram K, Mary Infanta X all students of the PGP 2014-16 batch. The project was done under the guidance of Prof Y L R Moorthi. The project report and other published sources contributed to the present case written by Prof Y L R Moorthi specifically for purposes of class discussion.*

---

[Page 2]

## 1.0 Strategic Brand Analysis

### 1.1 Customer Analysis

We look at different parameters and rate Airtel and Idea on a scale of 1 to 10. This will help us know the impact of these parameters on Airtel and Idea.

| Parameter | Airtel | Idea | Reason |
|-----------|--------|------|--------|
| 3G | 8 | 6 | 3G Market share of Airtel in India is 3% in 2011 and is expected to grow to 18% in 2015. 3G Market share of Idea in India is 2% in 2011 and is expected to grow to 11% in 2015 |
| 4G | 9 | 6 | Airtel is the first Indian telecom to launch 4G services in 2012 and offer 4G services on mobile phones in 2014. Airtel has announced the launch of 4G services in 296 towns across India whereas Idea is expected to launch 4G in 10 circles by June 2016. |
| Geography | 8 | 6 | Airtel's distribution channel is spread across 1.5 Mn outlets in India covering approximately 86.7% of the country's population which is more than that of Idea |
| Customer churn | 9 | 7 | Airtel has the least customer churn among telecom companies. |
| Call drop complaints | 6 | 7 | Maximum call drops against Airtel (30 Mn) till July 2015. Call drops against Idea found to be 10 Mn till July 2015 |

### 1.2 Self/Competitor Analysis

#### 1.2.1 Airtel

##### Strengths/Weakness

**Strengths and opportunities**

- With a rich experience of 19 years in telecom industry, it has become world's 4th largest mobile operator with operations in almost 20 countries.
- It is one of the pioneer brands in the industry having a high brand recall and a whopping subscriber base of 200+ million customers.
- The Company has highly valued stakeholders, like Sony Ericsson, Nokia, Singtel and Apple. Such strategic alliances boost the brand equity and the bottom line of the company.
- The infrastructure of Airtel has extended across the country resulting in nationwide penetration.
- It holds the number 1 spot due to its excellent services in several countries.
- Vodafone, a leading multinational player, is Airtel's nearest competitor, in the Indian market. But it is seen as an external entrant. Airtel on the other hand is seen as a leading player hailing from India.

---

[Page 3]

**Weaknesses and threats**

- Airtel was the first company to lease infrastructure from telecom companies like Ericsson. Instead of buying telecom switches/exchanges Airtel leased them. Airtel also outsourced their entire IT infrastructure and reduced their capital expenditure considerably. It was hailed as a new business model in industry circles. Outsourcing operations however could be a double edged sword. It has helped Airtel lower its costs. But depending for infrastructure on other companies comes at a risk. It may negatively affect operations. However, so far, there has been no [adverse impact noted in original].

- With margins being low in the industry and acquisitions turning out to be bad investments, Airtel group is under debt (**Rs 66,367.20 crores as of March 2015**). Some of this hefty expense is on account of acquiring Government auctioned spectrum for telecom services. Spectrum auctions can run into legal disputes and cause disruption in business. For instance, the Supreme Court of India canceled 122 licenses awarded in the 2008 in 2G spectrum auction causing great distress to service providers.

- Vodafone group, the competitor is relatively more profitable, less exposed to debt and has better access to technology because it spends 140 million British pounds on research. Bharti spends negligible amounts, if any. Besides, the entry of new competitor Reliance Jio is likely to take the prices for telecom services further down from their present depressed levels. Bharti might find it therefore difficult to service their debt.

- Airtel acquired Zain's telecom operations business in 15 African countries at a price of 9 billion dollars. 4 years after the acquisition the business is still struggling to turn around.

Brokerage recommendations on Bharti (last 6 months upto March 2016, Source: moneycontrol.com):
- Buy: 3
- Hold: 3
- Sell: 1

#### 1.2.2 Idea

##### Strengths/Weaknesses

**Strengths and opportunities**

- IDEA has a good reputation due to its association with the Aditya Birla Group. Its customer base makes it the third biggest telecom operator in India.

---

[Page 4]

- Idea inherits a strong culture from the parent AVB group.
- IDEA won multiple HR awards nationally and internationally.
- Idea's product communication is creative and smart.
- It has a strong footprint in rural and semi-urban areas. Its total customer is 160 million customers.
- The parent AVB group is very good at spotting takeovers. It is optimally aggressive in its acquisitions. Over the years it acquired several telecom companies like Spice communications, Escorts telecom, Escotel mobile, RPG cellular.

**Weaknesses and threats**

- Less popular brand compared to Airtel and Vodafone because of smaller presence in metropolitan and urban areas.
- Lack of value added services like broadband, Direct to Home etc. though these will be attended to soon.
- Weaker presence in enterprise market.

Brokerage recommendations on Idea (last 6 months upto March 2016, Source: moneycontrol.com):
- Buy: 1
- Hold: 3
- Reduce: 1

A summary of financial results of the two companies is given in Appendix 4.

---

[Page 5]

## 2.0 Brand Identity

### 2.1 Brand as a product

#### 2.1.1 Product Scope

| Airtel | Idea |
|--------|------|
| When the product class is mentioned Airtel is the first (Appendix 1) in customer recall. | When the product class is mentioned Idea is probably the third (Appendix 1) in customer recall. |
| The product scope extends to other services like digital television, Broadband. But most strongly associated with only mobile services. | The brand name is basically mobile services and only now moving into other services. |

#### 2.1.2 Product Attributes

The benefits given by the two service providers are listed in the ads below.

---

Besides, IDEA uses a differentiating strategy to challenge incumbent players like Airtel, Vodafone etc. A few elements of the differentiating strategy are:

---

[Page 7]

- Affordable lower prices
- Famous personality for brand endorsement
- Innovative offers like "WOMEN'S CARD"

#### 2.1.3 Quality/Value

Appendixes 2 and 3 give the customer's view on quality and value respectively.

#### 2.1.4 Use Occasion

| Airtel | Idea |
|--------|------|
| Though it is a functional product used in all occasions it specifically associates itself with being connected with friends. With campaigns like "Har-Ek-Friend-Zaroori-Hota-Hai [1] and " Jo mera hai woh tera hai" [2] | Idea takes a problem solution route and finds innovative ways out of difficulties: i) "Why do I get overcharged, no idea" "Get Idea"; ii) Teaching remotely in village through a cell phone; iii) How to connect when you do not know the language (Malayali in Haryana – get language prompts on mobile through a friend) |

#### 2.1.5 Association with users

This is related to the perceived user of the brand.

| Airtel | Idea |
|--------|------|
| Airtel's target users are college goers and youngsters though it has users in all age groups. It is more urban. | Idea is perceived as a brand for the aam aadmi, the middle class in tier 2 cities as evidenced by their ad campaigns such as "No Ullu Banoing". The message is "get information from internet, do not allow anybody to make a fool of you". Or "Use internet to empower yourself", become, say a rated restaurant chef/chief (https://www.youtube.com/watch?v=-XAch4IfjCw; IIN Chef ad, Published on 9 Apr 2015) |

---

[Page 8]

#### 2.1.6 Link to country/region

Airtel is available in Africa too. Idea is confined to India.

### 2.2 Brand as an organization

Organizational attributes are defined as the distinctive features of an organization that uniquely differentiate it from its competitors. Organizational attributes are created by the people, culture, values and programs of a company. Analysis of these attributes for Airtel and Idea is given below.

#### 2.2.1 Airtel

**Vision, Mission and values:**

The vision of Airtel is to become the most loved brand in the country. It aims at enriching the lives of millions and winning lifetime customers by providing exceptional service. Airtel works towards its vision driven by its three primary values - **Alive, Inclusive and respectful** [3].

**Alive**: Airtel is alive to the needs of the customers. It is proactive in servicing them. It works with passion, energy and can-do attitude to help its customers realize their dreams. Innovation and entrepreneurial spirit are important driving forces of Airtel.

In 2003, Airtel signed Rahman as its brand ambassador and launched three advertisement jingles which were a raging hit. The advertisement theme was youth, energy and bonding. Besides, Airtel also roped in energetic and charismatic brand ambassadors like Shah Rukh khan and Kareena Kapoor to spearhead an advertisement campaign. The number of Airtel subscribers went to 100,000 after the launch [4].

Airtel offered innovative, new service offerings like Airtel Magic, Airtel caller tunes, Easy charge, Blackberry services, One-rupee mobile video services and Airtel money. Airtel was the first company to offer technologically superior services like 3G and 4G.

**Inclusive**: Airtel promotes diversity, provides service to all types of customers. It embraces change and is willing to anticipate, adapt and deliver changes that provide enriching solution to its customers.

Airtel has also decided to enter the rural market given increasing competition in urban India. This can be seen in their initiative of providing 'One Touch Internet' that started last year to help first time internet users learn through trial packs and tutorials.

---

[Page 9]

Airtel entered into strategic alliance with Nokia to bundle a handset with the connection. It also forged alliances with microfinance institutions to enable the rural customers to adopt its service.

**Respectful**: Airtel aims to act with humility, candour and honesty to achieve mutual respect of its customers.

Many ad campaigns of Airtel like "Dil jo chaahe, paas le aaya" [6] in 2010, "Har friend zaroori hai" in 2011, "Jo mera hai who tera hai" in 2012, Unlimited night calls for young couples in 2014 were used to provide an emotional colour to their campaign. This provided an abiding connect for Airtel among the youth.

#### 2.2.2 Idea

**Mission and values:**

Idea's mission is to delight customers by meeting their individual communication needs anytime, anywhere [5]. The primary values of Idea Cellular are derived from the values of the Aditya Birla Group. The major values are Integrity, commitment, passion, seamlessness and spirit. The five values are explained as follows:

- **Integrity** – Honesty in every action
- **Commitment** – Deliver on promise
- **Passion** – Energized action
- **Seamlessness** – Without boundaries in letter and spirit
- **Speed** – One step ahead always

Idea portrayed its values of passion and spirit in various advertisement campaigns. The theme of Idea as an enabler of the common man is reflected in advertisements like "No ullu banaoing", "An idea can change your life" (preventing caste war) [7]. Idea is committed to social issues like education and saving trees, that is promoted in it advertisements, for e.g., education for all [8] and saving paper [9].

**Summary — Brand as organization:**

| Attribute | Airtel | Rating | Idea | Rating |
|-----------|--------|--------|------|--------|
| Vision, Mission and values | Alive | 9 | Idea has not been able to promote all of its values through its operations so far. | 6 |
| Global/Local presence | Airtel is a MNC. It is present in 20 countries across Asia and Africa. | 9 | Idea is currently present in India alone. | 3 |

From the above table it can be inferred that as an organization Airtel is a stronger brand than Idea.

---

[Page 10]

### 2.3 Brand as a Person

**Airtel:**
Airtel brand when converted to a person, can be seen as a fun loving person, aged 18-26 years, from tier I and tier II cities. The brand is about teenagers, relationships in family, friends, love and breaking the barriers of communication. Therefore, brand endorsers like Sachin Tendulkar, Shahrukh Khan, A.R. Rahman and Kareena Kapoor are used as aspirational role models. Also, ads like the fastest 4G network communicate that Airtel users are at the frontier of technology. The symbolization is clearly that of a person in 20s who spans the space between tech savvy and evangelism. Marketing campaigns of Airtel *"Har ek friend zaroori hota hai"*, *"Express yourself"*, *"deewarein gir jaati hain, faasle mit jaate hain, jahan do baatein ho jati hain"*, *"Jo tera hai, wo mera hai"*, *"Together good things happen"* have been extremely successful in connecting with customers. Airtel also unveiled a new youthful and dynamic global identity in November 2010 with a new logo and an Airtel signature tune composed by AR Rahman. The ad *"Endless Goodbye"* was nominated as the best ad at Cannes Festival, 2010. According to Mohit Beotra, Head – Brand & Media in 2011, the brief given to the ad agency for 'Har Ek Friend Zaroori Hota Hai' was to "find an insight in the lives of young people to connect with them strongly".

**Idea:**
IDEA would be a 30-35 year old semi-urban male, a rural well-wisher with innovative ideas to solve problems related to education, poverty etc. Abhishek Bachan is the brand ambassador and the ads like 'What an Idea sirji', 'No Ullu Banaoing' are mostly focused on the semi-urban and rural audience. Similarly, advertisement campaigns like IIN, focus on helping people use connectivity to reach their goals and rediscover self-respect.

### 2.4 Brand as a symbol

Apart from functional benefits, a brand can use strong symbolism to connect with its customers. Its component dimensions are visual Imagery, metaphor and brand heritage.

#### 2.4.1 Visual imagery

Airtel's 'i' in the red logo emphasizes innovation. The capital 'A' was deliberately chosen to underline leadership position of the firm. This was recently changed to small 'a' to connect to the target young customer segment. Airtel signature tune composed by A R Rahman is the first thing that comes to mind when the brand is mentioned.

Idea's Distinctive yellow colour with the inverted 'i' differentiates it from the red of Airtel and Vodafone. The various marketing campaigns of IDEA *"An Idea can change*

---

[Page 11]

*your life"*, *"Honey Bunny"*, *"No Ullu Banaoing"*, *"What an Idea Sirji!"* have also successfully managed to create an impact.

| Airtel | Idea |
|--------|------|
| The new Airtel logo underlines **'a'** in the brand name Airtel [10]. The 'a' was chosen to express friendly connect between customers and Airtel [11]. | The exclamation mark (i) expresses the problem solving attitude of Idea. Idea positions itself as a "Thinker who tries to solve social problems" |
| Red color of Airtel is used to express its energy and passion | Yellow color keeps it distinctive from its competitors Airtel and Vodafone |

Idea is more distinctive and expressive when compared to Airtel. Airtel's symbol has been criticized for being similar to Vodafone in its communication and color choice [12].

#### 2.4.2 Metaphors

A brand metaphor can be a shortcut to convey brand's value.

| Airtel | Idea |
|--------|------|
| The 'a' in Airtel logo denotes the starting letter of Airtel. | The exclamation mark in Idea showcases the nature of the brand. "Use mobile, save paper" [9], "an Idea can change your life" [7] (preventing caste clash) are good examples that showcase Idea as a thinker who tackles tricky issues. |

### Discussion Questions

**Comment on the content of the brand identity planning model? How can it be improved? (Check for internal consistency of data. There is no need to look for data outside the case).**

**Compare the value propositions of the two brands?**

---

[Page 12]

### Appendix 1 — Survey of 30 respondents (Brand Recall)

| Brands | 1st Recall | 2nd Recall | 3rd Recall |
|--------|-----------|-----------|-----------|
| Airtel | 60% | 37% | 0% |
| Vodafone | 23% | 40% | 17% |
| Docomo | 0% | 0% | 13% |
| Idea | 0% | 0% | 20% |
| Reliance | 0% | 0% | 17% |
| BSNL | 17% | 10% | 20% |
| Aircel | 0% | 13% | 13% |

*Key finding: Airtel has overwhelming first recall (60%) while Idea has zero first and second recall in this survey of 30 respondents, appearing only in third recall at 20%.*

### Appendix 2 - Factor wise Telecom Ranking

This exhibit shows a colour-coded consumer satisfaction survey across four service dimensions — Network, Call Centre, Customer Service, and Internet — for six telecom operators: Airtel, Airtel (BSNL listed separately in some cells), BSNL, Idea, MTNL, Reliance, and Vodafone. Each dimension uses a 5-category scale: Extremely Happy (dark blue), Happy (medium blue), Neutral (light blue), Unhappy (orange), Extremely Unhappy (red).

Key observations noted in the document:
- i) Airtel was rated much better than Idea on Mouthshut.com
- ii) Idea gets poor ratings in Mouthshut.com on billing accuracy

### Appendix 3 — Value Comparison (Price Parity)

This exhibit shows a screenshot from www.komparify.com comparing pricing plans for Airtel and Idea. Both show Plan 199 (199.0 for 1 Month) as the "Best Plan for Difference", with:
- Monthly Pack Cost: Rs 199.00
- Estimated Monthly Usage Charges: —
- Service Tax: Rs 27.86
- **Estimated Monthly Cost: Rs 226.86** (identical for both)

*Source: www.komparify.com*

*Implication: The pricing of both Airtel and Idea on equivalent plans is identical at Rs 226.86/month, suggesting price parity between the two brands at comparable plan levels.*

---

[Page 13]

### Appendix 4 — Consolidated financials – 2014-15

| Item | Bharti (Airtel) | Idea |
|------|----------------|------|
| Total revenue | 94,613.90 crores | 32,040.56 crore |
| Net profit | 5.76% | 10.11% |
| Return on Equity | 8.56% | 13.86% |
| Return on capital employed | 4.01% | 7.41% |
| Return on Assets | 2.71 | 5.28 |
| Debt/equity | 1.07 | 0.73 |
| Interest coverage ratio | 2.58 | 5.72 |
| Enterprise value | 226,607.29 crore | 81,367.14 crore |
| EV/EBITA | 6.58 | 7.21 |
| EPS | 13.00 | 9.00 |

*Key financial observations: While Bharti (Airtel) is much larger by revenue (94,614 crores vs 32,041 crores) and enterprise value (226,607 crores vs 81,367 crores), Idea is more profitable — higher net profit margin (10.11% vs 5.76%), higher ROE (13.86% vs 8.56%), higher ROCE (7.41% vs 4.01%), lower debt/equity ratio (0.73 vs 1.07) and much higher interest coverage ratio (5.72 vs 2.58). Idea's superior profitability ratios despite smaller size reflect more efficient capital deployment.*
